(The Lord Mayor of Belfast) in the Chair.

Mr. DEPUTY-SPEAKER: Before
calling upon Mr. Robb, I am sure we all
regret the absence of Mr. Speaker. I
had a letter from him a day or two ago
saying that he is keeping better and
hopes to be back in his place in a very
short time.
Hon. MEMBERS: Hear, hear.
Unemployment Assistance (Determination of Need and Assessment of
Needs) (Amendment) Regulations
(Northern Ireland), 1939.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That the Regulations
made by the Ministry of Labour on the
18th day of December, 1939, under
Sections 33 and 46 of the Unemployment
Assistance Act (Northern Ireland), 1934,
authorising increases in the assessments
of the needs of applicants for unemployment assistance be hereby approved."
I should, perhaps, explain that in
December last the Unemployment Assistance Board for Northern Ireland
decided, following the example of the
Board in Great Britain, that the cost of
living had increased to such an extent
that the existing rate of unemployment
assistance required revision. Between the
1st September and the 1st December last
the cost of living increased by 11 per
cent., and the Board recommended that
an increase should be made in the rates
of unemployment assistance. They prepared draft regulations which were
approved by the Ministry, and they were
brought into force on the 18th December
last. These regulations continue in
operation only for one year unless both
Houses of Parliament approve of them.
It is that approval I am seeking now.
Briefly the changes are the same as the
changes made in Great Britain. They
provide for an increase in the rates of 2s
for a man and his wife, 1s in the case
of a member of the household of 16 or
over, and 6d in the case of a child under
16. For a person living alone, including
a boarder or lodger, the increase is 1s 6d
a week. Under previously existing
regulations special provision was made
for an increase in allowances to a householder with dependants and no available.
resources, and care is taken in these
regulations to insure that that increased
allowance is not set off against the
increase now provided. Again, where
under the existing regulations the
advisory committee provided for a concession in the applicant's favour regarding rent the amending regulations preserve this provision in the applicant's
interest by providing that the amount
which may be paid in rent shall be calculated on the basis of the old scale.
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Committee
22 FEBRUARY 1940
Certain increased allowances paid under
winter-time adjustment regulation will
not be affected by the new regulations.
These are the main provisions of the
regulations, and as I have said, they must
be approved by both Houses within
twelve months of their making. It is
within twelve months now, and I ask the
House to approve.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Hire-Purchase Bill (S.).
Order for the House to be put into
Committee read.
Resolved, "That this House do now
resolve itself into Committee on the said
Bill."-(Mr. Robb.)
In Committee accordingly.
[The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST
in the Chair].
CLAUSE 1 (Application of Act).
This Act shall apply in relation to
all hire-purchase agreements and creditsale agreements under which the hirepurchase price or total purchase price,
as the case may be, does not exceed—
(a) where the agreement relates to a
motor-car or railway wagon or
other railway rolling stock, the sum
of fifty pounds,
(b) where the agreement relates to
livestock, the sum of five hundred
pounds, and
(c) in any other case, the sum of one
hundred pounds,
and in this Act the expressions "hirepurchase agreement" and "credit-sale
agreement" shall be construed accordingly.
Major DOBBS: I beg to move in Clause
1 to leave out paragraphs (a), (b) and (c)
and insert the words "two hundred
pounds." I am quite certain that every
Member of this House is entirely in agreement that something must be done with
regard to hire purchase. I am entirely
in agreement with the Members of the
House, but I do feel that although this
is a crying necessity I am floundering in
the dark. In moving this amendment I
would like to point out that I, and I am
certain every Member of this House,
would like a considerable amount of information on this Bill. If I may take
the last first, for the sting is in the tail,
paragraph (c) presumably deals with the
hire-purchase and also the credit sale,
though it is hire purchase we really have
in our minds of furniture, sewing
machines and so on. The hon. and gallant
Senator, Major Boyle, on the Second
Reading made some very good points upon
that paragraph as to why £200 should be
substituted for £100. I do not think
there is any necessity for me to go over
those points.
There
I would like to come then to paragraph
(b). I cannot find that this practice of
hire purchase with regard to livestock is
at all prevalent in these counties.
may be a certain amount of it. I have
come across one or two cases, but certainly
nothing of such importance that this £500
with regard to livestock should be
embodied in the Bill when dealing with
smaller sums with regard to house furniture and so on. It seems to me to be
quite unnecessary. To come to paragraph (a), dealing with motor-cars, railway wagons and so on, of course it is
common knowledge, and has been for a
very long time, that an enormous trade
has been done in motor-cars by hire
purchase. It seems to me rather amazing
that a new motor-car can be obtained for
£50. I thought our friends in the United
States of America were doing all they
could to get it under £100. That a new
motor-car can be bought for £50 is
astonishing. I think the limit of £50 is
quite inadequate. £200, I think, it should
be. Of course if you are dealing with
second-hand motor-cars £50 seems to be
a very extravagant limit. A great many
second-hand motor-cars can be bought for
£5. I said I was floundering in the dark.
On this paragraph (a) I really stick in
the dark. £50 is a very small amount
for a railway wagon, and surely people
who purchase railway wagons have got
sufficient business ability to see that in
any agreement they sign there should be
no pitfall.
We come to other railway rolling-stock.
What exactly does that mean? Does it
mean locomotives? I think the locomotive
that can be bought for £50 beggars description. I cannot see any sense in
having these three separate amounts of
£50, £500 and £100. If you make it
£200 you have got some sense and it

THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
should meet a great many cases. I beg to
move.
Mr. ROBB: This Bill, as I told the
House on the occasion of the Second
Reading, is in essence a copy of the
English Bill. It is quite true that the
circumstances here are different from
those in England, but the corresponding
Bill in England became an Act only
through the operation of a compromise.
It was introduced as a Private Member's
Bill and was sent to a Select Committee.
In that Select Committee were those
who represented the owners of goods
that were the subject of hire purchase,
and those representing the
hirers.
public who might be
The
matter was vigorously fought out in
that committee and the result was
compromise between the two views. It
was freely stated at Westminster that
had there not been this compromise the
Bill would not in fact have been passed.
general
a
The Government of Northern Ireland
announced their intention of passing a
Bill upon the same lines as the English
Bill, and we have not had any organised
opposition to this Bill in Northern
Ireland, which we might have had had
we announced our intention of bringing
in a Bill which would differ from the
English Measure. Accordingly, although
I am sure every Member of the Committee appreciates the validity of much
that the hon, and gallant Senator has
said, on behalf of the Government, I
cannot accept his Amendment, and for
two reasons. In the first place, the Bill
which we announced we intended to
bring in was to be a Bill upon the same
lines as the English Bill, and that disarmed opposition.
In the second place, I would like to
emphasise the great advantage it is to
us in a matter of this sort to have the
law the same in the two countries. In
a commercial matter such as this it is
difficult when the law in the two!
countries is not the same, because a great
many of the transactions in hirepurchase that are put through in our
area are in fact transactions by companies established in Great Britain, or
companies financing from Great Britain
transactions in Northern Ireland. Their
forms are printed in compliance with the
English Act, and will be in use here.
With regard to the details about
which Senator Dobbs has asked for information, I do not think the hirepurchase of railway wagons exists in
this area, although they have it in
England.
Major DOBBS: For £50?
Mr. ROBB: The sum of £50 was fixed
in England because, I am informed,
there is a trade in England on hirepurchase lines for second-hand railway
coal trucks, the price of which does not
exceed £50. We do not have such a
trade here, and we have no experience
of it. With regard to livestock, I have
been informed since the Second Reading
debate that there was some small trade
on these lines in regard to livestock, but
it hardly exists. With regard to the
£100 limit on ordinary furniture, that
was a compromise. The view taken was
this: that, after all, people who could
pay £200 for furniture were perhaps
able to look after themselves and did
not need so much protection as those
in a poorer way. But the main points
on which the Government rely are,
firstly, that we announced our intention
of introducing here a Bill on the same
lines as the English Act; secondly, that
this Act was in fact a compromise between two views; and, thirdly. that it
is a great advantage to us to have our
law the same as the law in Great
Britain. For these reasons I cannot
accept the Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: I suppose that when
you come to the Question as regards this
Amendment you will limit yourself to
omitting up to the words, "sum of,"
in line 14 so as to save the subsequent
Amendment standing in the name of the
hon. and gallant Senator opposite
(Major Boyle). The reason I mention
that is that there are really two
questions involved in the Amendment
before us. The first is the omission of
the three paragraphs and the substitution of £200, and secondly, no omission at all, but merely changing £100 to
£200, which is the effect of the Amendment in the name of the hon. and
gallant Senator opposite.
As regards the other question, the
right hon. Gentleman, the Leader of the
House, speaking for the Government,
referred us to the discussions in England.
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Committee
22 FEBRUARY 1940
I am afraid I cannot altogether agree
that we are bound in Northern Ireland
by discussions that took place in England.
It is all very well for the two interests in
England to consult together, but we have
no evidence that the two interests in Northern Ireland would take the same view
if they had been consulted.
As regards railway wagons, I am prepared to believe that that question does
not arise in Northern Ireland, and therefore whether we put on a limitation or
not is of no consequence. As regards the
motor car, we ought to be assured that
the people who sell motor cars at a price
not exceeding £50, and the people who
may buy them at that price have been
consulted and have arrived at this view.
I confess I do not myself see why a motor
car of that class should be selected for
special treatment. With regard to livestock, that is a difficulty to which I am
calling attention on a later Clause. The
difficulty from which we cannot get away,
much as
we would like to, is that we
cannot say that livestock are not goods.
The definition of goods in the Interpretation Clause is fatal to that. Livestock
are goods within the meaning of this Bill,
but one cannot help thinking the Bill
would be very much simpler if the promoters
had confined themselves to furniture and
household requisites. It would have
saved us a certain amount of desultory
conversation. I do not see how you can
put livestock under £500 in a separate
category from the other things.
But the most important point to which
I wished to draw attention was to suggest
that the Amendment to be put should
stop at the words "sum of" in line 14.
On the whole I am rather in favour of
the Amendment as making in the direction of simplification and not doing any
great violence to the desirability of conforming to what they have passed across
the water.
Major DOBBS: I must thank the
Leader of the House for the small light
he has thrown on this matter of hirepurchase arrangements being financed
from Great Britain. That, I know, is
perfectly correct in a great number of
cases, but I rather imagine that that has
rather to do with motor cars. I do not
think the finance companies in England
care much about financing the hire
purchase of cars which cost less than £50.
I do not suggest that anybody should
finance the purchase of motor cars costing
£1,000, but up to £200 it is very necessary. I wipe out entirely railway
wagons and rolling stock because it is
apparently not the practice to purchase
them under this system in Northern Ireland, but it is rather strange to see these
things included in the Bill if they are of
no importance in this connection. Under
the circumstances I ask leave to withdraw
the Amendment.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: In
my opinion this Bill has been drafted to
protect the poorer class of the community.
There is a great deal of trade done in
buying second-hand motor cars under the
hire-purchase system, and £50 is a
reasonable amount for a second-hand car.
With regard to railway wagons and
rolling stock, it is quite possible that a
small contractor requiring to excavate
soil or other material might buy under
the hire-purchase system a number of tip
wagons and portable lines on which to
run them. That would be provided for
under this Clause. I think the Clause as
it is drafted is quite reasonable and
proper, and that the whole Bill has been
designed to help and to protect poorer
people who can speculate only in comparatively small sums.
Mr. DEPUTY-SPEAKER: In view
of this Amendment, does the hon. and
gallant Member (Major Boyle) wish to
proceed with his Amendment?
Major BOYLE: Yes, I would like to
go on with

Major BOYLE: I beg to move in
Clause 1, paragraph (c), to leave out the
word "one" ("one hundred pounds")
and insert two."
I do not think I need say much with
reference to this Amendment, which is
simply to increase the sum of £100 to
£200. It will not in any way affect the
Act of Parliament passed in England.
That was a private Act.
Since it was
passed in England some months have
passed, and the purchasing power of £100
is very considerably reduced, as I am sure
a great many of us are aware. By
increasing the amount to £200 I feel
that a great many of what we may call
the poorer people will be brought in and
receive benefit. Otherwise they will be
excluded. When this war is over, if ever
it will be, we do not know what the purchasing power of £100 will be. I had
hoped from what the Leader of the House
told us that the Government would accept
this Amendment. This step-by-step
policy is splendid, and I agree with it in
every instance. In this particular
instance by making this change I think
we would be doing what is right. I hope
the Committee will agree with my
Amendment. I need not say anything
further.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: In view of
what the Leader of the House has stated
here I am sorry I cannot support the
Amendment proposed by Major Boyle,
although I am certainly in sympathy
with it. There is nothing in the Bill to
prevent a hirer making two or three or
more agreements in respect to the purchase of household furniture. I think
to meet the people the hon. and gallant
Senator has in mind it is not necessary
to alter the Clause. They can enter into
several agreements for the purchase of
goods, household furniture or anything
like that. They are confined to the sum
of £100 in only one transaction.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM :
There is another view. After all this
hire-purchase system has been a great
benefit to a number of the poorer people.
It has enabled many families to furnish
their homes comfortably and well by
making small weekly or monthly payments. As it is, £100 worth of furniture
for a working man's home is fairly
reasonable. Supposing you made it
£200 would it not be an encouragement
to some people to furnish their homes
extravagantly. They would be increasing the amount, and it would be difficult
for them to pay the instalments. There
is that doubt about it. I really think
you would be doing best to leave the
Clause as it is and not place a temptation
in the way of poor people to purchase
furniture, mangles, sewing machines and
other things in an extravagant way.
One hundred pounds is a fairly decent
sum for these people, and is sufficient
to be hung as a burden round their necks
while the weekly or monthly payments are
made.

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am
was not here at the start.
I agree with
the hon. and gallant Member (Major
Boyle) in this amendment. I strongly
agree with him in increasing the figure of
£100 to £200. All we have to consider
is this: that once the hirer goes over the
sum of £100 this Bill is not going to save
him. He is outside the scope of the Bill.
It encourages the seller of the article to
get over the £100 and so evade the clauses
of the Bill. Let us take the ordinary
common or garden dwelling-house of today. We have a small dining-room,
drawing-room and two bedrooms. In
some you have three bedrooms. I would
like to ask who could furnish a respectable
dining-room that you would invite your
friends into for much less than £50? Let
us ask ourselves what a dining-room suite
would cost. If you could buy any decent
suite below £50 I would be glad to hear
it. The dining-room is only the start of
the furnishing. Take the drawing-room.
I venture to say the same sum would be
required to furnish it. As to the bedrooms we must ask ourselves how they
could be furnished under £50. You will
find when you go into the whole question
of furnishing kitchen, dining-room, drawing-room and two bedrooms that £100
will not do it. It has been suggested
that if you want to evade the Act you
could have two or three agreements. That
is what it amounts to. If you go and
purchase furniture and agree to pay £99
on one agreement there is nothing to
prevent you entering into another agreement for another amount. If the seller
of the furniture wishes to evade the Act
he will encourage the purchase to go over
the sum of £100 and the Act does not
cover it. I would point out to the Committee that these considerations are worth

Mr. LESLIE: I did intend to get up

The second remark that I was going

getting at and we are going to put an

CLAUSE 2 (Requirements relating to
hire-purchase agreements).
(1) Before any hire-purchase agreement is entered into in respect of any
goods the owner shall state in writing
to the prospective hirer, otherwise than
in the note or memorandum of the
agreement, a price at which the goods
may be purchased by him for cash (in
this section referred to as the "cash
price"):
Provided that this sub-section shall
be deemed to have been sufficiently
complied with-
(a) if the hirer has inspected the goods
or like goods and at the time of
his inspection tickets or labels were
attached to or displayed with the
goods clearly stating the cash
price, either of the goods as a whole
or of all the different articles or
sets of articles comprised therein,
or
(b) if the hirer has selected the goods
by reference to a catalogue, price
list, or advertisement, which clearly
stated the cash price either of the
goods as a whole or of all the
different articles or sets of articles
comprised therein.
*
*
*
*
*
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
2, sub-clause (1), par. (a), after "goods"
("or like goods") to insert "exhibited
to him by, or on behalf of, the owner."
Sir Joseph Davison
Rt. Hon. J. H. Robb
Mr. H. Taylor
What I imagine the paragraph of the
proviso was originally getting at or referring to was the perfectly normal and
ordinary practice when a man goes into
a
or
salesman's shop-furniture, I am
afraid is still the principal matter-and
sees something, say a wardrobe, ticketed
at £2 or whatever it may be, and chooses
it there and then. Delivery is subsequently made from the warehouse or
from the factory. By the insertion of
the words "or like goods" one is brought
at once against a difficulty as to whether
the word "like" means similar
identical, and as to that I am not going
to express an opinion. What I do think
we want to do, and this is the object
of this Amendment, is to bring the man
who actually does the deal, the owner we
call him here, into direct relationship
with these "like" goods. Therefore, I suggest that we should make the proviso
applicable only to the owner who has
shown either personally or by deputy the
like goods to the person who proposes to
hire them. I think that is sound, and,
therefore, I beg to move.
Mr. ROBB: The Government will
accept this and the succeeding Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
2 of sub-clause (1), par. (b), after
"advertisement" to insert "issued by the
owner or made use of by him in connection with the hiring.'

CLAUSE 3 (Requirements relating to
credit-sale agreements).
(1) Before making any credit-sale
agreement under which the total purchase price exceeds five pounds, the
seller shall state in writing to the prospective buyer, otherwise than in the
note or memorandum of the agreement,
a price at which the goods may be purchased by him for cash (in this section
referred to as the "cash price"):
Provided that this sub-section shall
be deemed to have been sufficiently
complied with-
(a) if the buyer has inspected the goods
or like goods and at the time of
his inspection tickets or labels were
attached to or displayed with the
goods clearly stating the cash price,
either of the goods as a whole or
of all the different articles or sets
of articles comprised therein, or
(b) if the buyer has selected the goods.
by reference to a catalogue, price
list, or advertisement which clearly
stated the cash price either of the
goods as a whole or of all the
different articles or sets of articles
comprised therein.
(2) A person who has sold goods by
a credit-sale agreement under which the

Address to His Grace the Governor.
Mr. ROBB: I have to report to the
House that a Privy Councillor of Northern
Ireland has waited on his Grace with the
Address from the Senate (pursuant to the
Order of the 19th December, 1939), to
which his Grace has been pleased to
return the following Answer:
I have received with great satisfaction the expression of your thanks
for the Speech with which I opened the
present Session of Parliament.
Questions to Minister.
ADVANTAGES OF MOLE
DRAINING.
Major DOBBS: If land drainage is
mentioned to any Member of this House
I imagine he immediately thinks about
arterial drainage, that is, drainage on
a very large scale. But if you talk about
it to a farmer he immediately thinks
of field drainage, that is, drainage of his
own fields. Now, the ordinary field
drainage, as usually carried out, is a
laborious and an expensive process. You
have got to dig a trench 2ft. 6ins. or so
deep, put at the bottom of the trench
short tile pipes and stones with sods
upside down, and then you fill it in. It
is, as I say, expensive, and it takes a
long time to do.
But there is another form of drainage,
mole drainage, that is comparatively
cheap and comparatively quick to carry
out. Mole drainage is carried out by a
machine that looks very much like the
body or framework of a plough sitting
on the ground. From the centre of that
framework is a coulter carried down into
the earth, a sort of knife-blade. At the
bottom end of the knife-blade is a cigarshaped small bar, which is the mole. It
is put into operation by towing that
frame over the surface of the ground.
The coulter or knife-edge cuts through
the grass and ground underneath it, and
the small, cigar-shaped iron bar is forced
to travel at whatever distance it is set
below the surface, some 18 inches or so.
It is forced to travel according as the
framework is taken over the ground, and
leaves behind it a small, cylindrical hole
which is the drain. If you want to drain
a portion of a field, if there is a convenient ditch and the slope of the field
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
29 FEBRUARY 1940
goes into it, or if there is an old drain
you follow it. Otherwise you must make
an open trench and drop your mole into
it and pull it up the field. You do that
several times, and when that is done you
have that piece of field thoroughly
drained by a considerable number of
small subsidiary drains five yards or more
apart, exactly as the nature of the land
requires, all draining down into that one
drain and from there the water is carried
away.
I am quite certain, incidentally, that
-certainly in County Antrim-a lot of
our old drains made 50 or 60 years ago
were made a great deal too deep. The
ground where they are could be greatly
improved by the use of the mole drain,
making use of these old drains to carry
away the water. May I give an illustration of the efficiency of mole draining.
I think it is an illustration that will
appeal to the right hon. Gentleman the
Leader of the House, because from certain
illustrations he has given I gather he is
an ardent golfer. Some years ago I was
taken over a golf course in the midlands
of England. I said to my friend of one
or two fairways, "These could be greatly
improved if you used mole drainage.'
Rather more than two years afterwards I
was back there and my friend showed me
with great pride what his committee had
done to improve the fairways. They improved them by mole drainage.
,,
Some twenty years ago I tried mole
drainage myself and from what I did I
came to the conclusion that for the
ordinary farmer mole drainage is really
not possible, at any rate for the ordinary
farmer in the North of Ireland. I came
to the definite conclusion that the
ordinary farm tractor-I used a small
farm tractor to pull the machine-of 20
or
30 horse-power is not sufficiently
powerful for our very heavy, strong land.
In consequence I would like to put it to
the Government, who are definitely trying to do their very best with regard to
the improvement of our fields, that they
should have some outfits, a very powerful
tractor and a small mole draining machine
and a team of one or two men-one at
any rate who should be very knowledgeable in drainage, and put these outfits
throughout our counties. I am sure if
there were one or two demonstrations
under the auspices of the Ministry of
Agriculture you would then almost
immediately find that the farmers in
Northern Ireland would be very keen
indeed in using these outfits to mole
drain their land and the result would be
that all over this country our meadows
and farms would be greatly improved
because drainage would be cheap and
quick.
I beg to ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if any steps are
being taken by the Government to make
the advantages of mole draining available
to farmers in Northern Ireland.
Mr. ROBB: I have been supplied with
the following reply.
The answer is in the negative. There
is only a very small area of the land in
Northern Ireland, which, by reason of
its sub-soil, is suitable for improvement
by mole draining machinery.
no
The Ministry of Agriculture is satisfied, after demonstrations at various
centres in the past, that useful
purpose would be served by directing
special attention to this method of land
improvement.
SALVAGE AND COLLECTION OF
WASTE.
Major BOYLE: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can
state what steps the Government have
taken or are proposing to take in connection with the salvage and collection of
waste. I am asking this question in the
hope that assistance and guidance will be
given to local authorities to enable them
to go in for the collection of waste of all
sorts, so that it may be available for industry again as raw material. An urban
authority in December last made an
order for the collection of waste paper,
but this was not carried out, and this
month the council stated that the
collection would not pay, and so would
entail a charge on the rates, the order
was cancelled.
In England the Ministry of Supply
has a salvage department whose motto
is, "It is waste to waste". This department appeals for waste of all descriptions to be collected and made available
again as raw material for industry.
Every ton collected saves carriage at sea,
voyages of our ships, the use of foreign
exchange, and, most important of all,
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister

THE SENATE
[Major Boyle]
the lives of our merchant seamen and
men of the Royal Navy. The largest
organisations for the collection of waste
material are the salvage and cleansing
departments of the local authorities.
The materials which are most useful are
waste paper, bones, scrap metal, rags,
and kitchen waste for pig and poultry
feeding. Scrap metal is at present
actually being brought back from France,
and many of us hope that many guns
captured in the last war and now
scattered through the country may be
collected, melted down, and returned to
Germany in such shape or form as our
military authorities may consider most
suitable.
Before the war we imported thousands
of tons of wood pulp from Scandinavia
and Finland for the manufacture of paper.
The present rate of collection of
waste paper in England is a thousand
tons a week. Should not we help?
Our housewives and organisations, Boy
Scouts, etc., with their decentralised
schemes are anxious to assist and
co-operate. But a collection and disposal
organisation is required. I think that
after our Prime Minister's inspiring and
heartening broadcast the other night we
cannot but feel that Ulster wants to help
in this field of war as in the others. I
would like to read a short letter that
appeared in The Times from a working
man. It said: -
After months of unemployment I
have now got a job at an incinerator,
and it grieves me to burn a wagon
load of paper and miles of string every
day. I have two pals with other firms
in the same position. Believe me, sir,
to be a true and faithful British
subject, but I mention one good point
re that swine Hitler-he is no waster.
I hope the Government will be able to
give a satisfactory answer to this
question.
Major DOBBS: Before the Leader of
the House answers the question I would
like to know if his attention has been
drawn to an advertisement which has
been appearing in The Times newspaper
almost continuously for some considerable time. It takes the form of rough
notes made by a business man who puts

there is always something like this:
"That reminds me, I must see Jimmy
about all our scrap. The country needs
it now." That is put forward by the
Iron and Steel Control, Steel House,
Tothill Street, London, S.W.1. Is there
anything answering to that Iron and
Steel Control in Belfast? It seems to me
that, quite apart from the business
houses in Belfast where there must be a
lot of iron and steel scrap, there is also
a great deal of it amongst the farms of
the country, and I should like to know
if there is anyone people could get in
touch with to get this scrap taken away.
Dr. GIBSON: I would like to know
if the Leader of the House observed a
letter in the Belfast News-Letter last
week from a lady at Bushmills saying
that they had been asked to collect waste
paper, that they had collected paper all
over the neighbourhood, and that it had
become such a nusiance to them by its
bulk that they had had to burn it. She
wanted to know if it would not be possible
for the Government to devise some means
of collecting this paper.
Mr. GLENDINNING: The Portrush
Urban District Council asked people to
collect paper and they asked the Boy
Scouts and Girl Guides to call for it. In
my humble establishment we have been
collecting paper for the past three or
four weeks, but there it still lies. It is
really necessary that this waste should
be saved, and I strongly support the
suggestion contained in the question.
Mr. BARCLAY: I was wondering if
it would be possible to create a centre to
which all this waste material could be
brought. In the city of Belfast we have
many poor people who have ponies and
carts and donkeys and carts. If householders were asked to save waste paper
these people could go about the country
and collect it and they could be paid for
doing this work. As it is, too much is
expected for nothing, and the result is
that paper is burned.
Just before Christmas, Boy Scouts in
the district in which I live distributed
circulars asking householders to save all
their paper.
We did so, but the paper
is still there. Some time ago I met a
Boy Scout and I said to him, "When
are you going to collect this paper?" He
replied, "We cannot get a cart.
"" In
my house there is a pile of paper, but
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
29 FEBRUARY 1940
the boys do not come for it. If the
collection of this paper was put in the
hands of hawkers who were paid so much
per hundredweight for it you would get
plenty of paper.
Mr. ROBB: That is exactly the point.
What is the price that is being offered
for this scrap? I am not very familiar
with the present price, but before the
war broke out a ton of this waste paper
could be had for less than 7s, delivered
at the mill. If that is all the value
of waste paper you will realise the
difficulty of providing for its collection
and transport. However, the matter
has not been lost sight of.
Soon after the outbreak of war the
Ministry of Commerce made contact
with the controllers of the various raw
materials in the Ministry of Supply with
a view to ensuring that Northern Ireland's resources were fully utilised in the
national interest. Amongst the matters
discussed was the salvage of household
waste. It will be apparent to the House
that the initial difficulty lies in the
segregation of the waste into its various
categories. The first essential is, of
course, to ensure that householders
should carry out a preliminary segregation by separating kitchen refuse,
waste paper, metal, bones and rags.
From this point onwards the organisation
of a salvage scheme becomes progressively more difficult. In Northern Ireland there is the further consideration
that where the waste cannot be utilised
locally the cost of collection and shipment to Great Britain might more than
offset the advantages to be derived from
its collection.
The salving of waste paper has, in
particular, been receiving the attention
of the Ministry of Commerce for some
time past. In this instance the waste
can, of course, be used within Northern
Ireland itself, and arrangements have
been made which even at present procure
for our paper manufacturers a supply of
waste paper which represents a considerable contribution to the Northern Ireland paper industry's requirements of
raw material.
The salving of waste flax is also receiving special attention and the measures
which have been taken to this end will,
it is believed, result in a useful contri-

bution being made to the requirements of
the paper manufacturing industry in
Great Britain.
On the general question, I can assure
the hon. and gallant Senator that the
Ministry of Commerce and the Ministry
of Home Affairs, as the Departments concerned with local government, are giving
the matter their urgent attention and
that, given the necessary co-operation on
the part of the public generally and those
local authorities which at present collect
household refuse, it is hoped that the
maximum use will be found for household waste within Northern Ireland.
Mr. LESLIE: I would like to ask the
Government whether any special attention is being paid to the collection and
salvage of what I might describe as
better class tin. We all know that
amongst household waste and in household dustbins there are a large number
of tins which have been used for products of a very inexpensive character,
and there are other tins-I was thinking
more of biscuit tins-of a higher class
tin. There are also tins which have contained tobacco and cigarettes, and it
seems to me that that particular tin
must have something more than
negligible value. It might be well
worth while taking special steps to ask
for its preservation, or otherwise to see
that in that direction there is no waste.
I can only leave that suggestion to the
Government, as no notice was given of
a supplementary question.
Mr. ROBB: An effort of that kind was
made during the last war. The difficulty
is one of transport. These things are
very bulky. If you could get them to
Belfast and compress them,
then you
could perhaps transfer them to England
where they would be useful, but it is
just like waste paper. If you had a
baling plant for waste paper in various
centres throughout the Six Counties it
might not be so costly to transfer the
baled paper, but a baling plant is an
expensive thing, and the cost would
possibly outrun the value of the material.
The same thing applies to tin.
Major DOBBS: May I ask if it is absolutely necessary to send everything over
to England. Could not scrap of what-

CLAUSE 12 (Powers of Court in
certain actions by owners to recover possession of goods)
*
(1) Where, in any case to which the
last foregoing section applies, an owner
commences an action to enforce a right
to recover possession of goods from a
hirer the action shall be commenced in
the county court having jurisdiction
where the hirer resides or carries on
business or resided or carried on business at the date on which he last made
a payment under the hire-purchase
agreement, and after the action has
been commenced the owner shall not
take any step to enforce payment of
any sum due under the hire-purchase
agreement or under any contract of
guarantee relating thereto, except by
claiming the sum in the said action.
(2) Subject to such exceptions as
may be provided for by county court
rules, all the parties to the agreement
and
any guarantor shall be made
parties to the action.
*
*
*
*
(10) Where an owner has recovered
a part of the goods let under a hirepurchase agreement, and the recovery
was effected in contravention of the
last foregoing section, the provisions of
this section shall not apply in relation
to any action by the owner to recover
the remainder of the goods.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move to leave out
"shall" where it first occurs and insert
"may."
The House will remember that the
hon. and learned Senator (Mr. Leslie)
upon the Second Reading of the Bill
drew attention to Clause 12 and pointed
out that there were two things which
he wished to criticise. In the first place
it seemed to him that the Clause took
away from the high court the jurisdiction
that it had, and that this House and
Parliament had no power to do that. In
the second place he pointed out that it
was usual where new jurisdiction was
conferred on a court to do that directly
and not by way of inference. On consideration, may I say, both those points.
were agreed upon, and the amendments
that are down on the Paper in my name
are to meet those two objections. With
the leave of the House I will deal with
the amendments at the one time. The
first is to substitute the word "may" for
"shall". The Clause reads:
Where in any case to which the
last foregoing section applies, an owner
commences an action to enforce a
right to recover possession of goods
from a hirer the action shall be commenced in the county court.
It is proposed to meet the objection of
Senator Leslie that we are taking away
from the high court by using the word
"shall" the jurisdiction it possessed, and
that we could not do that, and accordingly it is proposed to insert the word
"may" instead of "shall". The next
Amendment is to leave out certain words
from "agreement" and to confer
jurisdiction on the county court
expressly, a matter that had been done
inferentially before. The other amendments that follow are consequential on
these two amendments. Accordingly, I
move the first Amendment.
I

entirely meet my views of the case.
may, perhaps, remind the House that
the right hon. and learned Gentleman
has done something I did not attempt to

referred to in Clause 12, whereby the
action may be commenced in the place

Mr. ROBB: I beg to move in paragraph 4 to leave out from "those"
("those herein before mentioned") to the
end of the paragraph and insert "just
mentioned. If it does he may put an
end to the agreement on those terms."
This Amendment is a little bit different
to the others. If hon. Members turn to
page 20 they will see how the paragraph
reads. Since this Bill was before the
House we have had a request from
bankers, who are the main bankers
financing hire purchase transactions all
over the United Kingdom, asking us to
alter these words to the words as they
are in the English Act. The reason they
ask us to do that is that they have
hundreds of thousands of forms printed
with the English words only, and they
were afraid that they might have
difficulty or some people might have
suspicion by using these forms for transactions in Northern Ireland. Of course
in these days we want to save all the
paper we can. We do not want to scrap
old forms and print new ones. The
alteration does not make any difference
in the sense.
Mr. LESLIE: Oh, yes.
Mr. ROBB: The words proposed to be
altered do not make any difference in the
sense.
Mr. LESLIE: Few things give me
greater pleasure than supporting any
proposition made by the right hon. and
learned Gentleman, but really there are
limits. What we have got before us in
paragraph 4, page 20, is a perfectly plain
expression couched in perfectly good
English and utterly unmistakable in its.
intention. We have learned since
became Members of this House that the
wording of an Act of Parliament must
we
be so plain that not only can it be read
and understood by everybody who reads.
it in good faith, but it must be so plain
that those who read it in bad faith cannot misunderstand it. I am afraid I
have got to say that the effect of the
alteration proposed would be to turn
good English into bad English and to
produce a result which, if examined
critically, is not sensible but is confusing
and liable to lead to disaster. The
excuse given, of course, is put up by the
other side-
Mr. ROBB: I object to the word
"excuse". "Reason" was the word
used.
Mr. LESLIE : I accept the correction. The reason given is the complaint,
shall we say, made from England that
they had some hundreds of thousands of
forms printed, and they are afraid, they
have so little confidence, I suppose, in
the ability of the people who use those
forms as to imagine that there might be
some mistake made by somebody if they
had to use different forms for Northern
Ireland. It seems to me we can get over
that perfectly simply by printing a few
more of those forms with a large notice
across the top or in the centre with the
words "for use in Northern Ireland
only". Let us take the words it is proposed to introduce. Instead of the words
"hereinbefore mentioned", which, as I
said before, is perfectly good sense and
perfectly simple, it is proposed to insert
the words "just mentioned". I say that
the word "just" is not normally used in
an Act of Parliament. It is not a
good word; it has two meanings. When
you say "just mentioned" you may mean
recently mentioned, or you may mean
simply referred to casually. What does
it mean here? It does not tell you. It
is intended to mean, I presume, recently
mentioned, and if that is so I have got
to point out that that is not very
accurate because the reference is not
surely to Paragraph 3 which deals with
goods damaged, but to Paragraph 2 and
Paragraph 1. Really the word is
inappropriate there. The phrase "hereinbefore mentioned" is perfectly right.
The phrase "just mentioned" is wrong.
Then it goes on, "If it does so, he may
put an end to the agreement on those
more favourable terms." We leave out

THE SENATE.

Wednesday, 13th March, 1940.
The Senate met at a Quarter past Three
of the Clock, Mr. DEPUTY-SPEAKER
(the Lord Mayor of Belfast) in the Chair.

Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Chartered and Other Bodies (Temporary
Provisions) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
move the Second Reading of this Bill. It
is an emergency measure and arises from
this cause, that there are within our area
certain corporations, especially Queen's
University, which
are controlled by
regulations and statutes that have been
made, and from time to time it occurs
that owing to the absence of members of
the corporation or members of committee
it is difficult to get a quorum and sometimes it is difficult to carry out the regulations as they stand. Following the
example we have from Great Britain this
Bill has been introduced dealing with
these bodies and making their constitution
a little bit more flexible in the meantime.
The first Clause deals with corporations
incorporated by Royal charter, and provides that the Governor may give directions modifying the provisions of any
charter, by-laws, regulations, or other instruments relating to the constitution of
the corporation or any body of persons
formed for the purpose of discharging any
of its functions. The second Clause
follows in a similar way for corporations
that are constituted under Act of Parliament or by a Minister under the power
given by Act of Parliament, and in that
case the procedure is to be by the
Governor of Northern Ireland by Order
in Council. I might say that it is not
likely that these first two Clauses will be
much availed of, because we have not got
very many of these bodies in Northern
Ireland.
But when we come to the third,
which deals with Queen's University, the
Government was asked that we here
should follow the example set in Great
Britain, and that an emergency statute
should be provided with regard to the
regulation and procedure of Queen's
University, and accordingly Clause 3 provides that the Governor may, by Order
in Council, on application made by the
governing body of the University, make
provision enabling emergency statutes of
the University to be made, varied or revoked subject to and in accordance with
the provisions of the Order. It is safeguarded in this way, that before any
Order is to be made the application must
be sanctioned by the three governing
bodies of the University, the senate, the
academic council, and convocation.
Clause 4 deals with details of these orders,
saying that they may operate retrospectively from any date back to 1st September, 1939.
Clause 5 specifically exempts the corporation of any city or borough; or the
council of any county, county borough or
other borough or county district, the commissioners of any town not being an
urban district, or the board of guardians
of any union; or any corporation or body
of persons by which functions are exercisable not only in, but also outside Northern
Ireland,
Queen's
except
University.
Clause 6 provides for the laying of Orders
in Council before Parliament. Clause 7
is the Interpretation Clause. The period
of the present emergency is defined as the
period beginning 1st September, 1939,
and ending with such date as the
Governor of Northern Ireland may by
Order in Council declare to be the date on
which the emergency that was the
occasion of the passing of this Act came
to an end. By Clause 8 the Act is to
continue in force until the end of the
period of the present emergency and shall
Chartered Bodies Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
then expire. These are the provisions of
this Bill, and I move the Second Reading.
Question proposed, "That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
Colonel SINCLAIR: I only want to
make one observation relating to Queen's
University, of which I have the honour
to be a member. I think the Government need have no hesitation in bestowing these powers, for they are very carefully safeguarded. You will notice that
unless we move an amendment asking for
certain elasticity to be introduced into
the statutes no amendment takes place.
Convocation, the senate and the council
must agree asking for extra elasticity. I
think from the way it is safeguarded the
Government may be well assured that no
indiscretion will arise. We cannot look
into the future, but as the Leader of the
House has explained, difficulties may arise,
and the provision is adequate and
generous. I can assure the Senate on
behalf of the senate of the University
that we are not likely to make any change
in our procedure unless there is some
urgent necessity for it.
Mr. LESLIE: I am
sure there is
nobody in the House will object to the
general provisions of the Bill before us.
It is as the right hon. Gentleman told
us, a purely enabling Bill, and we would
have seen for ourselves, those of us who
have read the Bill, that it is encompassed
about with every conceivable safeguard.
It cannot possibly do any harm to anybody, and it may do a great deal of good.
I do not think there was any precedent
of this class during the last war, but I
understood, perhaps wrongly, from the
right hon. Gentleman that something of
this nature had been passed by the
Parliament of the United Kingdom.
Mr. ROBB: An identical Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: I thought it had,
although I have not seen the Bill. It is
a natural development which one might
reasonably expect and to which no exception can be taken. The keynote seems to
be that to which the Leader of the House
alluded, its flexibility. That is, perhaps,
particularly illustrated as regards the
Clause relating to the University to which
the hon. and gallant Senator alluded, and
to which no exception can possibly be
taken. These Clauses are an example of
the safeguards to which I have referred.
Nothing can be done except at request
and with the protection suggested. The
only little difficulty I feel about the Bill
-and it is not a Second Reading difficulty is the question of what, for want
of a better word, I might describe as
reciprocity. Have we any chartered
bodies over here to which this Bill is
applicable that are not included amongst
the chartered bodies across the water? Is
there any conceivable difficulty as regards
that? If so, perhaps something ought to
be done at a later stage, but I confess I
do not myself know of any case in which
that question could arise.
We can legislate here for our own
chartered bodies, but we cannot do anything as regards chartered bodies across
the water. There they have legislation
for their chartered bodies, and they cannot touch ours, but is there any necessity
to introduce anything to connect the two?
That, I think, might be considered before
we get to the next stage, and the appropriate passage to consider the matter
would seem to me to be on the Question,
"That Clause 1 stand part of the Bill."
If there is anything to be said on that
point perhaps the right hon. Gentleman,
the Leader of the House, would take a
note of it and tell us if anything is required. I do not myself for the moment
suggest that it is.
The only other question that occurs to
my mind is in connection with sub-clause
(2) of Clause 8, which says that the Bill
shall continue in force until the end of
the period of the present emergency. I
am rather inclined to wonder whether
that is quite safe. What is going to
happen when the emergency expires,
perhaps suddenly, as we hope it may.
Shall we be under any difficulty there?
That again is a point that might be looked
into and as to which advice might be
tendered to us when the time comes. I
am sure the House sees what I am thinking of. In sundry other Bills we have
the suggestion that the Bill or whatever
it is should expire without prejudice to
anything already done thereunder, or, it
might be, that the effect of the Bill shall
continue for so many months after the
expiration of the emergency.

Local Authorities and Jurors Books
(Temporary Provisions) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This also is a Bill necessitated by the present emergency and is
similar in
scope to a corresponding
Act already passed in the Imperial
Parliament.
Its main object is to provide for the
suspension of the elections of local government bodies for a period of one year.
The Bill does not apply to all locally
elected bodies, because the only elections.
actually postponed are those for county
boroughs and those boroughs and urban
district councils which have annual and
not tri-annual elections. County councils,
rural district councils, and boards of
guardians are elected for three years, and
as the last election was in 1939 the next
election is not due until 1942. This Bill
only applies for one year and, therefore,
will not affect those elections. The
reason for limiting the postponement for
a year is in order that the effect on local
government administration may be judged
and the advisability of further postponing
the elections considered.
Clause 1 gives effect to the proposal to
postpone the elections, and also provides
that casual vacancies shall be filled by cooption and not by election. At the
present time in the case of certain bodies
these casual vacancies are filled by cooption, and it is proposed to make this.
the rule for all local government bodies.
It will be necessary, of course, to hold
annual meetings for the elections of chairmen and vice-chairmen, and Clause 2 provides for the continuance of the usual
procedure for these meetings.
As no doubt hon. Members are aware,
absence from the meetings of a local
authority for six consecutive months
renders a member liable to disqualification. During the last war it was
considered inequitable that this disqualification should apply to a member unavoidably absent either through joining some
branch of His Majesty's Forces or being
engaged in some other work of national
importance. Accordingly, in Clause 3, we
are continuing this exemption, and also
extending it to members of the Belfast
Water Commissioners.
The English Act has already provided
for the postponement of the preparation
of the Register of Electors in Northern
Ireland. As hon. Members are aware,
the Register, which is used for local
government elections, and for elections to
this Parliament, is the Register which is
prepared for the election of Northern
Ireland Members to the Imperial Parliament and, therefore, of course, this is a
matter which had to be dealt with by the
Imperial Parliament. The preparation,
however, of the jurors' lists is a matter
for this Parliament, and in Clause 4 it
is proposed to postpone the preparation
of any further jurors' books, and in
Clause 5 to relieve anyone from any statutory liabilities in this regard. Clause 6
does not find a place in the Imperial Act.
The remuneration of the majority of
local officers in Northern Ireland consists
of a salary, plus certain fees for the
preparation of electors' and jurors' lists.
In many cases these fees form a very considerable portion of the income of such
officers, and, indeed, local authorities in
the past have taken into consideration
these fees when fixing their salaries. The
Jurors Books Bill
Local Government Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
Government feels that it would be unfair
to these officers if some provision were
not made for enabling compensation to
be paid to them now, owing to the fact
that during the period of the war they
will not be receiving any remuneration
by way of fees for the preparation of the
lists to which I have referred.
It will be noted that the compensation
is only for "undue loss of income," and
that the Ministry is to be the judge of
what is fair and equitable compensation.
In calculating this compensation the
Ministry will take into consideration the
fees normally received in each case, providing of course for a deduction of an
amount corresponding to the out-of-pocket
expenses which would be incurred
normally by the officer. The Ministry
will also bear in mind the basic salary
paid to the officer concerned and the
amount of compensation, if any, will
depend to a very large degree on how
nearly the Ministry regards this salary as
a fair and equitable income for the duties
of the officer concerned.
Sub-clause 2 of the Clause preserves
the superannuation rights of those local
officers who have statutory duties to perform in regard to the preparation of these
lists. Where at present an officer is entitled to include his fees in the emoluments of his office, which are taken into
consideration for superannuation purposes, it would, I think, be inequitable
to deprive him of this privilege if he
should happen to retire while this Act
is in force.
The Act, as I have said, will expire at
the end of the year, but the term of office
of a member of a local authority will be
extended until six months after the Act
has expired, unless Parliament decides to
make other arrangements. I beg to
"That the Bill be now read a
move,
Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for to-morrow.

CLAUSE 1 (Postponement of local

In
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I rise to oppose
this clause, and the Bill as a whole.
my opinion there is no necessity whatever for it in the Six Counties. Whatever argument there may be for it in
Great Britain, where you have conscription and all man power engaged in war
work, there is none here for the simple
reason that, as far as I can see, there is
no rush of the Ulster loyalists to join
the colours, nor is there any serious dislocation in the Six Counties due to the
war.
This Bill is merely for the purpose of
keeping the Government's own henchmen
in office for the duration of the war, and
to lay the foundations for further gerrymandering of the constituencies after the
war. In the city of Armagh we have
had a Commissioner or a dictator for the
past six years. Why? Because the
majority of the voters of the city of
Armagh are Nationalists and Catholics
and the Commissioner, who costs the
ratepayers 4d in the £, is kept there for
the purpose of depriving the citizens of
Armagh of exercising the franchise, thus
preventing a majority Nationalist
council from functioning.
Further, it is a well-known fact that
arrangements are already on the way for
the gerrymandering of the city by way of
the extension of the city boundary. I
understand the Commissioner has already
set the machinery in motion to accomplish this, and we may be sure he will not
be withdrawn until this good work is
completed. Conferences of the Unionists
have already been held in the Armagh
City Hall to consider the gerrymandering
scheme and to make sure that the gerrymandering is well done, and then the
Commissioner, as he has already stated,
will apply to Armagh County Council
Jurors Books Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
for the extension of the city boundary,
and the gerrymandering of the city. The
county council will complete the good
work and make sure, as far as is possible,
that there will be no chance of
Nationalist majority council functioning
in the city again. This is one of the
a
reasons for this Bill to enable the
Commissioner to be kept on until this
gerrymandering extension of the city
boundary is complete and an accomplished fact.
A similar thing occurred in the cases
of the Nationalist majorities in the City
of Derry, Omagh, and the counties of
Tyrone and Fermanagh, where the
Nationalists have been victimised. Could
anything be worse even under Hitler?
You talk of the occupation of Poland,
but what of the occupation of the County
of Tyrone by a Unionist County Council
representing the minority. In that
county the Nationalists have a majority
of the population of 15,000, and they
can return only eleven county councillors
against the minority's sixteen. What of
Fermanagh, where the Nationalist population have a majority of 6,500 and can
only return seven members to the county
council against the minority's thirteen.
What of Derry City, where the
Nationalists have a majority of 2,500
local government voters and can only
return eight members to the Corporation
against twelve Unionists.
Then take the gerrymandering of
Omagh. No wonder that 35 per cent. of
the population of the Six Counties are
dissatisfied; no wonder we have unrest in
our midst when such a state of affairs
exists. You say the British are fighting
for the rights of the minorities and small
nations. Well, if the British are sincere
in this, then they will put their own
house in order by seeing that the 35 per
cent. minority in the Six Counties get
at least simple justice. For these
reasons I object to this Bill.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I

of the United Kingdom that is engaged

CLAUSE 6 (Compensation in case of
local officers).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill."
Major BOYLE: I would like to ask the
Leader of the House a question on this
Clause. I understand that the effect of
this Clause is to give to different officers
who are whole-time officers of local
authorities certain remuneration for
work they will not have to do. If that
is so, can the right hon. Gentleman give
the Committee any idea of the number of
these officials who will receive this remuneration for not doing anything, and
the amount it will cost the ratepayers. It
seems to me that these few people, who
are excellent officials, are going to be paid
money for something they will not have
to do. As a result of this war there are
a great many other people who will have
less work than usual to do, and they will
receive proportionately less money.
Mr. ROBB: It is quite true that these
people are going to be paid in respect of
work they will not have to do, but, as I
pointed out on the Second Reading, they
are not going to get what they would have
got had this Bill not been introduced.
They are to get only such sum as the
Ministry of Home Affairs shall consider
fair compensation for undue loss of
income. I cannot tell the hon. Member
the number of officials affected, nor can I
give any estimate of the amount they will
be paid. The source of the payments will
be the rates. It is quite true that these
officers are whole-time officials, but their
duties included the preparation of these
lists, and when they were appointed their
total remuneration, in most cases, was
fixed after consideration by the paying
body of the amount they would receive
from these fees, plus their basic salary.
In many cases the basic salary was fixed
very low, because they were going to get
these fees.
What is proposed by this Clause is that
the Ministry of Home Affairs shall consider each case on its merits. It will
consider the basic salary, and the net
amount the officer would have received,
after his out-of-pocket expenses had
been paid, from these fees. The Ministry
will not necessarily allot to that officer
the total net amount he would have
received from his fees, but an estimate
will be made of what will be a reasonable
salary for doing the duties he does
perform. Where the basic salary does not
amount to such a sum as the Ministry
thinks would be a reasonable salary for
the work he actually performs they will
direct that he shall be paid for what is
termed in the Bill "undue loss of
income". I think it is perfectly fair,
where a man's emoluments are in two
parts, basic salary and fees, and the fees
have been used as a squeezer to reduce
the basic salary, and when those fees are
cut off and the basic salary does not
appear to be reasonable remuneration for
the work the officer does do, some grant
in respect of loss of fees should be paid
to him. That is the principle of this
Clause.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Clause accordingly ordered to stand
part of the Bill.
CLAUSES 7 (Interpretation) and 8
(Short title and duration) ordered to
stand part of the Bill.

Local Government (Finance) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is a one Clause
Money Bill, and the effect of it is this.
It increases from £37,000 a year to
£60,000 a year the grant that is paid
from the Exchequer towards the cost of
the upkeep of the mental hospitals in
Northern Ireland. There is to be a
per capita valuation in which patients.
who are criminal lunatics and those who
are in mental hospitals under arrange-
109 Local Government Bill

14 MARCH 1940
Adjournment
ment with the Ministry of Pensions shall
not be counted, but the capitation grant
is to be paid in respect of all other
patients. The Bill provides that in the
case of a hospital serving two or more
counties the grant shall be paid to the
different counties in proportion to the
number of patients from each county in
the hospital. That is all the Bill does.
It entails an increased charge on the Exchequer and proportionately relieves the
rates. I beg to move, "That this Bill
be now read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Leader of the House whether it is a fact
that during the past few months the
Northern Ireland Road Transport Board
have acquired lorries from firms in
Northern Ireland who were not public
hauliers within the meaning of the Road
and Railway Transport Act (Northern
Ireland), 1935; whether in any such case
the firm previously forwarded a proportion of its traffic by rail; whether such
traffic has now, as a result of the acquisition of these lorries, been withdrawn from
the railway company and is being carried
by Transport Board lorries; and whether
such an arrangement is in accordance
with the terms and spirit of the Road
and Railway Transport Act (Northern
Ireland), 1935.
I would like to point out that the Select
Committee on Road and Rail Transport
last August issued their report, and we
find their unanimous recommendation
was that if the Transport Board was to
function and to be a success there must
be co-ordination of services, and as far
as possible useless
useless competition and
duplication of services must be eliminated.
When the Committee inspected the
depots of the Transport Board they were
surprised to see such a huge number of
I
lorries and buses only fit for scrap.
often wondered what became of them,
and I cannot help thinking they must
be a very serious loss to the taxpayers.
Now I understand the Board are purchasing more lorries from private traders
or companies who are not public hauliers.
I believe it is the practice to purchase
these lorries from these firms on the
understanding that the firms concerned
will give the Transport Board all their
traffic, which means that in a great many
cases traffic which heretofore has been
carried by the railway is now under this
undertaking to the Transport Board
transferred to the Board's lorries.
Thus we will see that instead of coordination of traffic we have open competition, and competition on a subsidised
basis which no public companies, railways or others could compete with.
Surely anyone will see that this does not
carry out the intention of the 1935 Act
or help co-ordination, and surely there
must be a very serious loss on these lorries
purchased. We all remember that when
these lorries were purchased from the
public hauliers the intention of the Act
was defeated. We had plenty of evidence
at the Transport Committee, which
proved that that Act not alone was
defeated, but that the public hauliers
from whom these lorries were purchased
immediately purchased other lorries and
entered into fresh competition with the
Board. I suggest that the same thing
will happen when the lorries are purchased from private firms even though
this undertaking must be given to give
the Board all their traffic, for the Transport Board has no means of enforcing
that undertaking.
I
say
that this in itself creates very
serious and unfair competition. Moreover it is in the nature of dealing in
lorries, and I shall be very much surprised if the motor trade when they hear
of it do not object very seriously to their
interests being interfered with. In the
Transport Board's report of 1939 there is
a sum of no less than over £200,000 for
depreciation, which may be taken as the
minimum figure, and if carefully
examined would be very much higher.
I do not wish to detain the House very
much longer, but I had intended to
supplement this question by further asking, in the hope that the Leader of the
House may see his way to take a note of
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
19 MARCH 1940
it, how many lorries were purchased since
August last, what was the horse-power of
those lorries, what became of such lorries
as were purchased, were they put into
service, or if sold what was the loss sustained on each lorry. And further, did
the firm from whom the lorries were purchased give an undertaking to the Board
that they would hand to the Board all
their traffic in future.
With regard to the second question, all
I would say is that if you look at the
Report of the Road and Rail Transport
Committee, page 97, paragraph 197, you
will see it stated by the Committee:
It will be apparent from what has
already been said that the present
position demands immediate and energetic action. If the situation is to be
saved and public transport placed on
a sound and economic basis, a definite
policy must be decided upon by the
Government in the near future. The
present state of drift and uncertainty
is doing damage and making a solution
of the problem more difficult.
I beg to ask the question.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The Road Transport Board have
furnished the following information with
regard to the matters raised in the
question:-
In three instances within recent months
the Board have purchased a lorry or
lorries from firms who were not public
hauliers within the meaning of the Road
and Railway Transport Act, 1935.
In one of the cases the Board undertook, on behalf of a large firm, distribution of their commodities from depots.
This work had been previously done by
the firm themselves. The Board is merely
doing the work formerly done by the
firm's own lorries, and such portion of
their traffic as formerly went by rail continues to be forwarded by the same way.
In the case of another firm, the greater
part of their work was done on their own
lorries, but substantial quantities of
traffic were forwarded from time to time
by a haulier whose undertaking was
acquired by the Board under the Act.
So far as the Board's information goes,
this firm did not forward any traffic by
rail.
In the third case, the merchant carried
almost all his goods in his own lorries.
Occasionally, when his lorries were unable
to cope with all his work, lots were forwarded by public transport, but in relation to the whole the amount was small,
and very rarely was the railway used.
In each of these cases the Board bought
such of the lorries as were necessary, in
addition to their own fleet, to deal with
the increased traffic. The prices paid
were the market value of the lorries themselves, and did not include any consideration for the traffic transferred. The result
of these arrangements has been to
transfer from private to public transport
thousands of tons of traffic every year.
The Government do not consider that
these transactions in any way cut across
the spirit or the letter of the Act of 1935.
On the contrary, they achieve one of the
purposes of the Act, that is to say, the
transfer of large blocks of traffic from
public
private transport to organised
transport. The hon. Senator asked a
number of questions at the end of his
speech. To answer them I would need
notice.

through petrol control to force their
