123 Old Age Pensions Bill-

THE SENATE
First Reading
Old Age and Widows' Pensions Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Robb.)
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Old Age and Widows'
Pensions Bill."-(Mr. Robb.)
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The existing pensions legislation provides
two main schemes under which pensions
are paid to applicants who satisfy prescribed conditions.
The first is the non-contributory old
age pension scheme. This scheme provides an old-age pension for all applicants who have reached the age of 70 and
who satisfy the means test and other
conditions as to residence and nationality.
In the case of blind persons the pension
is payable from age 40.
The second is the contributory
pensions scheme. This scheme provides
for insured persons an old-age pension at
age 65, and in addition it provides an
old-age pension at age 65 for the wife
of an insured man who is himself entitled
to pension, and also for his widow and
children. Pensions paid under the contributory scheme are contractual and not
subject to any means test.
The Voluntary Contributors Act, 1937,
admitted to the benefits of the scheme
men and women with incomes not exceeding £400 and £250 per annum respectively. These were, before the passing of
that Act, debarred from the scheme. The
present Bill does not materially affect
the basis of the contributory pension
scheme, nor does it affect the voluntary
contributor admitted under the 1937 Act,
to whom I have referred.
at an
The Bill has two purposes-the first is
to provide pensions for women
earlier age, and the second is to provide,
in view of the increase in the cost of
living, additional allowances for old-age
pensioners and for widow pensioners over
60. The objects of the Bill have already
been outlined in an explanatory memorandum, which is in the hands of Senators
and it is not, therefore, necessary for me,
at this stage, to enter into a very
detailed explanation of the provisions.
Under the first part of the Bill pensions
will be provided for women at an earlier
age.
This will relieve insured women
from having to look for industrial work
during the years between 60 and 65, and
will also provide earlier pensions for the
wives of insured men who are themselves
in receipt of a contributory pension.
In the first clause the pensionable age
for a woman who is insured in her own
right, or who is married to an insured
man who has attained pensionable agethat is 65-is reduced from 65 to 60. This
will be from the 1st July next.
To provide in part for the increase in the
amount, which will now be paid out each
year in old-age pensions, the contributions payable in respect of men are increased by 2d and in respect of women
by 3d. The total women's pensions contribution will now be about two-thirds of
the total man's contribution, whereas
hitherto a woman paid just half the man's
The reason for this change is to
provide on an equitable basis for the more
valuable concession now secured by insured women.
rate.
The proposed changes do not affect the
special contracts made in the case of
special voluntary contributors. These are
insured under a separate contract.
At
the moment the great majority of special
voluntary contributors are considerably
older than the average pensions contributor and they have been admitted under
specially favourable conditions. Consequential amendments of the Pensions
Acts are necessary, and these will be
found in the First Schedule.
A woman's right to sickness and disablement benefit under the Health Acts
will now cease at age 60 in consequence
of Clause 2, and she will no longer after
that age be entitled to unemployment
benefit, but in Clause 3 it is provided
that for a transitional period of five years
women who with the passing of the Act
suffer a hardship through, for example,
not being five years in insurance and,
therefore, unable to get a pension at the
age of 60 will, in the meantime, if they
are sick, be given sickness payments of
10s a week. They will also be able to
continue to qualify for old-age pensions.
At present a woman drawing a widow's
pension can get sickness benefit as well,
and this clause will preserve her right
during the transitional period to get a
10s sickness payment until she reaches the
125 Old Age Pensions Bill-

18 APRIL 1940
First Reading
age of 65. These sickness payments will
be made through approved societies.
Clause 4 is concerned with the financial
adjustments that will be necessary as a
result of the setting free of reserves which
societies have built up to meet the heavy
sickness claims of women members between the ages of 60 and 65. The reserves so set free will be transferred from
the societies and paid into a special
suspense fund established under the corresponding United Kingdom Act from
which the sickness payments will be made.
That Act provides that an Order in
Council may be made extending the scope
of the fund to Northern Ireland. It has
also been arranged that if any part of
the balance left in the fund after the discharge of the sickness payments referred
to is diverted to another purpose a proportionate amount will be allocated to
Northern Ireland.
Clause 5 provides that the additional
amount required for the payment of these
pensions will be provided by the
Exchequer. This will at first be about
£16,000 a year, rising gradually to about
£47,000 in the year 1950-51. About
3,500 insured women and 2,800 wives of
insured men will be affected.
Coming now to Part II, it is proposed
to provide additional allowances for oldage pensioners and for widow pensioners
over 60. It has for some time been
generally recognised that something over
and above the 10s pension is needed
where the pensioner has no other means,
and certain outdoor relief payments have
to this end been made by local authorities. Part II of the Bill removes this
liability from local authorities and lays
the burden upon the State. Instead of
outdoor relief, pensioners who are in need
will be granted supplementary pensions
payable at post offices in the same manner
and on the same days as their ordinary
pensions. The supplementary pensions
will be administered through the former
Unemployment Assistance Board, now to
be renamed the Assistance Board. Anyone who is eligible for a supplementary
pension will no longer have a right to
draw unemployment assistance.
Clause 10 provides that the administration of supplementary pensions will
be on the same lines as the present
administration of unemployment assistance, and in the Second Schedule will
be found the necessary modifications of
the Unemployment Assistance Act of
1934.
One important modification is that relating to Section 33 of that Act. Under
the provisions of that Section the Board,
in determining the resources of an applicant, disregards: -(1) The first 5s of any
sick pay from a friendly society, the first
7s 6d of health insurance benefits, and
the first statutory maternity benefit; (2)
the first £1 of any wounds or disability
pension; (3) one-half of any weekly
workmen's compensation payment; (4)
capital assets up to £25.
For the purpose of calculating the
means of a claimant to supplementary
pensions there shall also be disregarded,
as provided in the Second Schedule, the
first 7s 6d a week of any superannuation
payment and the first 7s 6d of any sickness payment under Part I of the Bill.
It is only after full consideration of
all the circumstances that the method
proposed in the Bill for the relief of
needy pensioners has been adopted
instead of that of granting a flat all
round increase of pensions. Many
pensioners are not in need, and to grant
an increase to such persons would create
a strain upon the Exchequer which could
not be justified in present circumstances.
Pensioners who have received their
pensions on grounds of insurance are
under no restrictions as to the amount of
their incomes. They may have substantial means, they may have property,
and they may continue at work without
any modification of their rate of pension.
Non-contributory pensioners, though
subject to a means test, may yet have
also substantial means. A single
pensioner may have property to the value
of £865 before the rate of his pension is
affected, and a married couple may have
twice that amount or £1,730 and still
receive full pension at the rate of 10s a
week each.
Other provisions refer to special
circumstances. Clause 11 refers to
pensioners who, through infirmity or other
cause, are unlikely to spend their supplementary pensions to their own advantage,
and provision is made in that Clause that
in these cases the Board may pay the
supplementary pension to some
some other
Old Age Pensions Bill-
THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Robb]
person. The pensioner will, however,
have a right of appeal.
If an applicant for a supplementary
pension has at any time during the
month ending 3rd August, 1940, been
receiving outdoor relief at a rate higher
than the rate which he would get according to the scale for supplementary
pensions, Clause 13 provides that the
Board may grant the pension at the
higher rate.
The days to be prescribed under Clause
14 for the payment of the weekly supplementary pensions will correspond to the
days on which the present old age and
widows' pensions are paid. Pensioners
will thus be able to draw both their
ordinary pensions and their supplementary pensions at the Post Office on
the same day.
It is not possible to give more than a
rough estimate of the number of
pensioners who will become entitled to
supplementary pensions. It is probable
that many will make claims who would
not apply for outdoor relief, and it is
estimated that out of approximately
90,000 pensioners as many as 25,000 may
be expected to make successful claims.
On this basis the cost of the supplementary pensions may be in the
neighbourhood of £250,000 a year, and
under Clause 15 the entire amount will
be borne by the Exchequer. The Unemployment Assistance Fund and supplementary pensions scheme are to be dealt
with separately in the Board's accounts.
These are the main provisions of the
Bill. It is the Government's policy to
keep the social services of Northern Ireland up to the same level as those in
Great Britain. So the Bill is on the
same lines as the measure recently passed
at Westminster, and I beg to move,
"That the Bill be now read a Second
time.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I am
sure all in this House will welcome this
Bill, especially when they recognise the
fact that it has already been passed
through the Imperial House.
We are
anxious that the workers in Northern
Ireland should have exactly the same
There
benefits as those across the water.
is no doubt but that this measure will be
of great benefit to the old couple-the man
who is over 65 and unable to work, and
whose wife will also be entitled to a
pension. She will now receive the pension
when she reaches the age of 60, while
her husband receives it at the age of 65.
It will be of tremendous benefit to a
considerable number of couples who are
living in straitened circumstances at the
present time; living, perhaps, on 10s per
week.
There has been considerable criticism in
regard to the means test. I do not
oppose the means test, because I know
there are quite a number of men entitled
to the pension at 65 who do not need a
supplementary allowance.
On the other hand, there are a good
number who are on the border line. Those
are the people I am thinking of now. I
met an old man two days ago. He is 76
years of age and has been drawing the 65
pension since he was entitled to do so,
and he is also receiving 10s from the
Engineers' Society. That man complained
bitterly about the means test. He has
not a wife, but he is trying to keep a roof
over his head as he has done for years
and years. I believe he has a little in the
post office to which he is holding on in
case he might need it in the future.
is a man like that who will not be entitled
to the supplementary allowance, and I
have every sympathy with such cases.
It
We have to recognise the fact that the
State always helps the less industrious
more than the industrious. I remember when the first pension at 70 was
introduced and the amount was 10s per
week, an engineer who had paid all his
life towards superannuation benefit of 10s
did not receive the pension; while a man
who had never worked regularly and paid
into no society got the full amount. That
is the class of case which profits from
State benefits. I hope the border-line
cases will be treated fairly and generously.
It would be unfair if they were not so
treated. Take, for instance, the 70
pension. The man who draws this pension
contributed nothing to it during his lifetime, yet he is the class of person likely
to receive a supplementary allowance. The
man of 65 who has been contributing
since the insurance scheme came into
operation in 1912 will not be entitled to a
129 Old Age Pensions Bill-
18 APRIL 1940

Second Reading
supplementary pension provided the Committee thinks he has sufficient to live on.
I hope those who are administering this
Act; those responsible for granting a
supplementary allowance, will be as
generous as possible to the border-line
cases. If this is done I am sure those
who have sufficient means will not
I do not think any person
grumble.
would ask for a supplementary pension if
he had sufficient means. It is the borderline cases I am thinking of, especially
some of the old shop fellows of mine who
are drawing 10s from the Engineers'
Society and who will have to be content
with 10s from this Act, having paid all
these years, while the other fellow gets an
increase without having had to pay anything.
I support the Bill because it will be
of benefit to a large number. I am sorry
the means test is necessary, but, as I have
already stated, I hope it will be generously administered.
Mr. LESLIE: I find that I myself, in
common with a good many other people
both in Northern Ireland and across the
water, view with something approaching
amazement the introduction of a Bill of
this nature at the present time. The
amazement does not come in any way
from a lack of sympathy with the objects
of the Bill, but it does come from the
circumstances in which we find ourselves
at the moment. If I understand the
matter correctly the Bill now before us,
taken as a whole, is likely to impose a
liability of not less than £300,000 a year
upon this Province. £300,000 is a sum
of really some little magnitude, and
whether that £300,000 is, so to speak,
met directly by our people or whether it
comes, as in fact I imagine it will come,
indirectly rather than directly it must
have the same effect as a reduction in our
Imperial contribution.
We shall certainly, I imagine, in making up our accounts with Great Britain,
be allowed the expenditure under this Bill
if the House accepts it on the same lines
as other social alleviation measures. However that is done the nett result must be
that the £300,000 or whatever the figure
may be, will operate as a reduction of
the amount that is placed to the benefit
of the general Imperial pool. I do not.
complain of that particularly, but I think
it is right that it should be pointed out.
That is the position at the moment, and
for some years to come.
I am afraid that we have to think of
what is likely to be the position after
the war; what will be the financial
position of the United Kingdom at the
conclusion of hostilities. Shall we then
be able to maintain our social services
in Northern Ireland on the same level
as they are maintained in Great Britain,
and will Great Britain be able to maintain the social services on the level in
force to-day? Those are problems which,
I think, are deserving of the consideration of the financial experts and, I might
almost add, of the financial prophets.
Passing from that, I am inclined to
ask whether people really want this Bill.
I understand, of course, that the woman
who is going to get an old-age pension
at 60 is extremely anxious that the Bill
should pass. One can quite understand
that, but what about the women, and for
that matter the men, who have got to
pay for it. I am thinking particularly

who will have to pay their additional 2d
per week and who will, in due course,
marry perhaps a man who is not an
insured person and will, if one may use
the expression, drop out of insurance.
Are these women anxious to pay this
extra 2d to secure the more
or less
problematical benefits offered by the Bill
before us. There should, I think, be
some information available on that point,
and, if so, there ought to be evidence
which might, perhaps, be laid before us.
All one can say is that there does not
seem to have been any great enthusiasm
either at Westminster or in another place
here for that part of the Bill which proposes that these people should pay that
extra 2d for the benefits that may accrue
to them under the Bill.

Rent and Mortgage Interest (Restrictions) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
This Bill is the same as a measure introduced at Westminster immediately after
the outbreak of war and effects precisely
a similar purpose.
Part I of the Bill
extends the present legislation controlling
rents and mortgage interest until twelve
months after the end of the period of the
present emergency. Part II of the Bill
imposes control on all dwelling-houses
not at present controlled, the annual
Ivalue of which does not exceed £75. The
effect of this provision is to limit the
rent and mortgage interest payable in
respect of a controlled house to that payable on the 1st September last permitting
the landlord to make the two increases
in rent referred to in Clause 6, paragraphs (a) and (b).
These increases are, firstly, an increase
representing 8 per cent. of the value of
any structural alterations made by the
landlord; and, secondly, where the landlord the rates he can add to the rent
pays
any increase in the rates over those in
operation during the last financial year.
These proposals are on similar lines to
those contained in the English measure,
while the figure of £75 annual value
corresponds to that in operation in the
provinces in England.
I might explain to hon. Senators that
Part II of the Bill is not merely enacting
legislation, but it is really a consolidation
of existing rent restriction legislation
as it will apply to the houses now
brought under control. In view of the
complexity of this code of laws the
Government considered it desirable
that the law should be consolidated to
the greatest extent, and legal practitioners and those whose duty it is to be
conversant with rent restriction will find
in Part II of the Bill a consolidation of
existing Rent Restriction law with the
substitution of amendments rendered
necessary to bring the provisions into
line with the proposals to which the
assent of Parliament is being asked.
There are only one or two points in
the Bill which I think it necessary to
deal with specifically. It will be observed
that, while the standard rent and rate
of interest are those prevailing on 1st
September last, the Bill does not apply
to any payments in excess of the standard
rent or interest made before the 1st May
next, the date on which the Bill comes
into operation. It will also be noticed
that Part II of the Bill applies to houses
whether erected before or after the date
the Act comes into operation, and that
the annual value is that on the appropriate day which is the 1st September
for houses valued prior to the outbreak
of war, and for houses subsequently
valued the date when the property was
first valued.
Another matter to which I would draw
attention is the provisions of Clause 14,
which reproduces the existing provisions
in that behalf with the substitution of
£13 for £8 annual value. The clause
provides that a house valued under this
figure shall not be deemed to be a furnished house and, therefore, outside the
scope of the legislation even if the owner
lets it with furniture. Apart from the
few points I have mentioned I do not
Rent Restrictions Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
think I need explain the provisions of
the Bill further.
I beg to move,
"That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I must say that I regard
this as a very disappointing measure. We
had hoped that after we had passed the
last Rent Restrictions Act in 1936which, it will be remembered, carried on
the restrictions introduced in 1932 for a
further four years that the 30th April
this year would have seen the end of rent
restrictions in Northern Ireland. I think
I am correct in saying we were given to
understand that that would be the case.
Now the proposal is to go back for a
period of some months after the conclusion of the present emergency. What
is going to happen after that? Will there
ever be an end to rent restriction? Is
the Government really in earnest in suggesting, as it has done on two or three
occasions, after intervals of years, that
the Government really does desire that
rent restriction should come to an end,
its place being taken, of course, by the
adequate provision of a sufficient number
of houses.
For the English measure and, I believe,
for this measure in another place, it was
claimed in support that the necessity for
extending rent restriction was due to the
war. One would like. to know what
difference the war has made to that
matter here in Northern Ireland. In
Northern Ireland the circumstances are
not the same as they are in Great Britain.
In Northern Ireland we have no evacuation either of school children-who might
perhaps not much affect the questionor civil servants or business houses. I
understand that a great part of the city
of London and surrounding district has
been evacuated not only by business
houses but also by civil servants, and
that must necessarily have put a strain
on the housing accommodation available.
There might be some justification in
England for State interference, but we
ought to be told what justification on
that ground-that is to say, circumstances
due to the war-exists in Northern
Ireland.
Passing from that, and assuming for
the moment that the Bill is required in
Northern Ireland, whether as a war
measure or not, and in order to be on the
same lines as Great Britain, I am afraid
I cannot agree with the Leader of the
House that this measure is the same as
that passed in Great Britain. First of
all, in Part I of the Bill before us, it is
proposed to continue control for twelve
months after the conclusion of the present
emergency. The provision in the English
Act, which received the Royal Assent on
1st September last year, is for six
months, not twelve. That is one not
wholly immaterial point of difference.
Moreover, we have in Northern Ireland,
as of course they have in Great Britain,
in existence a power on the part of the
competent
authorities to requisition
houses if they are really required. That
is the position to-day. I think I may say
that on the 1st September last year, two
days before the outbreak of war-and it
was on 1st September that the British
measure received the Royal Assent-the
power of requisitioning had not come into
general use, and it may be it had been
somewhat overlooked when the measure
was passed in such a hurry.
It
The Bill is in itself a difficult Bill.
is a Bill that is hard to follow. It is
particularly hard to follow this measure
when you have to approach it at such
express speed as is apparently required.
The war began on 3rd September and the
British measure has been in operation
since that time. In Northern Ireland no
measure to deal with the situation is
brought forward until the latter half of
April, 1940, in order to deal with the
measure which would expire on the 30th
of the same month. For all practical
purposes the House has got about seven
days to consider the matter.
I do suggest that this House has just cause for

I recognise and the right hon. Gentleman very kindly laid stress upon it—that

I believe, entirely absent on this occasion.
The main difficulty seems to me to
arise from Part II of the Bill. Part II
follows Great Britain as regards the introduction of the figure of £75. In 1920
-and I do not propose to take the House
further back in the history of rent restriction-when the Bill was passed in the
Parliament at Westminster; that is to
say, by a Parliament in which, of course,
Ireland as a whole was represented, and
it was passed at a date when the Northern Ireland Parliament had not come
into existence; in 1920 the figures were
not those inserted in the present Bill, or
those inserted in the Act which passed
at Westminster last September.
figures were, for the city of London and
the district of the Metropolitan policewhich is still, I believe, about 15 miles
from Charing Cross-a valuation of £105;
in Scotland, £90, and elsewhere, £78.
The
The right hon. Gentleman referred to
"elsewhere" as "in the provinces," and no
doubt that was strictly correct, but one
might perhaps remember that in England
the provinces include the city of
Birmingham with a population of over
one million, as well as little places like
Clovelly, in Devonshire. There is a
greater discrepancy in England than
exists in Northern Ireland. The valuation elsewhere was £78, not £75 as in
the subsequent legislation. That is 30s
a week, so that we have come down £3.
Taking that £75 valuation, it represents
a rental of about £120 a year, which is
a fairly high price to pay for a house.
This £120 a year house is certainly not
the class of house that was thought of
when rent restriction was introduced.
Some of us may have observed in the
newspapers the other day the view attributed to a gentleman of the name of
McAuley, who is connected with a very
well-known firm of valuers in Belfast.
His view was that £75 was too high a
figure, and that £50 or £60 would have
been ample. He thought that, perhaps,
there was some difference between valuations in England and valuations in
Northern Ireland. However, that was
his view, apparently. I do not like to
suggest that the House should be asked
to leave out Part II of the Bill. There
are reasons why some of it should be
included, though, perhaps, with some
amendment.
There are other difficulties, and one of
them, I am sorry to say, occurs to my
mind as I know it has occurred to individual members of the public. One difficulty is the positive animosity displayed.
throughout this Bill against owners of
property. It is all very well to say it
is merely keeping alive the animosity
which was exhibited in 1932, but the fact
remains that it is still there after eight
years.
Another difficulty is what I might
describe, perhaps, as the switch back to
the provisions of the Act of 1920, which
is twenty years ago and this is part
again of the animosity question—the incorporation in the Bill before us of certain
provisions we. introduced in 1932. Those
were provisions which followed on the
Crawford Report. You may remember
the Report on housing conditions of a
committee presided over by Mr. Crawford,
the then Member for the Mid-Antrim
Division in another place. That Report
drew public attention to some really
horrible and shocking instances of
oppression and, if not actual fraud,
certainly to instances of imposition. It
was in consequence of those revelations
that an attack, if I may put it that way,
was made in the Bill of 1932 on certain
methods of dealing with house property
which are not themselves immoral per se.
There is nothing necessarily immoral
in letting a house subject to an option to
a man to buy it if he wants to; nor is
there any objection in principle in allow-
Rent Restrictions Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
ing a man to buy a house by paying the
purchase money by instalments. The
objection taken to them then was that
methods of that sort were resorted to as
a cloak for getting outside the scope of
the Rent Restrictions Acts and obtaining
an exorbitant rent for the property in
question. They got the money but called.
it by a different name. That Parliament
decided to jump on, and they did so
rather heavily.
The House may remember, perhaps,
that I myself was much impressed at that
time by the great difficulty we were in of
separating the sheep from the goats. I
was averse to bringing in any measure
which, although it might very justifiably
operate against the goat, failed to protect
the sheep, and the House, at my instance,
passed a clause which may be in the
recollection of some of us, whereby it was
provided that if a man could come to
the court and establish the fact that his
transaction was a bona fide transaction,
and not one resorted to with a view to
obtaining a greater rent than he was
entitled to, he was given a certificate of
exemption from the penalties of the Act.
That clause is not reproduced in the
Bill before us, and it could not be,
because it was limited to twelve months
after the passing of that Act. But we
did put in some, protection, and we have
not done that on this occasion. We have
not even put in a provision that a
man, instead of repaying his deposit,
may, before he takes steps to set off
against that deposit any claims he may
have, pay that money into court, which
is a possible way of meeting the case.
That is almost the most serious objection
that there is to the Bill before us.
is the objection which is manifested by
Clause 19 to which I will come back in
a moment. Before I leave it I must say
that Clause 19 of this Bill has no counterpart in the English measure of 1939, nor,
so far as I know, in any other Statute
applicable to Great Britain.
That
If I am wrong I trust that I shall be.
corrected, but, according to the best of
my information and belief, there is
nothing like Clause 19 in operation in
Great Britain, and it has been introduced here and brought up, not afresh--
because it got through in 1932-but it
has been given not only a new lease of
life, but with a roaming commission to
cover higher valuations up to £75. That
is equivalent to £120 a year in rent.
I think, perhaps, I may as well
deal a little further with that Clause 19
at the moment. I shall, of course, at a
later stage ask the Committee to remove
it, because with that clause in the Bill is
impossible. Without that clause the Bill
might perhaps serve a useful purpose.
We have to remember, I think, that we
must deal with our difficulties in such a
way that we avoid working any injustice,
and also that we avoid allowing our Bill
to defeat our own object, which is to
ensure a sufficient supply of houses at
reasonable rents. If we introduce a
provision which discourages the letting
of houses-and I am thinking particularly
of furnished houses-we shall run the
risk of defeating our object. The danger
is not so much the danger of preventing
the building of new houses, because I
understand that house building is suspended throughout the Province, but
there is the danger of people who would
otherwise let houses refusing to let them,
and that is particularly so in the case of
furnished houses.
I propose to ask the Committee to expunge Clause 19 when the Bill is taken
in Committee on Thursday. I should
prefer a later date for the Committee
Stage, but before we take Clause 19 in
Committee I would respectfully ask hon.
Members to study the clause with care,
particularly perhaps sub-clauses (2) and
(3), and see for themselves what possibilities those sub-clauses present to the
tenant of roguish disposition. I think
they will see that those sub-clauses, carefully, shall I say, misapplied, would
enable a defaulting tenant to do great
damage to his landlord to get a higher
rate of interest on his money than he
would by depositing it at the bank, and,
finally, to "shoot the moon" and leave
the landlord to serve his writ on an
absent householder. If that clause is
carefully studied I think the House or
the Committee will agree that it cannot
be allowed to stay. I do say that these
difficulties are such as to require great
watchfulness on our part if we are to
attain our aim and avoid our desires
being defeated.
There are one or two other relatively
small matters which I think require

Major BOYLE: I presume the Government has brought in this Bill with the
idea of helping working-class housing? I
wonder if it is going to do that. Those
people who own working-class houses have
to keep them in repair with the hope of
getting some small return for their investment. By the extension of the present Rent Restrictions Acts for this
further period these small house owners
are going to have great difficulty in keeping the older class houses in repair. The
cost of the various materials necessary for
house repair has immensely increased,
and, I understand, is still increasing, and
in respect of most of this class of work
the landlord, I understand, is
entitled to any increase of rent because
they are not structural improvements.
The people who have these houses have
to maintain them, and improve them if
possible, out of income.
not
In the case of owners of house property let to the working classes in country
towns and villages, where perhaps the
average weekly rent is 4s a week, their
expenditure on these houses amounts to
from 25 per cent. to 30 per cent. of the
present income, which is a very big proportion. They have to pay for other
things as well. Property owners, as I know
them, endeavour to improve their property
and they require. a little incentive to do
So. If they have to pay three and four
times the normal amount for repairs their
rental is very heavily reduced, and I very
much fear that, from the point of view
of maintenance and repair, this village or
small town property, such as we have in
the city of Armagh, will deteriorate very
considerably and probably react against
the tenants.
The improvements that land owners do
in small houses is not very great, but they
are surely carried out for the benefit of
the occupiers. I am not aware that there
is any demand for this Bill where I come
from. There is certainly no great demand
for working-class houses. There are
several such houses empty in Armagh
and there is no great rush for them. I
am very much afraid that this proposed
extension of the rent restriction period
will not help the working classes.
As regards increasing the valuation to
£75, I made inquiries in Armagh and
I found that there is only one residential
house in the whole of Armagh valued at
£75. Any others are business premises.
It is a very fine house, occupied as a
Parochial House, where the Roman
Catholic clergy all live, and it is the only
house in Armagh with a valuation of
£75. There is no other house approaching that valuation. Therefore I should
have thought it was unnecessary to increase the valuation to such an extent.
It may be required in the city of Belfast,
but not so in the Province. I shall be
very much surprised if this further control and restriction is going to help the
housing question, and, personally, I am
not in favour of it.
Mr. ROBB: With regard to what the
last speaker has said, of course if there
is no demand for houses in any particular
locality the evil which this Bill is aiming
to prevent will not arise, and there will
be no rent control necessary. If there
is no demand for houses the rents will
not go up.
This Bill will function in
those places where there is a demand for
houses, and where owners take unfair
advantage of that demand. With reference to the animosity which Senator
Leslie said the Bill displayed against
house owners, I would point out that
there will be animosity against tenants
just as much as against landlords.
May I say that, as Senator Leslie has
pointed out, the theory upon which our
housing legislation has been founded is
this that increases of rent, and demands
for increases of rent, should be countered
Building Societies Bill 148
Rent Restrictions Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
by the provision of houses. That provision has been stopped by the war, and
it will continue to be stopped until some
time after the war. If by the provision
of houses we were endeavouring to prevent excessive, demands for rent, and the
provision of houses has stopped, then we
must revert to the control of rent. It is
a fact that the number of vacant houses
in Belfast has been diminishing, and the
demand for houses has increased. It is
quite true that we have not had any
evacuation of Belfast, but there has been
an influx into Belfast and certain other
parts of Northern Ireland, which has
created a demand for houses and has increased the scarcity of houses. As there
is no possibility of that scarcity being met
by new building during the war the
obvious course is to revert to the other
method of controlling rents, and that is
the reason for this Bill. Part II is
merely a consolidation of the existing
legislation.
As regards Clause 19, of which so much
has been heard, that Clause was deliberately inserted in the Act of 1932 by this
Parliament. It is part of the existing
rent restriction legislation, and if you
are extending to houses of £75 valuation
the existing legislation which Parliament
has seen fit to pass, then it must be incorporated in this Bill. Parliament decided
that it was a good thing to insert that
Clause in the Act of 1932 to deal with
certain matters that were brought to its
attention at that time. Those matters
might not arise again; no one can say
whether they will or not; but if they do
arise here is the clause which Parliament
has seen fit to enact to deal with them.
That is why Clause. 19 is in this Bill.
So far as the valuation of £75 is concerned, it would be inconvenient to have
a varying standard of valuation for
different parts of Northern Ireland, and
it was considered more convenient to
adopt the figure of £75, which was the
valuation in the. English provinces. It
is quite true that the provinces in
England include the city of Birmingham
with a million inhabitants, but the city
of Birmingham does not stand in any
different position compared with the
rest of England than does the city of
Belfast in relation to the rural part of
Northern Ireland. To fix a valuation of
£75 does not seem excessive in view of
what we experienced during the last war.
Those of us who lived in the city of
Belfast during the last war know the
extraordinary rents that were demanded
for houses. I personally know that on
the Malone Road there was a little unoccupied piece of ground on which a house.
was built. It could not have contained
more than two sitting-rooms and four
bedrooms, and a rent of £200 a year was
demanded and got for it. We do not
want that sort of thing to happen again.
Nobody knows how long the war will
last, and nobody knows whether these
evils will arise, but we want to have an
Act available to counter them if they do
arise.
On Question, Motion ("That the Bill
be now read a Second time ") agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House. for Thursday next.
Building Societies Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is not a war measure,
and I think we are to be grateful for
that. The introduction of a similar Bill
at Westminster was provoked by a case
that came before, the courts in England,
and the opportunity was taken to consolidate the law.
The recent British Act, in so far as
England and Scotland. are concerned,
made certain amendments in building
society law which had been found to be
necessary since the last legislation-in
1894 on this subject. The measure now
before this House is to make similar
amendments in Northern Ireland and
thus restore throughout the United
Kingdom general uniformity of legislation in regard to building societies.
Let me first remind the House that,
notwithstanding their name, building
societies do not undertake building operations. They lend money on mortgage to
people who desire to buy a house, but
who do not possess, or do not desire to
provide from their own resources, the
full amount of the purchase price. The
capital of a society is provided by the
issue of shares to members and by the
149 Building Societies Bill--

23 APRIL 1940
Second Reading
Buildacceptance of money on deposit.
ing societies have come to be recognised
as a popular medium of thrift.
It is, however, chiefly within the last
decade, when a number of cross-Channel
societies began to operate in this area,
that the rapid and widespread development of the movement in Northern Ireland has occurred. In 1922 the amount
outstanding on loans made by building
societies operating in Northern Ireland
was £253,000. By December, 1938, the
comparable figure was over 3 million
pounds roughly fourteen times greater.
The immediate cause of the British
Act of 1939 was a case in the courts
where the question was raised whether
building societies had legal power to
make any larger advances than they
would have made on the security of the
property only, or whether they could
advance a larger sum in consideration of
some additional security. The judge,
who heard the case in the first instance,
took the view that the societies had the
necessary power not only to lend money
on the security of property, but also to
lend additional money beyond what they
would have lent on the security of a
house if additional security were not
offered. I may say that the particular
case on which that point arose did go to
the Court of Appeal, but it was decided
there on a different point. In another
case the Court of Appeal in England has
held that building societies always had
power.
this
It is obvious that there can be no
objection to any lender obtaining collateral security for his loan and that,
in the case of
a person with small
means, there is an advantage in an
arrangement which enables a building
society to advance a high percentage of
the cost of the building. It was
alleged, however, in the Imperial Parliament, that one of the prejudicial effects
of the existing system, under which a
builder could enter into an arrangement
with a building society for the provision of collateral security against a
number of houses to be mortgaged with
the society by private individuals, was
that the prospective house-owner might
be led to believe that the house was of
a higher value than might prove to be
the case. The legislation now before
the
House is designed to provide
additional protection for buyers in this
connection.
In order that hon. Members may
appreciate the nature of these continuing
arrangements, or builder's pools, which
are a common means of providing
additional security, let me give a typical
example. A purchaser wishes to buy a
house priced at £1,000. The building
society, on its part, is prepared to
advance him £750 on the security of the
property alone. The would-be house
owner, however, has only £50 of ready
money, and the building society agrees
to advance to him a further £200 under
a guarantee by the builder. Usually
what happens is that the builder agrees
to pay into a pool a cash deposit covering the additional £200 which the society
is advancing.
The special feature of the arrangement is that the builder's guarantee to
the society is paid into a central pool
which provides security covering not
only one house, but a whole series of
houses, and the actual houses covered by
the arrangement may change from time.
to time as the loans on some are paid
off and as new houses are built.
Although this is a fluid arrangement, it
would, if worked in accordance with
safeguards such as those we propose in
this Bill, prove to the advantage of the
purchaser, who could not otherwise
afford to buy a house on the security of
the property alone.
Turning now to the detailed provisions
of the Bill itself, hon. Members will
observe that the measure is really in two
parts. While the position of societies is
activities
safeguarded, their
are also
regulated with a view to the protection
of members.
Clause 1 is declaratory in nature and
puts into legislative form the legal
position as enunciated by the courts in
the recent litigation.
Clause 2 limits the additional security
which may be taken by the society to
certain classes, and prescribes a limit of
75 per cent. of the purchase price as the
basic advance, with a maximum of 20
per cent.
the limit of the excess advance, where a society takes
additional security in respect of the
as
Building Societies Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr Robb]
advance to a member for the purpose of
defraying the purchase price.
Clause 6 requires the society to give
the member a notice in writing stating
the maximum amount which the society
would consider proper to advance upon
the security of the property if no other
security were taken by the society for
the advance; and also the amount, if
any, by which the advance exceeds this
maximum amount. The notice in question is required to be given before any
contract for the repayment of the advance is entered into. If the society
fails to do this, no sums are recoverable
in respect of the advance except by leave
of the court.
Clause 7 provides for the purchaser
receiving notice of any additional
security which would attach through the
existence of a contract between a society
and any person having a financial interest
in the estate. Failing such a notice the
purchaser may rescind the contract.
These two clauses will ensure that a
purchaser receives notice of any bargain.
or arrangement between a builder and
the society. He will also have notice of
the value which the society places upon
the property.
Hon. Members will observe that under
Clause 8 a society shall be deemed to
warrant that the purchase price is
reasonable, unless it gives to the member a notice in writing stating that the
making of an advance implies no such
warranty. It was found that builders
and some crooked societies did a deal and
fleeced the purchaser, and this clause
provides that the society guarantees
that the purchase money is a reasonable
price unless the society says it does not
give any such guarantee, in which case
the purchaser should be put on his
guard.
Clause 9 requires the mortgagee to
secure the best price possible when sellin the property, and Clause 10 prohibits
the payment of commissions by a society
or its officers.
Clause 11 places upon every director
or member of the committee of management of a society the obligation of
satisfying himself that the value of any
security taken will be assesed by a competent and prudent person. The
remaining clauses of the Bill are selfexplanatory.
The primary object of this Bill is to
make certain that all these transactions
are valid, and secondly to impose certain
restrictions on the activities of building
societies and their members, especially
their committees of management, in
favour of purchasers. I beg to move,
"That the Bill be now read a Second
time."
Mr. LESLIE: I am glad to recognise
that the Government really does propose
to do something, however indirect, to
help the housing shortage, and that it
does propose to do it on this ocasion by
doing something to make it more easy
to build more houses. That is the line
which presumably building societies will
take. It is rather different from trying
to increase housing accommodation by
controlling the rents payable for those
houses already in existence. I do not
know whether the Government's
repentance was very sudden; I am afraid
it was not, but there is a great difference
between the two methods. The one
taken now in this Bill is the right one.
The Bill is in some ways rather
similar in principle to the Hire Purchase
Bill which we parted with some weeks
ago. Very much the same principles of
equity are found in this measure. It is
a thoroughly common-sense Bill, and I
commend it to the House. There is only
one matter which I think will require
some attention on the Committee stage,
and it is the question of county court
jurisdiction. I think we must provide,
as we did in the Hire Purchase Bill,
for the direct bestowal of jurisdiction on
the county court, and we ought to consider whether the figure of £600 ought
to stand or whether we should raise it.
I do not suggest raising it as compensation for the omission from our Bill of
the provision in the English measure to
enable an Order in Council to be made
and the extent of the county court's
jurisdiction increased. However, I do
not think that will take us very long.
I imagine that this Bill will have a very
quick and unimpeded passage through
this House.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for to-morrow.

CLAUSE 1 (Continuance and Amendment of Rent Restrictions Acts).
Question proposed, "That the clause
stand part of the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: I should like to know
whether it is proposed to give the Committee any explanation of the difference
between this clause and the corresponding
English clause. In England control is
to cease six months after the end of the
present emergency. Here the suggestion
is twelve months. We were given to
understand, I think by some inadvertence, on Tuesday that this Bill was the
same as the English Bill. This is, to my
mind, rather a material difference, and I
wondered whether any explanation is to
be given to the Committee.
Mr. ROBB: The only explanation is
that the circumstances are not quite the
same, and the Government came to the
conclusion that twelve months was a
better margin to allow in this country
than six months. It is not a very material
matter one way or the other and, on the
whole, I think a period of twelve months
after the disturbance of this war is not
an unreasonable time to allow.
Mr. LESLIE: I am not myself inclined to split hairs over this matter, but
I take note with satisfaction of the admission that conditions in Northern Ireland
are not the same as those in Great
Britain, which is rather important.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill.

CLAUSE 3
(Dwelling-houses and
mortages to which Part II applies).
The following amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :
Clause 3, sub-clause (1), after paragraph (e) insert:
"(f) This Part of this Act shall not
apply to any dwelling-house situate
in a rural district and bona fide let,
or proposed to be let, at a rent which,
exclusive of any sum payable in
respect of rates, does not exceed three
shillings and sixpence a week."
Mr. LESLIE: This Amendment gave
me a certain amount of trouble, and I
am bound to confess that if it had not
been for paragraph (e) of this subclause I am not sure that I should have
brought this proposal forward. Paragraph (e), as the Committee will see,
relates to those dwelling houses provided
by local authorities under the Irish
Labourers Acts, and those are exclusively
in rural districts. It seems to me that
if you bring in those provided by a public
authority at the cost of the rates you
are bound to bring in any privately
owned cottages there may be to which
somewhat similar conditions apply.
There will be cottages let at not more
than 3s 6d a week which are under
control already, and, therefore, still come
under Part I of the Bill, but there are perhaps rather more that are not under con-
Rent Restrictions Bill—
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
trol, and perhaps never have been under
control. Some of those houses will be,
as a matter of fact, let at an uneconomic
rent at the moment, for various reasons,
not necessarily because the occupant is
employed by the owner, and there might
be some injustice done if such cottages
were brought under the scope of Part II.
As regards the rent of 3s 6d, I think
we may take it for granted that no Rent
Restrictions Bill would have been
brought in to deal with houses of a
maximum rent of 3s 6d a week, but I
was rather hoping we should hear the
views of hon. Members who live in other

apply in their neighbourhood, and
whether they think the owner of such a
cottage is already sufficiently protected
by that proviso in the definition of
standard rent which is to be found in
the Interpretation Clause, and also by
the proposal in the Bill about the
difference between rent and valuation.
A house let at 3s 6d a week means a
rental of £9 2s in the year, and would
probably represent, in the rural districts,
a valuation of between £5 and £6. I
am inclined to think we should insert the
words I have on the Paper, but I do
hope that the views of other rural districts will be laid before us.
Mr. ROBB: The Government could not
accept this Amendment at all. You are
going to except from rent control houses
in which the poorest people live. I do
not know how anyone is going to justify
that. You are cutting out houses let at
3s 6d and less.
Mr. LESLIE: But is not that house
controlled already under Part I?
an
Mr. ROBB: Then why touch it in this
part of the Bill? The analogy of the
labourer's cottage does not apply, because
labourers' cottages are not let at
economic rent at all. They are provided
by the local authorities for labourers, and
are dealt with under other Acts. To
insert the Amendment would mean that
protection would be taken away from the
poorest class of the community. I do not
think the Committee wants to do that.
The people who live in houses of this class
are the poorest section of the community,
and surely they are the people least able
to look after themselves, and, therefore,
most deserving of protection.
Mr. LESLIE: I must go back to what
I said before. If these people are so very
poor, and so on-and I do not agree
about the 3s 6d person in the country
being the very poorest-why has the
Government left them unprotected for so
long? Why did the Government allow
them to get out of Part I? Part II can
only apply to a house which is not caught
by Part I. The very beginning of this
Clause says that this part of the Act
shall not apply to any dwelling-house to
which the Rent and Mortgage Interest
(Restrictions) Act applies. Some of these
houses are already caught by Part I.
Others are in a different position, and I
want to keep these 3s 6d houses in their
existing position, because I think that
will be for the greater benefit of the
people who are likely to want to inhabit
them.
If you impose control on a house of that
class in the country, when the house falls
vacant it will remain vacant, because the
owner will not let it. That is a point on
which I lay stress. It is not in the

anybody from any other rural district,
and that puts me in great difficulty.
Apparently the Government has made up
its mind, and for any inconvenience that
may arise the Government must be prepared to be responsible.
Mr. ROBB: The Government is
perfectly prepared to bear the responsibility of protecting the poor people who
live in 3s 6d houses.

does not arise. I am rather afraid we

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
6, sub-clause (i), to leave out of paragraph (a) the words "(not including expenditure on decoration or repairs)."
Here, as the Committee presumably
recognises, I am up against a phrase
which occurs in the original Act of 1920.
I said the other day that I was going to
regard the Act of 1920 as the original
Rent Restrictions Act, although that is
not in reality the case. But the 1920
Act was the post-war Act on which our
subsequent proceedings were based. The
phrase "not including expenditure on
decoration or repairs" was in the 1920
Act, and I am going to suggest to the
Committee that you cannot assume that
conditions in 1940 are the same as they
were in 1920. I am also going to suggest
to the Committee that the tenant in this
case will have the protection given him.
by the proviso to this clause; that is to

carried out in his place that he does not
want he can say so and complain against
having to pay for them.
On the other hand, the landlord has to
carry out such repairs as are essential,
in spite of the fact that the cost of every

In fact, we are in 1940 at the beginning
of what is apparently going to be a great

Mr. ROBB: The Government cannot
accept this amendment. When a house
is let ordinarily in this country the landlord does the decoration and repairs, or
he may have an agreement that the
tenant should do them. Where he has
agreed to do the decorations and does
them it is part of his ordinary obligation
as landlord of this house, and he ought
not to be entitled to charge the cost, or
any part of it, against the tenant.
Bill says that that sort of thing should
continue. The landlord must do the
repairs if he is entitled to do them, but
where expenditure is incurred on
improvements then, that being something
the landlord has not contracted to do, it
is quite fair that he should be entitled
to get an increase in his rent of 8 per
cent. on his expenditure.
The

That is a very different thing from
allowing him to charge against the
tenant the cost of decorating and repairing the house, which he has originally
contracted to do. If the house
improved by a new bathroom, scullery or
sink, those are additions to the premises,
and it is only reasonable that the landlord should get an increase in rent of 8
per cent. of his expenditure on such
improvements, but he should not get any
increase in respect of something he has
originally contracted to do.
Major BOYLE: I would like to ask
the right hon. Gentleman if he can make
clear the difference between improvements and ordinary repair works. Considerable difficulty arises in contracts as
to what constitutes improvements. Take
a kitchen floor. In the country it may be
of mud, and if a tile floor is substituted
one has not been able, up to the present,
to claim it was an improvement, because
it was not a structural alteration. That
is one of the great difficulties of this Bill.
If the difference between improvements
and ordinary repairs could be clarified it
would help matters very much.
Landlords as I know them are very
anxious to improve their house property
even by making additions to them, such

he might have had if these improvements
could be included in this Clause. Up to

in getting anyone to believe they are
improvements.
Mr. ROBB: I would not like to stand

There are fat books on the subject, but

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause

I cannot help thinking that the particular point I am going to raise this afternoon is a point that escaped our attention
in 1932 when we were dealing with this
matter. When the Bill of 1932 was before us there was also before us that
Report to which I previously referred,
the Crawford Report, which dealt with
various evils particularly, but not entirely
165 Rent Restrictions Bill-

25 APRIL 1940
Committee
exclusively arising in Belfast. One of
the evils to which attention was called by
the Report of that Committee was the
evil resulting from sub-letting. Sub-letting was very much before the attention
of this House in considering the Bill, and
it was finally decided to accept a rather
long amendment dealing with sub-letting
which was proposed by the hon. and
learned Member who now represents, I
think, the Central Division of Belfast in
another place. We were anxious to deal
with sub-letting, and to some extent we
did, with a view to checking the evils
resulting from it.
Now we are faced with a proposal in
this Bill which is, as I need scarcely inform the Committee, a reproduction of a
sub-section of Section 5 of that 1920 Act
so often mentioned. But I have got to
remind the Committee again that this
is 1940, not 1920, and we are going to
Ideal with houses between £26 and £75
valuation. I am going to say that the
danger of sub-letting is even greater
when you get to the higher figure above
£26 than it is in the case of houses below
the figure of £26, because there is every
chance, one would think, of sub-letting a
house valued beyond £26, of its being
split up into tenements and sub-tenements
and so reproducing a kind of-I was
going to say slum, but that is not quite
the expression-but a kind of house slum
rather than street slum, which you run
the risk of bringing into existence.
We get over that if we give the landlord the right to resume possession if the
tenant, without his consent, at any time
after the commencement of this Act
assigns or sub-lets any part of the
dwelling-house; not, as it is in the Bill,
the whole of the dwelling-house or the
part which is not already sub-let and makes
up the whole of the dwelling-house. If
we make this alteration we are going a
step further to achieve the object of
the 1932 Act. This is particularly
a Belfast question rather than a country
question, but with the Crawford Report
in my mind I cannot but think this
Amendment is required. Therefore I
beg to move.
Mr. ROBB: I do not think I can
accept this Amendment.
This part of
the Bill applies to a house or part of a
house which is let as a separate dwellinghouse.. If you have a whole house and
part of it is sub-let as a dwelling-house
you get rent control applied to that part.
This particular part of the Clause deals
with the right of the landlord to recover
possession of his house. Under his
agreement of tenancy the tenant either
has or has not the right to sub-let. If
he is forbidden by his agreement to sublet then under the ordinary law the landlord can resume possession. If he has the
right to sub-let, then as between him and
his landlord there is no reason why he
should not sub-let, unless he has already
sub-let one part and then another part,
so that his interest in the house has gone
altogether.
The Clause says that if a man has sublet the whole of his house his rights under
this Bill are gone, and the landlord can
resume possession. If he has sub-let first
one part legitimately and then goes on
and sub-lets the rest of the house, so that
the tenant's interest in the house is
nothing but a profit rent, then the Bill
is not intended to protect that tenant.
The Bill is not intended to protect tenants
who make a profit out of their tenancies;
it is intended to protect them in the
occupation of their premises, and that is
what this Clause does. The landlord can
get possession if the tenant, without the
landlord's consent, assigns or sub-lets the
whole of the house. I think that is quite
right, and the Government cannot accept
the. Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: I am in agreement with the
paragraph as it stands for the reasons the
right hon. Gentleman has given, but my
complaint is that it does not go far
enough. It does not give protection
against those abuses which were. brought
to notice in the Crawford Report on subletting. My theory is that the landlord
should have the right to recover if any
part of the premises are sub-let without
his consent.
Mr. ROBB: Even though in the
original agreement the tenant is not forbidden to sub-let?

Mr. ROBB: I do not see why he

CLAUSE 16 (Penalty for excessive
charges for furnished lettings).
Where any person after the commencement of this Act lets any
dwelling-house to which this Part of
this Act applies or any part of such
dwelling-house at a rent which includes
payment in respect of the use of
furniture, and the rent charged yields
to the landlord a profit which, having
regard to all the circumstances of the
case, is extortionate, then, without.
prejudice to any other remedy under
this Part of this Act, the landlord shall
be liable on summary conviction to a
fine not exceeding one hundred pounds,
and the court by which he is convicted
may order that the rent so far as it
exceeds the amount permitted by the.
last preceding section of this Act shall
be irrecoverable, and that the amount
of any such excess shall be repaid to
the tenant, but any such order shall
be in lieu of any other method of
recovery prescribed by this Part of
this Act.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 16, to leave out from the word
"which" in line 33 to the word "then"
in line 34 (having regard to all the
circumstances of the casc, is extortionate).
This is a milder question. The expression "extortionate" as used here of
course, occurs in that not exactly blessed
Act of 1920. It does occur there, but
in somewhat different surroundings. The
expression in 1920 was "regard to all the
circumstances of the case, including the
margin of profit allowed by the last preceding sub-section is extortionate," and
so on. In this Bill the expression "last
preceding sub-section" has been left out,
it being thought that "all the circumstances of the case" included that also.
The question of whether rent is or is not
extortionate is rather an abstract question.
Whether it is profit allowed by the last
preceding sub-section is a matter of fact,
and as these matters will be dealt with
by resident magistrates it is desirable to
resident
keep
magistrates
fact,
especially having regard to the fact that,
as the Committee will see from Clause 25,
a county court shall have power to determine summarily any questions as to the
amount of rent or standard rent.
to
The power there of deciding what is
and what is not extortionate is given,
very properly, to the county court. If
we leave the words as they stand in the
text the magistrate first of all has got
to decide what is or is not extortionate,
which is not a question for him at all,
and then proceed to conviction and
penalty. We would get over that difficulty if we got rid of the word
"extortionate" and substituted the
words on the Paper, which is simply a
question of fact. I have got to admit
that even that might be a matter for the
county court, but one can say that in
either case a man brought before
magistrate would have an appeal to the
county court, and I think it would be
more simple that his appeal should be
against the question that the normal
profit has been exceeded rather than
the abstract question that the rent is
extortionate, because on the question of
extortion a lead is required to be given.
to the court.
a
Mr. ROBB: I think that this Clause
and the Clause preceding it are right.
Clause 15 says in short that where any
person lets a house and it is proved that
it yields the landlord a profit in excess
of the normal profit then the surplus is
to be recovered. That deals with a
simple case where a man has charged
something beyond the normal profit.
Clause 16 wants to deal with something
more than that. It wants to deal with
a case where the excess is so great that
it is really extortionate. That is the
difference between the two Clauses. It
is true that we do not define
"extortionate", and different people
might take a different view as to what
is extortionate, but we can only hope
that the court will evolve some rule on
the subject.

CLAUSE 19 (Provisions for preventing evasions of Part II).
on
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, to leave
out Clause 19. This is an amendment
dealing with a Clause which is of some
length. I mentioned this Clause
Second Reading. I invited hon. Members to read it carefully for themselves
and see what could happen under it.
This Clause was subject to a saving,
which I think I mentioned the other day,
enacted in 1932 as a direct consequence
of the revelations of the Crawford Report.
Cases were brought to light where the
grossest impositions had been attempted,
usually with success, on people living in
houses below £26 valuation. This afternoon we are dealing with houses between
£26 and £75 valuation, and incidentally
I am going to suggest that people who
take houses on that scale are not unlikely
to be in a position to defend themselves.
Some of them even consider it a proper use
of any savings they have to put them to
the employment of a lawyer to keep them
right in their agreement.
It is not unreasonable to expect a man
taking a house of a valuation between
£26 and £75 to consult his legal adviser
to see that he is properly protected. This
clause appears to be drawn on the assumption that the owner of the house is invariably a rogue and the tenant invariably an honest man. I am afraid
experience suggests that you may have
bad and good landlords.
Mr. ROBB: Six of one and half-adozen of the other.
Mr. LESLIE: If you have tenants in
quantity amongst them you are bound to
have one or two bad ones, and I am not
sure that people owning house property
in Belfast of the lower valuation have
any great reason to congratulate themselves on some of their tenants. Some
tenants seem to think it justifiable to
take any means of escaping the payment
of rent, and other tenants, when they
are short of coal, may think it quite
reasonable to use the bannisters and stairs
for fuel. I am not sure that you will not
find in Belfast tenants as expert in those
things as in any other town. The right
hon. Gentleman shakes his head.
Mr. ROBB: No, I agree.
Mr. LESLIE: What does this clause
really do? It was really aimed at the
purchase of houses by instalments, or
under an option. Neither of those things
is necessarily wrong. There is no
reason why a man should not take a house
on a term of even fourteen years with the
option of purchasing it, nor is there any
reason why a man should not take a
house and pay for it by instalments with
or without interest. What we did want
in the other Act to ensure was that it
should be an honest and bona-fide transaction, and that those methods of option
and instalment should not be made the
cloak for extracting for a limited period
a rent calculated at a rate in excess of
that which Parliament provided.
Now we are shifting to a much larger
scale, and I think we are departing
entirely from the English measure, which
has nothing of this nature. I asked on
Tuesday for correction if I was wrong in
saying that there was nothing of this
kind in England.
Mr. ROBB: There is nothing.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think the
Crawford Committee evils are likely to
arise in this larger house.
Mr. ROBB: May I interrupt the hon.
Member? There will be a great many

rapacity on the part of certain landlords
in Belfast it is rather a confession of
failure. The amount of the purchase
money has to be stated in the agreement,
and there is a limitation on the interest.
That is harmless enough. But if the
option is not exercised within twelve
months the owner is to pay back the
deposit with interest at the rate of 5 per
cent. I do not know why the wretched
owner has to pay 5 per cent, when all
the man could have got elsewhere is only
about 3 per cent. The owner is
not to be entitled to set off against
the deposit he has in hand any sums
due to him in respect of interest or
any other payments. He is to be permitted a right of action for recovery
of any rent up to an amount not exceeding the arrears in respect of six months'
use and occupation, and he must get a
receipt for the return of the deposit. I
cannot imagine a Belfast landlord not
getting a receipt, but this is probably
meant to apply to the landlord in the
country.
You have got to assume your tenant is
remaining to be served with the landlord's writ. He has got his money back,
and he is not using that money to take a
ticket to America or some other place.
He is waiting to be served with a writ
by the landlord. I do not think that is
likely to happen. With regard to instalments, I will come to the end of that
sub-clause, which says:
no action shall lie for use and occupation of the dwelling-house between the
date of the making of the agreement
and the termination thereof.
Your tenant being, I won't say a typical
Belfast tenant, but having got his agreement determined because he has used the
bannisters and the stairs for other
purposes, has got his deposit and instalments paid back to him. He has got all
that money, and the owner is not to set
off any
claim against those moneys.
Finally the tenant, if he is still there, is
not to be liable to an action for the, use
and occupation of the dwelling-house
between the date of the making of the
agreement and the termination thereof.
A man might occupy a house for eleven
months and depart without paying a
penny and having damaged the house
irreparably. This was put in the 1932
Act, not with my goodwill, but the
circumstances were not the same, and it
was not applied to a house of such high
valuation.
As regards the new houses the right
hon. Gentleman spoke of, I imagine that
none of those are covered by Part I of
the Bill. I really cannot for myself
agree to the retention of this Clause in
the Bill, and I hope the Committee will
agree. It will not do us any good, and
it offers an opportunity for perpetrating
considerable. injustices.
Mr. ROBB: This Clause was put in the
1932 Act and has worked fairly well,
although there has not been much
occasion for it to be exercised. It was
originally inserted to meet certain evils
discovered by a Committee of Inquiry
set up by Parliament. Its provisions
sound pretty drastic the way the hon.
and learned Member read it, but in
reality it is not as bad as he has made
it look. Take the first matter-the
agreement with option to purchase. This
Clause deals only with faked option
agreements. If the agreement is reasonable nothing happens, but where a man
is put in and sums of money are paid
and a pretence of an option is given in
order to get over the provisions of the
Act, that is the man who is hit by the
first part of this Clause, and it is a
fairly effective way of doing it.
With regard to purchase by instalments, the Clause does not deal with the
ordinary agreement for sale by way of
instalment. It deals with cases where
you have an agreement to sell a house
by instalment, but the tenant is not to
have any
interest whatever in the
property until the full amount is paid.
That is a dodge to escape the Rent
Restrictions Act. In the case of a man
who ordinarily buys a house with the
purchase price payable by instalments
177 Rent Restrictions Bill-
Committee
25 APRIL 1940

as soon as he has got his agreement he
has an interest in the house which he
transfer to someone else. This
can
Clause does not deal with such cases; it
deals only with fake agreements where
the tenant is permitted to occupy but
does not get any legal interest in or title
to the house. It is simply a dodge to get
round the Rent Restrictions Act. It is
this fake which is at the root of the evil
this Clause is designed to eradicate. I
do not think it is unreasonable. The
Crawford Committee felt very strongly
on what was happening at that time.
The same thing may happen again, and
I represent the Government in thinking
that it is not wise that we should weaken
the safeguards that were found necessary in 1932. We do not know exactly
what the new situation will produce,
and we should not blunt any weapons
in the hands of the Government to deal
with such evils.
Government
Mr. LESLIE: If the
had meant it only to apply to fake

attention to the provision here and the
provision in Great Britain.
The provision in Great Britain is not only £600,

the possibility of an Order in Council
being made increasing the amount over
which the county court may exercise
jurisdiction.
That proviso does not appear in our
Bill, and therefore the question seems to
me to arise whether we should regard
ourselves as stuck definitely to that £600,

question I move £800, which I think will
be likely to be sufficient. At all events I
am afraid the Committee will have to

expression, is rather a tall order for a
county court judge. £600 would seem
to be enough, but there is the other question of the proviso in the British Bill. If

ORDERS OF THE DAY.
Rent and Mortgage Interest (Restriction) Bill.
Order for Third Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I propose to move an
Amendment to the Motion of the right
hon. Gentleman. It is to leave out all
the words after the word "Bill" and
insert "be referred back to the Committee
for the further consideration of Clause
19 and for making better provision in
Clause 31, which deals with the commencement of the operation of this Act.'
""
I ought, I think, in support of this
Amendment, first to tell the House what
I believe would be the immediate consequence of the acceptance of the Motion.
Secondly, I thought perhaps you would
allow me to indicate, of course without
commentary, the alteration I should suggest in Clause 19 and Clause 31. Taking
first, the. important matter of what would.
be the effect of the acceptance of this.
Motion, I may be forgiven for reminding
the House that the Bill is divided into
two parts. Part I only proposes to continue the operation of the Rent Restriction Acts, 1932-1936, for a further period.
So far as that part goes, I say it is simply
a Measure for continuing the operation
of an Act or set of Acts of Parliament
which would otherwise expire.
Now it is provided in a Statute of his
late. Majesty, King George the Third,
passed in 1808-I remember the Statute,
but had to refresh my memory as to the
date, the point being whether it passed
before or after the Union of Ireland with
Great Britain-that where a Bill for continuing the operation of an Act of Parliament is in Parliament and providing that
the Measure continuing the operation of
that Act is passed during the same
session of Parliament, the actual date of
expiry of the first mentioned Act does
not matter. That is to say, if this Motion
is accepted and we pass the Bill on, say,
14th or 15th May, the fact that the Rent
Restriction Act of 1932 onwards would
expire on 1st May this year does not
matter. The Acts would not expire. The
position would remain the same. Consequently I submit we have ample time to
consider the matter at leisure, and there
is no occasion for the unseemly haste to
which the House was subjected earlier
in the week.
The second point is as regards the
alterations I suggest we should make. On
Clause 19, as the House may have heard
from the Committee, the question was
put that the clause be left out, but the
Committee decided the voting being 5
to 3-it should not be omitted. The
details of the clause, however, were not
considered naturally enough. I propose
to give the Committee another opportunity of considering these details, and,
of course, I must limit myself very
strictly to the details which are, it seems,
open to consideration without
without doing
violence to the decision already taken
that the clause, as a whole, should not be
cut out of the Bill.
Rent Restrictions Bill-
Third Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
There are three points. First of all, I
think the 5 per cent. interest ought to be
reduced to 2 per cent. Secondly, we
should add a third sub-clause to the
effect that the requirements as regards
repayment-it may be deposits or instalments or one thing or another-these
requirements should be deemed to be
satisfied if the owner instead of paying
them over to the tenant pays them into
court to abide the result of the action.
That is a simple matter. Thirdly, I think
we must leave out what is paragraph (g)
of sub-clause (3), which provides that a
man should have no action for the occupation of the house during the period from
the signature to the termination of the
agreement. That is what I suggest as
regards Clause 19.
As regards Clause 31, which is the
commencement clause, I think all that is
necessary is to insert the words "shall be
deemed to have come into operation on
the first of May." That will bring
Part II of the Act into operation as from
the first of May. The whole object of
the amendment is to obtain more time
for the consideration of what seems to
me a very important clause. I consider
that can be done without inconvenience
to anybody. It will not be necessary for
those in another place to sit on Monday
or any other day or anything else. There
is plenty of time for everybody.
Question proposed, "That the words.
proposed to be left out stand part of
the question."
Mr. ROBB: Of course I cannot agree
to this. This clause was discussed at
considerable length, and each of these
points was mentioned yesterday by the
hon. and learned Member. Now he
wants reconsideration of the whole
thing. If he wanted to put down amendments there was no objection to his doing
so, but he took the bull by the horns and
suggested throwing the whole thing out.
He was beaten, and wants to go back on
the thing. We cannot submit to that.
