possibility of agreement with the right
hon. Gentleman. Two points form the
main reason why the amendment was put
down in that form, but the whole clause
was objectionable. As to being beaten I
was defeated in a House nominally of 26
Members and the defeat was administered by 5 votes to 3. I cannot help
thinking people outside would think an
opportunity for reconsideration would not
be out of place. I cannot withdraw the
amendment.

Prevention of Fraud (Investments) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: The one object of this
Bill is to afford some further protection
to the public against the activities of
unscrupulous share-pushers. The Bill
189 Prevention of Fraud Bill-
26 APRIL 1940

Second Reading
follows the provisions of an Act, passed
last year by the Imperial Parliament,
which was based upon the recommendations of a committee presided over by
Sir Archibald Bodkin.
Within our recollection there have
been huge frauds upon the public by
financiers. It is true that they have not
occurred in Northern Ireland, but, since
our contact with Great Britain is so
close, it is necessary to take here also,
as well as across the water, measures of
a preventive nature.
The plundering of the public by misrepresentation of the value of shares, or
by dishonest forecasts of their
prospective value is, unfortunately, by
no means unfamiliar. Three years ago
leaflets warning the public against
share-pushing were issued by the million
through banks, savings banks and other
institutions, and it cannot be too
strongly stressed that investors should
consult with their bank managers, their
legal advisers or stockbrokers before
buying shares which are not of a high
and established reputation.
The tragedy of the situation is that
the victims of this form of exploitation
are often not only ignorant of financial
matters but also, as a rule, in no position
to afford to lose their hard-earned
savings and, no doubt, it is precisely
to the man or woman of limited means
that the inducements held out by the
share-pushers prove most tempting.
The method proposed in the Bill for
the prevention of this type of fraud
includes, first of all, a system of licensing of dealers in securities. Licenses will
be granted under Ministerial authority,
and before they are granted a deposit of
£500 will usually be required. It will
be an offence punishable by heavy penalties for a person to carry on the business
of dealing in securities unless he holds
such a licence. In order to make provision for the overwhelming majority of
honest dealers in securities there will be
exemption from this requirement in
favour of certain classes of persons-for
example, members of recognised stock
exchanges, municipal corporations, industrial and provident societies, building
societies, and persons acting in the
capacity of manager or trustee under
authorised unit trust schemes.
Certain transactions will also be
regarded as outside the scope of the restrictions imposed by this section of the
Bill, such as the issuing of a prospectus
which complies with the requirements of
the Companies Act.
and
The Bill also contemplates imposing
limitations upon the registration of industrial and provident societies, and
giving wider powers of control
supervision to the Registrar of Friendly
Societies. The industrial and provident
society has been termed the poor man's
company, but, nevertheless, it has been
used elsewhere to carry on business
which would more fittingly be undertaken by companies registered under the
Companies Act. These difficulties have
not arisen very much in our area, but
these provisions having been enacted in
England it is desirable to put similar
provisions in force here so that this area
may not be made a refuge for people
wishing to do this class of business.
The Bill also follows the British Act
by imposing restraint on any building
society whose practices appear to the
registrar likely to give cause for serious
complaint. Subject to the approval of
the Ministry, the registrar will be able
to take action to protect the interests of
such a society's investors and depositors
by suspending the activities of the
society.
The Bill also contains general provisions directed towards the prevention
of fraud. Clause 12 prescribes the
penalty of penal servitude for anybody
who induces a person to part with his
money under the conditions of misrepresentation which are defined in the
Clause. Clause 13 places certain
strictions upon the distribution
circulars relating to investments.
reof
I may say that in my own experience
the distribution of these circulars has in
recent years diminished in our area. I
do not know what is happening in
England. There was a time when the
receipt of circulars of this sort was very
frequent in Northern Ireland, but they
seem to have decreased, or perhaps I am
not now considered a fish likely to bite.
But although we may have been somewhat immune from such circulars, it is a
good thing to have the law here the same
as it is in England.
191 Prevention of Fraud Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr Robb]
The operation of the provisions as to
the licensing of dealers is made subject to
the determination by the Ministry of an
appointed day, and in view of the present
conditions it is not intended to bring
these Clauses into force immediately.
Clauses 10, 11, 12 and 13, concerning
industrial and provident societies and
building societies, and containing general
provisions for preventing fraud, will,
however, come into force as soon as the
Bill becomes law.
We hope the Bill will be successful in
its objects. Whether it will or not remains to be seen. The ingenuity of
people in trying to drive a coach and four
through Acts of Parliament is well-known,
but this Measure represents an honest
effort to do what we can to put a stop
to this evil. I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think that the
right hon. Gentleman need anticipate any
difficulty in this House with the Bill
before us. He almost made me flatter
myself that he had been reading a speech
of mine delivered in this House, though
not in this building, so long ago as 1923.
I spoke on the desirability of passing here
an Act similar to one passed in England.
The particular Bill was one which dealt
with criminal law amendment in the
special sense of the. term, and this is really
dealing with what might be criminal law
in a less special sense.
I
The right hon. Gentleman spoke about
the comparative immunity from this
shower of circulars in recent years.
rather think that, perhaps, that is partly
attributable to some, shall we say misfortune which befell an enterprising firm
which operated in the town of Leicester.
I will not give the name the right hon.
Gentleman probably remembers it. The
passage of those gentlemen to the hospitality of His Majesty did have a very
considerable effect, I think.
As regards the Bill there are only three
remarks I want to make, and two of
them are joined together. It will be
noticed that we do not prescribe in the
Bill any measure of the fees to be charged
for registration. It will also be noticed
that we have not any definite provision
for what I might call an interchange of
information between Great Britain and
ourselves. What I am thinking of is
something similar to what happens in
connection with racing. If a man is
warned off Newmarket Heath the corresponding Jockey Club in France takes
similar action. We have no provision for
that, and I imagine the intention is that
it should be dealt with on a reciprocal
basis. Although we know the manner in
which we will deal with the information
we receive from Great Britain, we cannot
provide for how Great Britain will deal
with the information received from us.
We must rely on cordial co-operation between the relative departments, which
are, I think, the Ministry of Commerce
here and presumably the Board of Trade
in England. I do not think there will
be any difficulty about that.
I should like to say a word about the
appeal tribunal provided for in the Bill,
the tribunal which is to re-examine any
refusal by the Ministry to register or
renew a licence. I should like to point
out to hon. Members, who have no doubt
compared this Bill with the English Act,
that the method we have adopted in
providing for an appeal tribunal is infinitely superior and very much simpler
than that existing in Great Britain.
Great Britain requires a much more complicated arrangement and one which, I
think, would not be at all suitable for
the inhabitants of the Six Counties. For
myself I propose to support the Bill and
although I cannot say that I have gone
through the Bill with a small comb, I do
not think it at all likely that I shall have
anything to trouble the House with when
the Bill comes before us in Committee at
a later stage.
Mr. ROBB: If the hon. Member will
look at Clause 3, sub-clause (1),
paragraph (b) he will see that the fee
for a licence is to be prescribed. We do
not put it in the Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: Yes, it will be prescribed. We do not do it.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

CLAUSE 1 (Licensing of dealers in
securities).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the. Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: A question seems to me
to arise on this Clause, particularly on
sub-clauses (2) and (3). It is a question.
which occurs frequently throughout the
Bill and, therefore, I thought I might as
well mention it now. If any amendment
is found to be necessary it will probably
be made in Clause 17, a general Clause
dealing with offences under the Bill.
Therefore I am proposing to put down an
Amendment for consideration after the
Third Reading, the point being one that
can very well be dealt with in that
manner, as it is in no sense controversial.
The question is, ought we or ought we
not definitely to provide that any indictments which may arise under this Act
should not be heard by a Court of
Quarter Session. My own opinion is that
the intention is that any indictments preferred should be preferred to a Court of
Assize presided over by a judge of the
High Court. In England they have a
Quarter Sessions Act, the date of which
I think is 1842, and we have no such
corresponding measure in this country.
The Committee will see, if they look
at various other Clauses in the Bill, for
instance Clause 10, sub-clause (6), which
provides for certain matters being dealt
with by a court of summary jurisdiction;
Clause 12, under which it is possible to
award penal servitude-I do not mean to
say that a Court of Quarter Session cannot do that too-Clause 13, sub-clause
(10), which gives the court power to order
the disposal of documents and so on;
and in Clause 6 there is a definite
reference to the High Court. I think the
intention is that those serious matters
should be dealt with by the High Court,
but the Bill does not make any definite
provision. I suggest that at a later stage
we should consider whether we should not
make that definite provision.
The Commitee will remember that not
only have we no Quarter Sessions Act in
Northern Ireland, but we have had no
Quarter Session grand jury. In England
in that respect a benighted countrythey have no grand jury at all, either at
Assizes or Quarter Sessions, and questions.
might arise here which would be better
197 Prevention of Fraud Bill

30 APRIL 1940
Adjournment
dealt with by a judge of the High Court.
Accordingly, after Third Reading, I
propose to move an amendment to
Clause 17, and in the meantime the
question can be considered.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
17, after sub-clause (2), to insert as a
new sub-clause :
"(3) A court of quarter sessions
shall not have. jurisdiction to try an
indictment for any offence against this
Act."
I mentioned this somewhat academic
matter at an earlier stage. It occurred
.
to me that it would be desirable that
Parliament in this instance should take
the responsibility of saying whether or
not any offences required to be dealt with
by indictment under this Act should be
dealt with by quarter sessions or not. As
the House knows, there are means
whereby the Attorney General can
arrange whether a matter goes to
quarter sessions or to assizes, and the
House also knows that in England since
the Quarter Sessions Act, 1842, a provision of this sort has been inserted in
I do not mean only
sundry statutes.
such statutes as the Forgery and Perjury
Act, but in Acts of Parliament found
about the later seventies.
Of course, there is some advantage
about hearing these matters at assizes.
The judge has the assistance of counsel,
a matter which at quarter sessions, in
Ireland at all events, is not always the
case. There is also an advantage in dealing with corporations. It is, as I say, an
academic question. In fact, I do not
think since this Parliament has been in
existence we have ever done this. It has
been done in England, and I think it
ought to be done in Northern Ireland.
For that reason I put the Amendment on
the Paper, and I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That these words.
be there inserted."
Mr. ROBB. I appreciate what the hon.
and learned Senator has said, but I do
not think it is necessary in Northern Ireland to make any such provision as this.
After all under this Bill there are many
offences triable at petty sessions and also
triable by indictment, and I cannot see
any real reason here why one should
necessarily skip quarter sessions. The
constitution of quarter sessions in Ireland is different. We have a county
court judge always as president of that
court. And there is a provision whereby,
as the hon. and learned Senator has said,
any case of real importance can be
directed by the Attorney-General to stand
over to be tried at the assizes. I do not
think this provision should go in, and I
do not wish to accept the Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: I very much doubt
whether the matter is of any great consequence, and I certainly do not desire

CLAUSE 1 (Application of Act).
*

(c) In any other case, the sum of two

Commons Amendment:
*
Page 1, line 14, leave out "two" and

""
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, That the House do agree
with the Commons in the said Amendment." Hon. Members will remember
that when this Bill originally came before
this House the sum of £100 was fixed as
the limit in paragraph (c) of Clause 1.
This House in Committee altered that
sum of £100 to £200, with the idea that
it would be better to extend the protection of the Bill to a larger transaction
than would be covered by £100. Since
that time the Bill has been before the
Commons, and that House restored the
sum of £100.
It should be remembered that this Bill
is a copy of the Measure that has been
passed and is now an Act in Great
Britain. The. figure of £100 which was
inserted in this place in the English Act
was in fact a compromise figure. When
the Bill was before the Standing Committee in England there were those who
wanted the. sum fixed at £50 and others
who wanted it to be £200, and as a compromise £100 was fixed. The theory of
that figure is that the Bill is to protect
the poorer people, who may be assumed
to be not so well able to look after themselves as richer people, and the figure of
£100 was fixed as being a reasonable
limit for the protection of the class of
people the. Bill was intended to protect.
On that ground I ask the House to agree
to the restoration of the original figure of
£100. That will be sufficient, we hope,
to protect those who are not able to look
after themselves.
It will also have the great advantage
of making the law uniform throughout
the United Kingdom. It has often been
pointed out to me that when you get
beyond the figure of £100 you come into
a class of transaction that the Bill is
really not intended to cover. I am told
that the electrical equipment supplied to
many garages is in many cases paid for
by instalments on the hire-purchase
system, and to bring that class of business
which would normally be between £100
and £200 under this Bill would be extending the scope of the Measure to a
class of transaction for which it was not
originally intended protection should be
given.
It might also be true, perhaps, to say
that the other House is possibly more in
touch with the people who are concerned
in the protection to be given by this Bill
than is this House. On the various
grounds I have mentioned that the
figure of £100 was originally a compromise figure; that it is a great advantage
to have the law uniform in both countries;
and that £100, even at the increased
prices of to-day, gives sufficient protection
to the poorer classes of the community
it is sought to protect-I ask the House
to agree to the amendment.

Major BOYLE: I am glad that we
have at last received some explanation
of why this sum was originally fixed at
£100. Hon. Members will remember
that when this Bill was before this House
in Committee no reason whatever was
given by the Leader of the House. He
told us he was not prejudiced one way or
the other, and would be satisfied by whatever the House did. It appeared to us
that the limited amount of £100 only
shortened the circle, and that by increasing it to £200 it would enable more
people of the working classes to be protected. Now, apparently, it is not
thought necessary to protect these people.
I myself am not convinced they do not
require protection. I do not agree that
it is only the poorer classes who require
the protection of this Bill, but apparently
that is considered to be the case.
sonally I am not convinced on that point.

be remembered, to extend
extend the Bill
to what I might describe, as I
did describe before, as the blackcoated worker; that is
your shop assistant or bank clerk and
people of that class who might very well
desire to furnish a house at an expense
something greater than £100. It was for
that reason, I think, that this House,
after division, decided to raise the

to say,
Whatever figure may be decided upon
ultimately, the fact remains that the

our Statute

Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
House do agree with the Commons in the
said amendment." The House will
Mr. T. McLaughlin
remember that when this Bill was before
it in Committee certain amendments were
made in the Schedule. They did not
touch the essentials of the matters in
question, but were only a re-arrangement
of phrases. The provision in the Bill is
that the agreement which is to be
embodied in a memorandum is to contain
a notice which is in the terms prescribed
in the Schedule to the Bill. It will be
noticed that the Bill does not say that
the memorandum is to contain similar
provisions to those in the Schedule, but
that it is to have embodied in it a copy
of the Schedule.
When I was discussing the matter before I thought all that was necessary was
that there should be embodied in the
memorandum substantially the provisions
that were in the Schedule, and it seemed
to me that it did not make any great
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Commons Amendments
21 MAY 1940
difference to the Bill, and that the
language was improved by the changes
which this House made.
Mr. LESLIE: But this House did not
alter the Schedule at all.
Mr. ROBB: You are quite right. I
made a slip. The Bill prescribes that the
exact terms in the Schedule shall be put
into the Act. It turns out that the hirepurchase trade is carried on to a great
extent by firms who operate both in
England and Northern Ireland, and that
after the passing of the English Act those
firms supplied themselves with large
stocks of forms conforming to the English.
Act, and in which the words that are in
the English Schedule are incorporated.
When they saw the Northern Ireland Bill
as it had passed through this House they
discovered that the terms were not
identical, and that in order to conduct
their business here it would be necessary
for them to print a large supply of forms.
They thought that was quite unnecessary.
the
It is not a matter in which any principle is involved; it is merely a matter
of convenience. At this particular time,
when printing is expensive and paper
difficult to get, it is perhaps reasonable
for these firms to request that
Schedule to the Northern Ireland Bill
should be the same as the Schedule in
the English Act. This House was not
willing to do that before, and I
willingly admit that the words in
the Schedule at present are an improvement on the words in the
English Schedule. However, the Amendment will not make any difference
in substance but only in form. Since
the Bill was before the Senate it has
been before the Commons and that
House was willing to consent to the
request of these large traders that
the words in the English Schedule
should be incorporated verbatim in our
Schedule. Purely on account of convenience and not on any ground of
principle I ask the House to agree to
the Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: On this occasion,
though with great regret, I am quite
unable to agree to the Amendment. I
have studied the proposed alteration with
a good deal of care-I think with
perhaps more care than the matter
deserved-but I cannot bring myself to
say that this is an occasion on which we
ought to concur. I think that those who
sit elsewhere must be encouraged to
think again. The proper form, I
believe, is for a Motion to be made in
another place to the effect that that
House does not insist on this Amendment, and, if it is thought fit, to appoint
a committee to confer with a committee
to be appointed by this House to discuss
the point of difference. That seems
rather like producing a sledge hammer
to crack a very small nut, because there
is no point of principle as regards the
words themselves, but there is a point of
principle to which I will direct attention
in a moment.
The right hon. Gentleman reminded
us that the proposal to make this alteration was made here and was withdrawn
without coming to the decision of the
House. But I do not know, and I have
been unable to ascertain, that the objections taken in this House were brought
to the notice of those who sit in another
place. I do not think they were.
According to such information as we
can get as to what happened elsewhere
the alteration was made without any
debate whatever. I think everybody
knows the history of this Bill. It was a
Private Member's Bill brought in at
Westminster, and it was brought in by
a lady. I do not know how much that
honourable lady did herself of the
drafting of the Bill, but I should be
inclined to suggest that this particular
paragraph was the product of a feminine
hand, because I do not think one
of the expressions would have been
used by a mere man, however inexpert a
draftsman he might be. The Bill underwent examination in Committee, and also
it received something in the nature of
touching up by the Parliamentary draftsmen across the water. I cannot but think
that those eminent draftsmen missed this
particular paragraph, for I do not think
they would have allowed it to go into the
English Statute in the form in which it
now appears there. That is more or less
by the way.
Our Schedule, I suggest, is not only
expressed in perfectly good English-and
I take note that the right hon. Gentleman
agrees that the Clause in the English Act
is not in very good English-but our

The

terms more favourable to him than
those just mentioned.
That, I think, is where the feminine
touch comes in the words, "just mentioned."
If it does, he may put an end to the
agreement on those terms.
on
It is not plain what those terms are.
The expression is not explicit and it is
ambiguous. We have always ourselves
maintained the principle here that whatever our Acts of Parliament might do,
whether we liked them or whether we did
not-and we do not always agree
every Clause of every Bill-that
Parliament's intention should be expressed in unmistakable language, and
I venture to suggest that it is to some
extent due to the maintenance of that
position that the language of our Statutes
has during the past twenty years so rarely
had to be considered by the courts. It is
very rare indeed for a court in Northern
Ireland to be asked to interpret a Northern Ireland Statute, and that is because our Statutes have always been expressed in the plainest possible language.
If we accept this amendment we shall be
departing from our established custom.
I do not think we ought to do that under
any circumstances whatever, certainly
not for the reason given us.
The only reason given us is certain
convenience as to printing. It has never
been the custom of this House to allude
to the cost of printing as having any
influence on the decisions of this House.
The view has been taken that for Parliament or this Chamber to descend to
considerations of that sort would be to
exercise a pettyfogging parsimony which
is entirely out of keeping with the traditions of this House. The House will
remember that we did do something of
this nature in order to avoid the
multiplication and reprinting of forms.
In 1932 we consolidated our Company
law and in the following year it was
discovered that one or two small matters
of inconvenience had arisen which it was
desirable to correct. Accordingly, when
this House passed the necessary Measures,
unusual form of phraseology was
adopted which enabled the two Statutes
to be construed under the date of the
first Statute and so saved an enormous
amount of public inconvenience.
an
That was done to save inconveniences
which would have been extreme rather
than to save expense. There were a large
number of forms scattered about in
various places. Every solicitor's office.
and the courts had those forms, and it
would have been highly inconvenient to
make an alteration. That unusual
expedient was adopted. But although
hitherto it has not been the custom to
raise the cost of printing, it is on record
that the question has arisen in another
place. An hon. Member who represents
one of the. divisions of Belfast once
addressed those who sit in another place
for nineteen hours. I do not say he did
so without repeating himself, but he
spoke for nineteen hours, and as a result
a good deal of Hansard was occupied by
his observations. Perhaps the right hon.
Gentleman, the Leader of the House, was
present when this took place.
Mr. ROBB: No, I was not there.
was
Mr. LESLIE: The question
raised as to the. printing of that nineteen
hours speech, and the right hon. Gentleman, who is now the Minister of
Commerce, speaking in his capacity as
Financial Secretary, drew attention to
the fact that Parliament could not enter
into the question of the cost of printing.
That is the only case in which
the matter has been seriously raised in
our Parliament. There is another aspect
of this question of printing. Some few
years ago we passed a Bill varying the
close season for game, and the result was
to render the list of close seasons printed
on the back of the game licence out of
accord with the then existing law.
What steps were taken? They did not
scrap the existing stock of game licences,
but printed a small slip and pasted it
over the other part. It is known in the
Civil Service as smothering. That is a
perfectly simple method of dealing with
Valuation Acts Bill-
Second Reading
21 MAY 1940
the thing if it is really inconvenient to
print a new supply of hire purchase
forms. I cannot imagine that the supply
of forms used in Northern Ireland is so
immense that that step could not be
taken, and every step should be taken
rather than to deface our Statute Book
with words of ambiguity. I cannot think
that a matter of this sort can be allowed
to develop into actual conflict between
the two Chambers, but I do think the
Government should, in another place,
invite the Commons not to insist on their
Amendment. For myself I could not,
for the reasons I have given, possibly
support the Amendment; indeed, I must
actively oppose its acceptance.
Question put, "That those words be
left out."

Valuation Acts Amendment Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: In the Interim Report of
the Valuation Committee, circulated a
short time ago, it was recommended that
the general revaluation, due to take
place in 1941, should be postponed:
Clause 1 gives effect to this recommendation. Revaluation has been postponed in
Great Britain and during a great war is
hardly an appropriate time for a revaluation intended to last for five years.
Machinery for dealing with the annual
revision of valuations will, of course, be
maintained to enable the reassessment of
property the value of which may change.
Clause 2 is concerned with the contribution to be made by counties and
county boroughs towards the cost of the
annual revision of rateable property. In
the 1932 Act, Parliament laid down the
prniciple that from the date of the first
general revaluation local authorities
should contribute half the actual cost of
annual revision. In order to fix the sum
to be paid to the Exchequer, the Act
required the Ministry of Finance to
ascertain the cost of annual revision in
the second year after each general revaluation, and this sum was to be
regarded as the standard cost of annual
revision for the separate years in each
quinquennial period.
Mr. W. S. Kingan
Mr. J. G. Leslie
Mr. J. H. Robb
Colonel T. Sinclair
Mr. H. Taylor
Up to 1932 local authorities had been
paying £1,705 a year towards the cost of
annual revision. This sum was fixed and
apportioned as far back as 1874 and was
equivalent at that time to one-half of the
actual cost of annual revision. In 1932
the cost of annual revision was estimated
at £13,500 and in the 1932 Bill, as introduced, provision was made for half this
cost that is £6,750-to be recovered
from local authorities in future. This
figure was reduced by the late Minister
of Finance, in Committee, as a special
concession to the purely arbitary figure
of £3,405, pending the eventual assessment for annual revision on the postrevaluation basis, that is, until the cost
of annual revision in the standard year,
which was fixed at the second year after
the 1936 revaluation, was known.
The cost in that year was found to be
£17,000, and as the law now stands local
authorities were liable from 1936 onwards to pay half this amount, that is
£8,500, instead of £3,405, which they
have paid since 1932.
The Government feel that it would be
unfair to expect local authorities to fulfil
retrospectively their obligation under the
1932 Act, which would be £8,500 per
year. Accordingly, after consultation
with representatives of local authorities
it has been agreed to take statutory
power to assess the charge for annual
revision in the period since 1936 at the
old rate of £3,405 up to and including
Valuation Acts Bill-
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
the current year. Under Clause 2,
therefore, the principle laid down in the
1932 Valuation Act that local authorities
should contribute half the cost of the
annual revision of valuation will only
operate from next April.
As regards the second part of Clause 2,
the 1932 Act said that "The Ministry
of Finance shall ascertain and certify the
annual expense of the annual revision of
the valuation of rateable property . . .
in the second period of twelve months
next after the coming into force of each
General Revaluation under this Act."
Now, with the postponement of the
1940-41 Revaluation this meant that a
new standard, for the purpose of assessing local authorities' contributions
towards annual revision, could not be
ascertained unless there was a General
Revaluation. This part of the Clause is
designed to enable the standard cost to
be revised in 1942, as though there had
been a revaluation in 1941, and meets
the objection of local authorities who had
urged that as the cost of annual revision
might alter in a downward direction
then, in the absence of a new standard,
they would be compelled to pay the old
rate of £8,500 per annum in relation to
a greatly reduced standard of actual cost.
The remainder of the Bill is concerned
with administrative matter and is selfexplanatory.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Resolved, "That this House do now
adjourn until Tuesday next."-(Mr.
Robb).
Adjourned accordingly at
Four o'clock until Tuesday next.

:-

Unemployment Insurance (Increase of
Benefit in Respect of Dependent
Children) Order (Northern
Ireland), 1940.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move,
"That the Order in Council made by
the Governor of Northern Ireland on the
19th April, 1940, under Section 65 of the
Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern
Ireland), 1936, for increasing the rate of
dependant's benefit payable in respect of
each of the first two dependent children
and the maximum amount of agricultural
benefit payable weekly to any
claimant be hereby approved."
one
The Unemployment Insurance Statutory Committee presents an annual
report to the Minister of Labour in Great
Britain on the financial position of the
Unemployment Fund there. The last of
these reports dealt with the state of the
fund at the 31st December, 1939.
Iwas found that at that date the balance
in the General Scheme Fund was
sufficient, in the opinion of the Committee,
to enable a substantial sum to be re-
It
leased to reduce the outstanding debt and
so effect a saving in the annual amount
of interest and sinking fund charges. The
Committee recommended that this saving
should be used to increase from 3s a
a week to 4s a week the rate of dependants' benefit in respect of each of the
first two dependent children.
The Committee also found that the
state of the Agricultural Fund justified
the payment of a similar increase of dependants' benefit to persons insured under
the Agricultural Scheme of Unemployment Insurance, and recommended that
the maximum weekly amount of agricultural benefit payable to an individual
should be increased from 33s to 35s.
On the 5th April, 1940, an Order giving effect to these proposals as from the
11th April, 1940, was made by the
Minister of Labour in Great Britain
after he had obtained the approval of
Parliament, and the provisions of that
Order have been applied to Northern
Ireland by the Unemployment Insurance
(Increase of Benefit in respect of Dependent Children) Order (Northern Ireland), 1940, which is the subject of the
Motion before the House.
In accordance with the provisions of
the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern Ireland), 1936, the Order would.
cease to have effect after twelve months
unless, before the expiration of that
time, it is approved by Parliament.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

On Question, Motion agreed to.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Ministries
Bill, to which they desire the concurrence
of this House.
Ministries Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That this Bill be now read
a Second time." As the House is aware,
the object of this Bill is to concentrate in
one Department under a Cabinet Minister
all matters that are essential for public
security and civil defence. It is proposed
to do that by establishing a new Ministry
and to that Ministry there will be transferred air-raid precautions, civil defence
and the constabulary forces, including the
Ulster Defence Volunteers. The Bill
gives power to also transfer to this
Ministry from time to time by Order in
Council any other powers that may be
found appropriate or necessary.
Hon.
Members will, perhaps, have seen in the
Press that the creation of this new
Ministry will not impose, so far as the
salary of the Minister is concerned, any
charge upon public funds.
In presenting this Bill to the House the
Government ask for the co-operation of
everybody in the Six Counties in aid of
the immediate problem before this
Ministry. That is the co-ordination of
all measures necessarily taken for the
purpose of the protection of this part of
the United Kingdom in so far as lies
Ministries Bill--
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
within the ambit of the jurisdiction of
the Government of Northern Ireland.
To take the Bill as it stands, Clause 1
provides that there shall be established
a Department of the Government of
Northern Ireland which shall be styled
by the name of the Ministry of Public
Security for Northern Ireland. Under
sub-clause (2) the Ministry is to have.
power in respect of matters within the
jurisdiction of the Government of Northern Ireland, to take such steps and precautions, and to issue such orders, as the
Minister may consider necessary for
public security, civil defence, the preservation of the peace and the maintenance
of order, the co-ordination of civil defence
services and the protection of persons and
property from injury or damage, in
the present emergency; and, without
prejudice to the generality of the foregoing provisions, the Ministry shall have
such functions of any department in
relation to any of the said purposes as
may be transferred to the Ministry under
sub-section (3) of this section.
Under sub-clause (3) the Governor of
Northern Ireland may by order transfer
to the Ministry from any department any
functions exercisable by such department
in relation to any of the purposes
mentioned in sub-section (2) of this
section, or may by order direct that the
Ministry and such department shall exercise any of the said functions concurrently.
By sub-clause (4) the provisions of subsection (3) of section one, section two and
section three of the Ministries of Northern
Ireland Act, 1921, shall apply to the
Ministry as they apply to each Ministry
mentioned in the said Act. The provisions
mentioned here apply to the distribution
of the work amongst the staff and enact
that the Ministry shall be a body
corporate.
Sub-clause (5) says that the provisions
of section four of the Administrative
Provisions Act (Northern Ireland), 1925,
shall apply to the Ministry. That enables
the staff to be paid.
Sub-clause (6) makes the adjustment
I have. mentioned. It enacts that the
salaries of Ministerial Offices Act, 1921,
as amended by subsequent enactments,
shall have effect as if for the Schedule
to that Act there were substituted the
Schedule to this Act. That provides for
a reduction in the salaries of those
Cabinet Ministers who receive salaries,
and the application of the reduction to
the salary of the new Minister.
Clause 2 says:
It shall be the duty of every person
affected by any order issued by the
Ministry in pursuance of this Act to
comply with that order, and if he fails
to do so he shall be. guilty of an offence
against that order. The provisions of
sections two, three and four of the Civil
Authorities (Special Powers) Act
(Northern Ireland), 1922, shall apply
to offences against orders issued as
aforesaid as they apply to offences
against the regulations contained in
the Schedule to that Act, but subject
to such modifications and adaptations.
as may be made by Order in Council
under this Act.
That deals with the authority for
prosecution and punishment.
Clause 3 provides that the Governor of
Northern Ireland may by Order in
Council make such incidental, consequential and supplemental provisions as
may be necessary or expedient for the
purpose of giving full effect to this Act,
and it sets out the various Departments,
the functions of which may be transferred.
Clause 4 is the Interpretation Clause,
and says the expression "Department"
means a Department, Minister or other
authority of the Government of Northern
Ireland; and "Functions" includes
jurisdiction, powers and duties.
Clause 5 is the Short Title. of the Act,
and provides that the Act is to continue
in force until Parliament otherwise
determines.
Question proposed, "That this Bill be
now read a Second time.

to leave home at ten minutes to
eleven. At the same time, the Bill
is SO clearly expressed, if I may

Major DOBBS: I want to make
several points on this Bill, but before I
do so, I want to put it in its proper
perspective and to give you the history
leading up to it. To-day the Empire
faces the most serious situation in her
history. It is just as serious for Ulster
as it is for the Empire.
How many
people in Ulster realise this? Perhaps
five in every 100. Why? Because they
been
have
completely misled. For
instance, very little that has been ordered
to be done in England has been ordered
to be done here. Take a few instances.
The first is conscription. They have
been told there will be no conscription
in Ulster. Ulster, you need not fight.
Why is there no conscription in Ulster?
Well, I do not intend to go into that now,
but I should flatly refuse to believe any
reasons against conscription now, unless
they seemed absolutely rock-bottom
right, with the facts put forward to
substantiate them. My wife asked
senior officer just arrived here last week
what he noticed most on this, his first
visit to Ireland. Without hesitation he
replied: "The number of young men
standing about the streets."
a

the guns,

We all remember that from the very

Major DOBBS: What I want to point
out is that this Bill is a red herring. It
is a matter of compromise. That is not

greatest General in France has let France

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to say a word in favour of this
Bill.
I am very pleased the Government
has taken this step. I do know there
some disappointment amongst the
people of Northern Ireland, who felt that
everything was not being done that should
be done in looking after the interests of
Northern Ireland during this terrible
emergency. The very fact of the Government setting up this Ministry with a
member of the Cabinet at its head will
give a good deal of satisfaction to people
outside. One pleasing feature of the Bill
is the measure of control it exercises. It
gives to the new Ministry the control of
A.R.P., the police, and the Volunteer
Defence Force. I can readily understand
that so far as Imperial defence is concerned that is a matter entirely in the
hands of the Imperial Government.
I for one very much regret that conscription has not been extended to
Northern Ireland. Although this is not
a matter within our jurisdiction I understand our Government was very willing to
see conscription put into operation here,
but the Imperial Government was not
anxious to do that. It was impossible for
our Government to do it without their
sanction. I believe it is only fair that
the son of one person should do his part
as well as the son of another. The burden
should fall on all alike, and I am very
much in favour of conscription being extended to Northern Ireland.
There is something else I cannot understand. If they are so anxious for men—
and the men of Northern Ireland would
be as good recruits as any to be got elsewhere-why are they not exerting themselves to obtain as many volunteers as
they want for the Imperial Services.
I know a considerable number of young
men who have already volunteered and
passed the doctor, and yet they have not
been called up. I have a boy who is
breaking his neck to get into the Service.
He passed the doctor in April last and
was told he would likely be sent for a
fortnight or three weeks after Easter.
Although he has telephoned them continually he has not been called up, and
he tells me there are quite a lot of young
men who are in exactly the same position.
They are very anxious to go, but there is
no desire to have them at the present
time.

Therefore, I for one do not lament the
fact that we have a large number of
young men at home who should be in
the Army. That is a matter for the
Imperial Government. If they are busy
with conscripts and intend to rely on
our volunteers afterwards, I am satisfied
they will get them in abundance when
they want them. I think it must be
lack of equipment that prevents the
Imperial authorities looking for volunteers
here.
Another thing we have to remember is
that we have a considerable number of
young men employed in aircraft and shipbuilding production, work which is of
great national importance.
There is one
thing I regret very much, and that is
that the best advantage is not being
taken of the skilled men employed in the
aircraft factories and shipyards. That is
deplorable, because skilled labour is very
scarce. They are in desperation to get
skilled workers everywhere in the United
Kingdom; in fact they are diluting
labour, and I am very sorry that the best
is not being taken out of the skilled men
who are employed in the aircraft factories
and shipyards of Northern Ireland. It
must be due either to want of material
or engines, but their work is not being
utilised to the fullest extent possible.
It is not the fault of the men, because
they are loyal and willing enough to do
their best work and put forth the
maximum effort when aircraft are so much
needed. I hope something will be done
speedily to put these matters right.
I am
sure the people of Northern
Ireland will give all the assistance they
possibly can to the. Minister who is responsible for this particular Department.
I wish him success and hope he will safeguard our position so that this country
will not be used as a stepping-stone to
make an attack on other parts of the
United Kingdom.
Mr. BARCLAY: I support the Second
Reading of this Bill for several reasons.
In the first place, I know that those who
live in the country districts will now have
somewhere to write to for information.
People in some of the country districts
have asked me where they can get information as to what they are to do. They
have no one to tell them what to do, but
the setting up of this Ministry will give
these people a Department to write to.
There are districts where very little has
been done so far as A.R.P. is concerned.
For instance, nothing has been done in
Cushendall and Cushendun and the
district along that coast, which is in sight
of Scotland, and is a very likely place. for
parachute troops to land.
Nothing has been done there. There
is some small organisation, but they have
no gas masks or nursing organisation or
bandages, and that is a very likely place
for the enemy to land. I had two very
respectable men with me last night and
they wanted to know how they could.
organise a volunteer defence force in the
district I live in. I could not give them
very much information, but they will now
be able to get it from this new Ministry.
I have great pleasure in supporting the
Bill.

Industrial Assurance Bill-
Second Reading
27 JUNE 1940
THE SENATE.

Thursday, 27th June, 1940.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Paper Presented.
Ministry of Home Affairs:
Regulation made under the Civil
Authorities (Special Powers) Acts
(N.I.), 1922 and 1933.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Industrial Assurance and Friendly
Societies (Emergency Protection
from Forfeiture) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The Bill of which I now ask the Second
Reading is, as its title indicates, an
emergency measure designed to afford
some protection against the lapsing of
insurance policies of certain classes which
the owners may find difficulty in maintaining owing to circumstances arising
directly or indirectly out of the war.
a conse-
The Senate will be aware from the
reports laid upon the Table year by year
that industrial assurance in Northern
Ireland has attained very large dimensions. Upwards of £1,000,000 is collected
each year in premiums upon industrial
assurance policies. In our area it is
inevitable that there will be some cases
of hardship and difficulty as
quence of the war, and the provision of
this Bill will afford some relief by
enabling the payment of premiums to be
suspended in approved cases without endangering the assurance effected by the
policy. The Clauses of the Bill closely
follow those of the British Act of Parliament to which the Royal Assent was
given on 14th March last. The Senate
will be aware that the great bulk of
assurance business in Northern Ireland
is in fact in the hands of British
companies and societies.
Coming to the Clauses of the Bill,
Clause 1 states the classes of policies to
which the Measure is to apply. They
are:-
(a) policies of industrial assurance;
(b) policies of assurance upon human
life effected with collecting societies,
not being policies of industrial
assurance; and
(c) policies of assurance upon human
life, in respect of which there are
separate premiums, effected with
registered friendly societies other than
collecting societies.
The protection given will not extend,
however, to policies exceeding £50 in
amount (exclusive of any bonus additions)
or to policies in respect of which not less
than two years' premiums have been
paid.
Clause 2 of the Bill requires every
notice of forfeiture to contain a statement in a prescribed form that an application may be made in writing for protection from forfeiture on the ground
that the default is due to circumstances
arising directly or indirectly out of the
war. On receipt of such notice the
owner of the policy may make his application to the company or society for the
protection of his policy. If such application is refused an appeal will lie to
the Industrial Assurance Commissioner.
There are equivalent provisions with
respect to policies which are not
policies of industrial assurance in Clause
8 of the Bill. They require the service
of a notice before any policy within the
prescribed limits is forfeited.
It is not to be expected that the
companies and societies should continue
to carry the risks upon policies of insurance where no premiums are being
received without some adjustment, and
this adjustment is provided for in
Clause 3 of the Bill. First of all (a) if
a claim arises upon a policy before the
expiration of the period of grace (which
is such date as may be fixed by Order
and three months afterwards) the amount
of the policy is to be reduced by the
amount of the outstanding premiums and
interest.
(b) If, however, the policy remains in
force at the expiration of the period of
grace and the outstanding premiums have
not been paid the amount of the policy
239 Financial Provisions Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Robb]
will be reduced by the sum produced by
multiplying the amount of the premiums.
by the factor specified in the second
column of the Schedule to the Bill. For
myself I do not profess to understand the
mathematics of the Schedule.
(c) In the case of an endowment policy,
which is in force at the expiration of the
period of grace, the date of the maturity.
of the policy will be postponed as in
paragraph (c) of sub-clause 1 of Clause 3
of the Bill.
In Clause 4 there is provision for the
determination of the protection in cases
where the owner of the policy is no
longer unable to pay the premiums. The
protection allowed by the Bill is to cease
as from an appointed day which will be
the date fixed in Great Britain at the
conclusion of the war, and thereafter
there will be a period of grace of three
months in which the suspended premiums
may be paid.
These are the main provisions of the
Bill to which I ask the House to give a
Second Reading.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Exchequer and Financial Provisions Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: I ask the House to read
a Second time this Bill, which, of course,
is a financial Bill. Clause 1 increases
the capital of the Civil Contingencies
Fund from £100,000 to £200,000. The
Civil Contingencies Fund is a reserve
fund which may be drawn upon at any
time when there is not immediate cash
available, and is provided for that
purpose. It may happen during the
curious times in which we live that the
amount of capital in that fund will not
be sufficient, and the Government propose
to increase the capital from £100,000 to
£200,000.
The second Clause is to transfer from
the Reserve Fund to the Road Fund the
sum of £21,063. It is simply a matter
of book-keeping.
Clause 3 provides that the Government
shall purchase the stock issued by the
Road Transport Board of Northern Ireland in the case of the "A" stock at its
nominal value, and in the case of the
"B" stock at 90 per cent. of its nominal
value. This follows the recommendations
of the McLintock Committee and other
committees.
Clause 4 places Ulster Savings Certificates under the regulations applicable to
United Kingdom Savings Certificates.
Clause 5 extends the period of the
appointment of the existing Comptroller
and Auditor-General. The Comptroller
and Auditor-General has come up to the
time when he would in the ordinary
course retire. This proposal is to enable
him to retain the office until he attains
the age of 67, as there is difficulty in
getting a full panel suitable for appointment to this office. Clause 6 is merely
the short title and construction. I beg
to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I think the only Clause
in the Bill that is likely to evoke, I was
going to say public interest, but I may
perhaps say provoke any examination in
this Chamber is the Clause relating to
the redemption of the transport stock.
As the right hon. Gentleman says that
follows the recommendation of the Joint
Committee which sat on transport
matters. That Committee definitely recommended that the transport stock
should be redeemed at its face value in the
case of the "A" stock, and having regard
to the position created by a decided case
the "B" stock should be taken for 90
per cent. But that Committee also
recommended that when the stock was
redeemed arrangements should also be
made for the payment of the outstanding
interest. I do not observe anything in
this Clause dealing with that question of
interest, and I think the House might
reasonably be expected to be told something about that.
The suggestion in the Bill is that the
Government should purchase by agreement, and exactly what they mean by
agreement in this connection I am not
quite sure that I appreciate. It is
obvious that the Government cannot purchase stock at par or on any other terms
except by agreement, and whether the
expression "agreement" is meant to
Appropriation Bill
Adjournment
27 JUNE 1940
imply negotiation, amongst other things,
as regards that matter of interest I do
not know, and I should like to be told.
Mr. ROBB: So far as the purchase of
stock is concerned, I understand that
that has been agreed with the stockholders. The matter of interest is being
negotiated, but an agreement has not
yet been reached.
Mr. LESLIE: That being so, is there
any power being taken under this Bill
to pay that interest?
Mr. ROBB: No.
Mr. LESLIE: That means that a
separate Measure will have to be presented to Parliament in due course. That
is as far as we can reasonably take the
matter to-day, and that is all I want to
know. There is nothing else in the Bill
that calls for any observation whatever.

Questions to Minister.
PROTECTION OF PETROL PUMPS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs :-
To ask the Leader of the House whether
adequate steps have been taken to ensure
that petrol pumps are put entirely out
of action in the event of a raid by the
enemy.
Major DOBBS: I asked the owner of
a petrol pump a few days ago what
arrangements he had made to prevent his
petrol being taken by the enemy. His
reply was that he had a padlock. The
enemy, we must realise, is prepared to
go to all lengths, to commit murder if
necessary. So on the one side you have
got a padlock, and on the other side a
murderer, if necessary.
on
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
much regret that I cannot, in the public
interest, give detailed information
this subject, but I can assure the hon.
and gallant Senator that this matter has
received attention.
APPLICATION OF EMERGENCY
ORDERS.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether the
Emergency Orders issued at Westminster
apply to Northern Ireland, and, if not,
whether steps will be taken immediately
to make them effective here.
Last Tuesday I stated in this House
that on the previous Friday revolvers and
live ammunition had been sold over the
counter of a shop in County Antrim.
Some considerable time ago all firearms
were collected from gunsmiths shops and
so on in England. I hope they have now
been collected from every shop that holds
them in Northern Ireland, because if
Hitler makes his raid on Northern
Ireland you may be quite certain that a
very short time before that raid takes
place every shop holding any firearms of
any description will be held up and those
firearms, with their ammunition, taken
by traitors. Those firearms will be used
to strike our men in the back. I am
not going any further into this question,
although there are other Emergency
Orders that have been put into operation
in England, dealing with church bells,
milestones and other things.
Mr. ROBB: I presume that the hon.
and gallant Senator is referring to
Orders made under the Emergency
Powers (Defence) Acts. Many of these
Orders when made are applied to the
United Kingdom as a whole; many
others, however, are made in respect of
particular parts of the United Kingdom,
of which some have been made in respect
of Northern Ireland.
In general the powers granted under
the Emergency Powers (Defence) Acts
are exercisable by the Imperial authorities in regard to the whole of the United
Kingdom. The question as to the application or non-application of any particular Order to Northern Ireland is
decided by the Imperial authorities, after
consultation with the Government of
Northern Ireland, when time permits
and circumstances so require.
With regard to the ringing of church
bells, to which the hon. and gallant
Member referred a few days ago, that has
been brought to the attention of the new
Minister of Public Security, and I have
no doubt he will take appropriate action.
Major DOBBS: This question of firearms is so important that I wonder if
the Leader of the House would put it
Industrial Assurance Bill 246
Oral Answers.
2 JULY 1940
to our authorities here to have every
firearm-and there are firearms in shops
in Northern Ireland-collected and put
in a safe place of custody. We are not
fighting an election; we are fighting a
most terrible war, and everything possible should be done to help us to win
that war.
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of the
reply to the earlier part of the question
as to the application of emergency
Orders to Northern Ireland, it may be
remembered that on one of our emergency Measures some few weeks ago I
asked whether it would be possible for
Members of both Houses of this Parliament to be furnished with copies of those
emergency Orders which did apply to
Northern Ireland. I should like to know
if any steps have been taken towards
that end. I have not myself received
any copies of such Orders, and one knows
nothing about them except what one
reads in the public newspapers.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I was
speaking only yesterday to one of the
largest traders in firearms in Belfast, and
he was complaining bitterly about the
restrictions that were imposed on himself and similar traders. Their stock has
to be carried at premises other than the
shop, and they have been required to
remove the lock and other essential parts
of guns so that they would not be of any
use to persons who did not possess those
essential parts. Therefore, a gunsmith's
stock would be useless to anyone who
was not in a position to assemble the
various parts of the guns.
Mr. ROBB: In reply to Senator
Leslie, it is true that these emergency
Orders have not been circulated. They
follow one another with such rapidity
that it would be very hard to keep track
of them unless one made a special study
of
them. It has not been found,
feasible to circulate these Orders.

CLAUSES 1 (Application of Act to
assurance policies), 2 (Protection from

CLAUSE 4 (Determination of protection).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill".
Mr. LESLIE: I rather want to enter
a mild protest against the use of the
word "revoke" in this Clause in that
sense. So far I must confess I can find no
precedent either in the. Statute Book or
in English literature for using the word
"revoke" as applied to something done
by somebody else. You can revoke anything you have done. yourself, such as a
will or power of attorney, or anything
done by your predecessor in title, as a
Ministry frequently take power to do. To
quote an historical example, the
celebrated edict issued by Henri Quatre
about the end of the sixteenth century
was revoked by his successor about a
hundred
later.
years
You can do that, but I can find no
precedent for revoking any notice, issued
by someone else. To revoke means to call
back, not to call off, and here, we are
using it in the sense of to call off. Your
commissioner is to have power to call off
the notice. I do not think it is a proper
word to use. A proper word would have
been "cancel," annul," or "quash." I
do not want such a use of the word to
become a precedent because I do not
think it is in accord with the best forms
either of literature or the wording of a
statute. I am not going to move
amendment as it is not worth while.
an
Mr. ROBB: I would like to say that
the New Oxford Dictionary says that
revoke means cancel.
Mr. LESLIE: It will also be seen, if
the right hon. and learned Gentleman
refers to Chaucer, that it means revive.
It is a bad word.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill.

I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
would like to ask the Leader of the House
if he is able to give us any information
as to negotiations that may be going on
between the Imperial Government, the
Southern Government, or the Northern
Government with regard to the defence
of Ireland. I ask this question because I
am very much perturbed by the
expression of a number of cross-Channel
papers regarding this matter. I find they
all call for a united Ireland; in fact, one
of the papers to-day said, "Ireland unite.
Bury old quarrels and old resentments.
I thought those people would have been
well satisfied if they had paid particular
attention to our Prime Minister's statement on Friday or Saturday in which
""
Defence of Ireland
Defence of Ireland
2 JULY 1940
he very distinctly made it known that we
are prepared to make any sacrifice for the
defence. of Ireland, and to associate with
those in authority in Southern Ireland
for that purpose, provided they send away
the representatives of Germany and of
Italy who are at present stationed in that
part of the country. If they had paid particular attention to that statement I
think it would have altered the opinion
expressed by a number of these cross-
Channel papers.
It is hardly possible that those people
understand the position when they ask
us to unite. Here in Northern Ireland
you have a very loyal population anxious
to do all they possibly can to assist the
Imperial Government in the prosecution
of this war, and on the other side of the
Border you have a country that has proclaimed itself neutral. Whether they
have a sufficient force to protect themselves in the event of an invasion-we
know perfectly well that neutrality has
not prevented the Germans from invading
other countries-I do not know. These
papers and the people across the water
should try to understand the position.
Surely they do not want Northern Ireland to make all the sacrifices. If they
are. not satisfied with the statement made
by the Prime Minister what do they want
the people of Northern Ireland to do?
I look upon that statement as being a
representative one, a statement from the
whole of the people of Northern Ireland.
If Southern Ireland are prepared to ask
for the protection of the British forces
I am sure they would get it. What
troubles me so much is the fact that the
papers continuously say unite, and they
ask us at the same time to sink all our
differences. One paper to-day goes so
far as to say in reference to Lord Craigavon's speech, "Who can approve the
provocative terms of the statement?''
I do not see anything provocative about
the statement made by Lord Craigavon.
It was very clear. It went a long way
to meet the position. I would be very
glad if the Leader of the House could
give us information about any negotiations that may be going on between the
Imperial authorities and Southern Ireland, or between the Imperial authorities
and Northern Ireland.
As a representative of the Unionist
workers of Northern Ireland I say that
we should be taken into their confidence,
and if there is anything going on we
should be made aware of it immediately,
because I know of no people who are
more at a loss than the working classes
in Northern Ireland. They are determined to maintain the position they have
secured. If our Prime Minister and the
Government are aware of anything going
on or of any attempt being made to
undermine our position it should be
made known to the people and made
known pretty early so that they may be
in a position to resist, and let the world
at large know we are not going to be
imposed upon. (Hon. Members: Hear,
hear.)
Mr. ROBB: I have no information
whatever-not merely no information
that I can give the House, but no information whatever-of any negotiations
between the Imperial Government and
Southern Ireland. The position of Northern Ireland has been defined publicly
by the Prime Minister on behalf of the
Government, and with that position I am
quite certain the great majority of our
people agree.

Questions to Minister.
PRICE OF SCRAP METAL.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs: :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the price of scrap metal is controlled in Northern Ireland as it is in
England; and if not, whether steps will
be taken to have the price controlled in
Northern Ireland.
Major DOBBS: With regard to the
first question I have tabled, some little
time ago a question regarding salvage was
asked in this House by the hon. and
gallant Member, Senator Major Boyle.
The reply he received was this, that the
cost of collection and shipment to Great
Britain might more than offset the advantage to be derived from its collection.
On the face of it, this was a very unsatisfactory answer. Some little time
afterwards I was in London and went to
the Ministry of Supply. There I had
the good fortune to see the gentleman
who was in charge of the collection and
disposal of scrap metal. I explained the
situation to him, and he said,
"First
with regard to the price, we cannot let
C
the price go up. We cannot have A
saying, I want a shilling more,' and B
saying, I want another shilling,' and
so on, and the price gradually going up.
We must keep the price down and, if
necessary, it will be controlled, and, if
necesary, scrap metal will be confiscated.
We must have that scrap, because it is
vitally important. With regard to
transport, we, the Ministry of Supply,
are prepared, if necessary, to charter
ships to bring it from Belfast to Great
Britain. There is no question about it;
we must have the scrap."
Efforts are being made here to get that
scrap, and efforts are also being made
to get any scrap metal the farmers have.
But the farmers are asking this question:
Will the price of scrap metal rise? It
must be remembered that the price of
scrap metal at the present moment is in
the neighbourhood of £2 a ton. Towards
the end of the last war it rose and rose
and rose until at one time it was between
£9 and £11 per ton. I think the farmers
definitely have a right to know whether
the price of scrap metal is going to rise,
and I do hope the Leader of the House
will be able to inform us that the price
is controlled here, and that it will not
rise.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The answer to the first part of the question is in the affirmative; the second
part does not, therefore, arise.
Oral Answers
Oral Answers
11 JULY 1940
Major DOBBS: I am very glad to
have such a satisfactory answer.
TRANSPORT OF SCRAP METAL.

A lorry that has to go to Belfast or to

so on would have a load, we hope a full
load, of scrap metal the owner could
collect from his neighbours and bring in.
Mr. ROBB: As the hon. Senator is
no doubt aware, the Parliamentary
Secretary of the Ministry of Education
is engaged in making arrangements for
the collection of scrap metal in districts
where organised voluntary collections are
not at present being undertaken by local
authorities. Under this scheme scrap is
being delivered voluntarily at rail-head
or collected in dumps and conveyed from
these to rail-head, either through the
owners of
voluntary co-operation of
lorries or by the Northern Ireland Road
Transport Board.
to
The scrap is conveyed to Belfast by rail
and the net proceeds when sold go
the British Red Cross Society. There is
no necessity, in order to implement this
scheme, to grant a concession such as is
sought by the hon. Senator.
As to owners of scrap who wish to sell
it for private gain, such persons are engaged in a purely commercial enterprise,
and no reasonable argument can be given
for granting to them a concession which
persons engaged in other commercial
enterprises do not enjoy.

Mr. LESLIE: Arising out
Arising out of the
answer, may I ask whether the Government has considered the danger that
through undue centralisation, perhaps in
the interests of the Red Cross, scrap metal
may, in fact, be lost. The point is that
in some country districts, at all events,
scrap metal is being contributed to be
collected by someone, presumably in a
lorry driven by petrol which costs money,
to be sold, not for the benefit of the

Sir CRAWFORD MCCULLAGH: I
think I can in some degree clear the air
so far as the collection and disposal of
scrap in Belfast is concerned. This
gathering of scrap is not as simple as it
looks. The Belfast Corporation has a
contractor for all scrap delivered at the
tip-head. It is a contract which will
not be terminated until next March.
That contractor has a perfect right to
collect anything he can take, either scrap,
paper, or anything else he can sell
again or make a profit out of. This
contractor waived his rights for a fortnight for a special drive for the collection
of scrap in Belfast. He was entitled to
all he could get from the sale of that
scrap, but he waived his rights in that
respect for a fortnight.
In that fortnight's drive we collected
and delivered free of cost to our contractor over 1,100 tons of scrap. That
we sold at the rate of £1 a ton. The
hon. and gallant Member opposite
mentioned a price of 45s a ton, but from
what I have heard we got too much for
our scrap, and for this reason. It has to
be delivered in a central depot. You
cannot ship it across the water as you
receive it. At the central depot it has to
be examined by experts who separate it
into the different grades. Across the
water there is a very substantial fine if
by any chance brass is left with the
metal, because if brass gets into the
melting pot it does a lot of harm.
Consequently the man who bought this
scrap for £1 a ton has to employ an army
of men to separate and grade it, because
in the collection of this scrap you get a
lot of stuff that is absolutely useless.
You get thousands of zinc buckets, and
all the tins the people have collected during
the past twenty years are gathered up and
sent in, and these are worth practically
nothing. That has all to be separated
and sent away properly graded.
As regards the price, there is no fixed
price here, although in theory there is a
fixed price, because none of that metal
is dealt with here. It has all got to be.
shipped across the water, where the price
is fixed. There is a maximum price for
every grade. We sold rails last week for
£6 a ton. Any scheme to collect this
scrap at different centres will not work.
It will have to go to some authority
where it can be properly graded and
shipped across the water.
Major DOBBS: When I mentioned the
price of 45s a ton I was speaking of scrap
collected from farmers, and not scrap
collected in Belfast.

be considered.

Question proposed.
Major DOBBS: May I be allowed to
ask a question of which I have given.
private notice. I desire to ask the
Leader of the House whether the Minister of Agriculture intends making an
Order that all farmers should make their
fields unsuitable for landing by hostile
aircraft, and, if so, whether he will also
issue instructions as to the best methods
of field obstruction to be used.
There was a write-up in the papers on
Saturday, headed "Defence Measures,"
and it started, "The Ministry of Agriculture appeals to all farmers.' It then
went on to say that special attention
must be paid, and so on. I am afraid
the farmers do not know exactly what
they are to do, and therefore I ask
whether an Order will be made. I would
like to point out that if one farmer
makes his fields so that they cannot be
used by hostile aircraft it is not very
much good if all the farmers around him,
perhaps with bigger and flatter fields,
have not done anything. If an Order is
to be made farmers would like to know
what is the best kind of obstruction..
There is a golf club and the members
propose to put railway sleepers into the
ground to a depth of three feet, leaving
six feet out of the ground. A very easy
thing for farmers to do is to get some
larch poles about seven feet long and
drive them into the ground, leaving five
feet out of the ground. Farmers would
like to know how thick they should be.
Would poles three by three do, as used
for ordinary palings? Farmers do not
know what obstruction would be the best
to use and what would not be of any use
against hostile aircraft.
I would also like to ask the Leader of
the House whether steps will be taken to
see that A.A. and G.P.O. telephone
kiosks, especially those along the coast
roads, will be of no use to the enemy.
Mr. ROBB: The making of a Defence
Order in the terms suggested by the hon.
and gallant Senator is not a matter for
the Ministry of Agriculture. At the request of the military authorities, however, the Minister of Agriculture has
during the past week issued an appeal to
all farmers in Northern Ireland to take
immediate steps to obstruct all fields that
are capable of being used for the landing
of aircraft. Press notices and circulars
have already been issued describing the
simplest means of doing so; moreover the
Ministry's Area Tillage Officers are
already in touch with farmers in their
areas who have fields that could be used
for the landing of aircraft.
With regard to the other question of
Automobile Association and G.P.O.
telephone kiosks, this is a matter which
falls entirely within the competence of
the military and postal authorities, and
the Government is confident that these
authorities may be relied upon to do
everything possible to achieve the objects
which the hon. and gallant Senator has
in view. I shall, however, see that the
remarks which the hon. and gallant
Senator has made are brought to the
notice of those concerned.
Mr. LESLIE: That question of the
farmer obstructing his field is really a
rather wide one, probably wider than
E
Defence Measures
Defence Measures
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
some of the fields are.. The difficulty is,
of course, that a large farm may easily
contain a dozen fifteen acre fields which
are required for ploughing and so on, and
it is rather important that the individual
field or farm should be inspected by some
competent authority and intimation
given as to which of those particular
fields is under consideration at the
moment. I myself have got one field
which is fairly well protected, because it
had the railway and telegraph wires
immediately alongside it, and a patch of
rushes in the middle. In a great many
cases there are trees still left in the hedgerows which afford some protection. The
only possible way of dealing with the
thing systematically is for the local
military authority to go round and put
his finger on this and that spot and send
an intimation to the owner of the land.
I do not think the larch pole, could be
very largely used in my part of the
world because it is very hard to get larch
at all, and still less larch poles seven
feet long. I do not know that a pole
would be strong enough, but in any case.
you cannot stick poles all over a large
field because you would destroy the field
for its natural purpose. It is a case in
which expert guidance, brought down to
the terms of the. particular locality, is
required. I should like to be told to what
date it is suggested the House should
adjourn.
