Ministry of Labour:
Unemployment Insurance Bill (Northern Ireland), 1940. Memorandum by
the Minister of Labour explanatory of
the Bill.
Presented (by Command) and ordered
to lie on the Table.
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV. be suspended for this day's
sitting for the purpose of taking the
Second Reading of the Unemployment
Insurance Bill when it is brought from
the House of Commons".-(Mr. Robb.)
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Unemployment Insurance Bill, to which they
desire the concurrence of this House.
Unemployment Insurance Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time".-(Mr. Long.)
I
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time". This Bill is a copy
of a Bill that has just been passed at
Westminster. As the House is aware, our
unemployment insurance scheme is
closely linked up with the scheme in the
United Kingdom that it is not only desir-
SO
able but necessary that our scheme should
keep pace with and really be a copy of
the scheme as it applies in the United
Kingdom. This Bill follows the lines of
the Bill at Westminster. The main
objects of the Measure are to provide
certain increases of benefit which involve
increases of contributions, and to bring
into insurance a new class of contributor
whose claims for consideration have from
time to time been very vigorously urged.
The increases in benefit and contributions affect four main classes of insured
persons who, under the Unemployment
Insurance Act are called men, women,
young men, and young women; the
former two classes being persons of 21
and over, and the latter two classes
persons of 18 to 20. Under the general
scheme of unemployment insurance the
Bill provides an increase of benefit of 3s
per week for men and women, and for
young men and young women who are
entitled to benefit for their dependants
the weekly increase proposed is 2s.
Under the agricultural scheme the proposed increases are 3s per week for men
and young men who are entitled to benefit
for their dependants, and 2s per week for
other young men. In the case of women
and young women the increase proposed is
2s per week. Provision is also made to
raise the maximum weekly rate of benefit
of agricultural insured persons from 35s
to 41s per week.
Corresponding with that the Bill proposes to increase contributions in respect
of the classes whose benefit rates are
increased. The increase in contributions
will be borne in equal shares by the three
contributing parties-the State, the employer and the worker. Each of the three
parties will pay an extra ld per week
under the general scheme, and an
extra d per week under the agricultural scheme. The agricultural
further
rate will be increased by a
Ad from all parties on and after 6th July,
1942. The rates of contributions for
persons under 18 remain unaffected under
both schemes.
The next part of the Bill, Clause 3,
deals with a different subject. In order
to give insured persons, particularly those.
who are casual workers, further encouragement to obtain as much additional
work as possible, an alteration is proposed
in what is called the continuity period.
263 Unemployment Insurance Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr.
Robb]
At the moment, where. there are less than
three days proved unemployment in any
six consecutive days, such days do
not count for benefit purposes or towards
the statutory waiting period. Under the.
terms of the Bill it is proposed that where
there are two days of unemployment in
any six consecutive days those days
should count towards the waiting period
or for the purposes of benefit. The statutory waiting period still remains three
days as before.
In regard to the new class which the
Bill proposes to bring into the unemployment insurance scheme the House is aware
that non-manual workers at present cease
to be insurable if their rate of pay goes
beyond £250 a year. At the present
time more than ever a person in receipt
of pay which exceeds £250 a year suffers
real hardship if he becomes unemployed
and is thrown back on his own resources.
The present war has accentuated the
problem of this class, and it would be
most unfortunate if large numbers of
moderately paid workers should in the
present circumstances pass out of insurance through an increase in their pay.
The remedy which the Bill proposes for
this is to increase the insurance limit for
non-manual workers from £250 to £420.
The change is to take effect from the 2nd
September, 1940.
It is also proposed that the new class
should, as from 1st September, 1941,
become eligible for unemployment assistance.
The remaining Clauses of the Bill
are really administrative, and do not
affect the main purposes of the Measure.
I emphasise again that this Bill is doing
for us what has been done in Great
Britain under the Act just passed by the
Imperial Parliament, and it is necessary
that our scheme should be on parallel
lines.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think it is
possible for any thinking person to support this Bill at the present time. I am,
of course, quite aware that, as the right
hon. Gentleman has said, the Bill before
us is, so far as it goes, the counterpart
of a Measure which has been before the
Parliament of the United Kingdom. I
am not quite sure, I admit, whether the
Bill at Westminster has already received
the Third Reading and the Royal Assent
or whether it is still before the Second
Chamber.
Mr. ROBB: I believe it has passed
through both Houses, but I will verify
that.
Mr. LESLIE: There seems to be some
doubt as to whether the united wisdom
of the Parliament of the United Kingdom
has yet arrived at a solution of the
problems that suggest themselves to us
in connection with this Measure. I do
not think this is an occasion when we
should blindly rush into similar steps
with Great Britain. One has supported
before steps in that direction, and we
are all agreed that, so far as we can, our
social services should be kept on
parallel lines with those of Westminster;
but, to put it shortly, can we forget the
old fable of the frog and the bull?
Between the United Kingdom and ourselves there can be no doubt, I think, as
to which is the bull and which is the
frog.
We know that hitherto the Treasury
of the United Kingdom has been able to

war may have we do not know. How

Passing from the general proposition to the Bill itself, and going
through it briefly, I propose to take
the Clauses separately, or rather to
bestow a little attention on the
Clauses individually, because the Bill
seems to me to be a Bill which is soluble
into its component parts, that is to say,
it will be possible for the House to assent
to the Second Reading and to attack
individual Clauses in Committee.
The Bill involves, first of all, an increase of the rates of benefit, with the
natural corollary of an increase in contributions. That is one problem. The
extension of the continuous periods of
unemployment is another problem, and
the introduction of the additional
Clause relating to black-coated workers
is yet another problem. The rest of the
Clauses are complementary. So it would
seem practical, if the Bill is given a
Second Reading, to divide it by rejecting this or that proposal which the Bill
contains. I put that in rather unnecessarily as an introduction to the procession
in which I propose to ask the House to
accompany me through the various
Clauses.
In Clause 1 we have the proposal that
the benefits should be increased. I am
quite sure that those people who are
going to obtain the increase in benefit
will be very pleased to get that increase.
That I do not question for a moment.
But whether this is the proper time to
confer that increase seems to me to be
open to question. As we know, the cost
of living in this month of July is higher
than it was in the month of June, but,
if we
are to have any regard to the
figures, the cost of living in the month
of July, 1940, does not exceed the cost
of living in the month of July, 1914,
by anything like the same proportion
that the cost of living in the month of
July, 1920, exceeded that for the basic
period of July, 1914.
Moreover, during the last war, if I
remember correctly, the Exchequer was
not contributing at the rate of some sixty
millions a year, in order to keep the
cost of living in respect of the purchase
of essential commodities, principally
food, down as it is at present. At
present, if I understand the recent pronouncement at Westminster, the Exchequer is paying about sixty millons a
year in order that the cost of food may
not rise to an abnormal point. Consequently, perhaps, the present figures
compare extremely favourably with the
relative rise in the cost of living during
the late war, and in face of that it is
proposed to impose still further expenditure in order that the additional benefit
may be paid.
It is all very well to help people to
meet such a rise in the cost of living as
there is now, but must we not look a
little forward? Does the old maxim
respice finem no longer have a meaning
in the present day? What is going to
happen when the war is brought to a
successful conclusion? Is there not going
to be, to use a common expression, a more
almighty slump this time than there was
before? And, if so, what is going to
happen? Does anybody really believe
that at the conclusion of this present war,
which is costing so many millions, our
social services will be able to continue on
their present basis? We know there must
be severe unemployment in many of our
267 Unemployment Insurance Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Leslie]
industries. We know also that the
capacity of many employers to employ
labour must be seriously diminished. We
must know that the sources of taxation
will be practically exhausted. We must
know also that the sources of savings from
which money can be lent will be very
seriously depleted. Is it possible to look
forward to the end of the war on the
assumption that the social services will be
able to continue on the same basis? And
if it is not are you really conferring any
benefit on the beneficiaries of those
services by raising the level of those
services?
Passing to the second Clause, that
Clause suggests, although this is not in
itself a very important matter, that the
extra burdens should be placed upon the
employers. Those burdens will scarcely,
I suggest, assist the employers in competing for foreign trade in the markets of the
world. I imagine that the burdens of our
employers at the moment for health and
unemployment insurance are the highest
in the world, and it is still desirable that
our trade interests should compete in the
markets of the world. Is it worth while
adding yet another straw to the back of
the already stooping, quivering, almost
collapsing, camel? Is that not what we
are doing? Just the other day we put
a fraction on the stamp to accelerate an
increase in old-age pensions. Is it not
rather unwise to do that?
There is a sub-clause there which deals
with the agricultural problem, where the
benefit is again to be advanced. So far
as I can tell-it is difficult to speak
positively because different parts of the
Province differ enormously in this matter
--the fixed rate of agricultural wages
recently adopted by the Board is rather
too high. I do not say more than that
because we do not yet know the prices
that will be obtainable for agricultural
produce, nor do we know how much agricultural produce there will be to market.
We cannot forecast the weather or the
yield. I believe I am right in saying that
the prospect of yield is satisfactory, but
as regards the weather none of us with
our experience of the past weeks would
venture to prophesy. We think the agricultural wage is rather higher than it
should be, and, in any case, the
amount a man can obtain by being
out of work is unreasonable when
compared with the rate of wages he can
obtain by being in work. I am not
anxious further to decrease the margin
of that proportion.
I spoke just now of the probability of
what is called a slump succeeding the
present war. I suggest that the
probability of that slump coming and
affecting agriculture is even greater than
it is in regard to some other walks of
life, such, for instance, as shipbuilding.
One can readily understand that after
the present war there will be something
in the nature of a boom in shipbuilding
if anyone has the money to build ships.
Agriculture after the late war suffered
a very serious relapse.
We are told that that is not going to
happen again. But your North of Ireland farmer is a man with a long
memory, and I am not at all sure that
he accepts at its face value the assurance cast at him from one Minister or
another, either at Westminster or Stormont, that that will never happen again.
I am not sure that the Northern Ireland
farmer is altogether prepared to accept
that. He will think that when the war
is over he will be left to bear the brunt,
perhaps when he has spent his resources
in growing a crop at a high rate of
wages, and then when he markets the
crop he will find the bottom has dropped
out of the market and he is at a very
serious loss.
Clause 3 provides that the three days
of unemployment shall be reduced to
two. One asks, why? I was not impressed by the explanation given by the
Leader of the House. He rather suggested that the idea of that was that it
would encourage men to seek employment.
Mr. ROBB: Yes, certainly.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not follow that.
If you cut the waiting period from three
days down to two days, what is the encouragement for the man to seek employment until the expiration of the third
day? It really is not here. If a man
who formerly could not get unemployment benefit for three days can now get
it after two days of unemployment, what
is the encouragement for him to work on
the third day! In any case, surely that
must mean something in the direction of
extra expenditure. I presume that is

Second Reading
the class referred to in the White Paper
as the continuity rule, although that is

May I remind the House that the old
theory on which this unemployment insurance was based-and I am now going
back thirty years-was that unemployment benefit was not to keep a man on
the full rate of his wage living, but was
to help those savings which his thrift
had acquired during his period of employment? I venture to suggest we have
departed from that very sound theory
very seriously for many years, and this
proposal is a further step on the downward path.
Now I come to Clause 4, dealing with
the introduction of what has been
described as the black-coated person, the
man whose income ranges between £250
and £420. I am going to suggest that
in Northern Ireland the man in receipt
of £350 or £400 a year is not altogether
comparable with the man in receipt of
the same income in Great Britain,
because salaries for that class of person
are rather lower in Northern Ireland than
in England. I am also going to suggest
that there is no excuse whatever for
bringing that class of man into a scheme
of compulsory insurance. Surely a man
of that standing in life ought to be
entitled to say whether he wants to come
in or stay out.
This is
is presumably a compulsory
scheme. I have. on previous occasions
mentioned my preference for a voluntary
scheme as opposed to a compulsory
scheme. The answer usually given is this:
If you leave these State insurance schemes
voluntary no one would go into them and,
therefore, they would be failures. I was
never able to appreciate why in a presumably
democratic community you
should use that argument if you are not
going to allow a man to choose for himself whether he will be insured or not.
Are you running your scheme on democratic lines or are you using powers that
are claimed in an obscure place. on the
Continent of Europe?
I do not think you can compulsorily
bring in a man on that salary if he does
not want to come in. That is a matter
which might be dealt with in Committee,
because it will be possible to cut that
Clause out of the Bill without doing any
great violence to the main principle.
Again, you have to remember as regards
that that your man passing up to the
£420 maximum is not always going to
stay there. He will, of course, come in
at the earlier figure of £250, and he
might come into the £420 class for a year
or more and then pass out. Then what
is going to happen to his contributions?
It is all very well to say that application
had been made by some people and some
organisations representing this class that
they should be included, but I do not
know any association which has the right
to speak for the whole of this class. I
cannot myself arrive by any mental
process at the conclusion that it is right
to bring such a person into this scheme.

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to say a few words in favour
of this Bill. I think it would be a
calamity if this House departed from the
step-by-step policy with Great Britain so
far as unemployment insurance is concerned. It would create a tremendous
amount of confusion. The industrial
workers of Northern Ireland are closely
linked with the workers of Great Britain.
They have the same organisations, and to
make any change in our insurance scheme
would be a great mistake and lead to confusion. Furthermore, we have always
been given to understand that the
workers in Northern Ireland would not
suffer on account of being separated from
the rest of the United Kingdom, and that
all social legislation would apply here the
same as in Great Britain.
I understand that this Bill has been
passed in the Imperial Parliament without any division. I heard it announced
on the wireless. Senator Leslie has represented that the Bill makes a great change
by bringing into insurance black-coated
workers, but I should like to point out
that an agitation has been going on for
a very considerable time to include this
class of worker in our unemployment
insurance scheme. There are a large
number of people who have a salary of a
little over £250 a year and who up till
now have been shut out of the insurance
scheme. No hardship is being imposed on
this class, because they are being brought
into the unemployment insurance scheme
not only for unemployment benefit but
also for unemployment assistance. Under
the existing insurance schemes the worker
is receiving considerable benefits. He
receives not only the standard benefit,
but unemployment assistance benefit as
well, and the black-coated workers who
are now to be brought under the scheme
will receive the same benefits as the industrial workers. It is a long time since
the limit of £250 was fixed, and it is time
it was raised. The sum of £250 is a
very low limit, and I think it was set in
1912 and has never been altered.
The Unemployment Insurance Fund has
really been losing money in consequence
of the fixing of the limit at £250.
There are many persons who rise to a
position which gives them over £250 a
year, and they then cease to be insured
persons. Such persons often neglect to
become voluntary contributors as I myself
did when I ceased to become an insured
person. There has never been any improvement effected in the condition of the
workers without someone saying we were
running a tremendous risk, and that it
would lead to bankruptcy. That occurred
when the old-age pension of 10s a week
was introduced, and it was said when the
unemployment insurance scheme was first
mooted. The unemployment insurance
scheme was virtually forced on the people
of the United Kingdom. Even the trade
unions were opposed to it at first, but
in the course of time they found it was
of great benefit.
It proved a benefit to none more so than
the Imperial Government, because during
the last war they accumulated a large
sum of money in the fund. When the
war was over and men from the Services
were sent back to industry and were
unable to find work the Government
were able to assist these men from the
large sum which had accumulated in the
unemployment insurance fund. That
saved the country a great deal of trouble
because otherwise there might have been
a revolution. It is no use saying that
this Bill will bankrupt the State. It
marks only a slight advance on the lines
we have been travelling for some years.
From time to time the unemployment
insurance scheme has been extended as
has, also the Widows', Orphans' and Old
Age Contributory Pensions scheme. We
have for a long time been gradually
273 Unemployment Insurance Bill-
25 JULY 1940

Second Reading
improving the conditions of the people.
until we have given them something to
fight for at the present time.
It is a wise thing to have the workers
contented. We have a very fine class of
workers in Northern Ireland, and they
should be provided for in case of hardship or unemployment. Senator Leslie
has spoken of a slump after the war, but
we cannot tell what the conditions will
be then. There may be no slump at all,
and the Imperial Government are doing
their best to avoid rises in wages and
costs in order to prevent such a slump
taking place. Let us hope we will emerge
from the war victorious. That is the
principal thing. By including blackcoated workers in the unemployment insurance scheme the fund will receive a
large sum out of which the Government
will be able to assist the contributors if
a slump does take place.
So far as the industrial workers are
concerned it is very necessary that we
should move step by step with the
workers across the water. If we do not
do so we shall be in a difficult position.
We have got to consider our re-insurance
agreement with the Imperial Government
which will operate only so long as we
pay benefits similar to those in Great
Britain, and it would be a calamity to
depart from that policy.

after this war is over.

a higher wage and a higher unemployment insurance contribution, and I do
not know that he will get a higher price
for his produce, especially having regard
to this climate of ours. I would like to
know if he is going to benefit by this
Bill.
Mr. ROBB: I hope he will, because
the object of this Bill is to try to keep
labour satisfied. If you have a dissatisfied
class of labour you are not going to foster
the development of your industry. Under
this Bill the farmer will have to pay a
halfpenny per week more in respect of
each person he employs. This Bill does
not increase the wages a farmer has to
pay. I cannot say that taxes will be
increased as a result of the Measure,
because any deficit will be made up under
the re-insurance agreement, but that is
a very remote possibility. If the workers
here were treated differently to their
fellow workers in the rest of the United
Kingdom they would have a grievance.
If that grievance is removed they ought
to be a more contented class, and a more
contented class of employee is all the
better for industry.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not know that I
have very much to say. I congratulate
the hon. Senator on my left (Mr. J.
Cunningham) on being a little more upto-date in his information than the right
hon. Gentleman, the Leader of the
House. I gathered that Senator
Cunningham listens to the broadcasts
during the evenings, and apparently the

we do owe to Senator Joseph Cunningham

was a matter we ought to know.
On the other hand, Senator Cunningham
rather left us under the impression that
as a result of the last war the Unemployment Fund did not pile up a large
debt. The right hon. Gentleman will no
doubt be able to correct me if I am
wrong, but my recollection is that there
is a very large debt on the Unemployment Fund; that it is now being paid off
by relatively small sums per year; and
that it is a condition of the arrangement
with the United Kingdom that that
method of reduction shall continue. If
it is proposed that that debt-and
Senator Cunningham did in fact propose

Passing to what was said about recruiting, I am afraid I cannot agree that
raising unemployment benefit will not
have a deleterious effect upon recruiting.
I am afraid it will. I have made

told, and unemployment benefit does, I
am afraid, militate against recruiting.
Now we are proposing to increase that
rate of benefit.
The right hon. Gentleman seemed to

we should object if the Treasury of the

Question to Minister.
SAVING OF FLAX SEED.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to ask the Leader
of the House whether any information
can be given as to the arrangements made
for ensuring the saving of seed from the
1940 flax crop.
The House will be aware, I think, of
the importance of ensuring a sufficient
supply of flax during the year 1941, and
perhaps later years, and the House will
probably remember that during the late
war-by which I mean the war that
raged from 1914 to 1918-it was found
necessary to issue a Defence of the Realm
Order requiring the saving of seed. It
was a matter of compulsion. Whether
any arrangements have been made for the
saving of seed for the flax crop next year
I confess I do not know, and I think it
will be agreed that if any such arrangements are made, or are to be made the
time has come when full information
should be given to those concerned. It
may very well be that the demand for
flax for strictly war purposes is not so
great this time as it was last. But at all
events flax is important if only for the
support of the export trade, and I
venture to suggest that any information
that can be given will be very thankfully
received and would, perhaps, enable some
effective action to be taken.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
Arrangements were made by the Ministry
of Agriculture. with certain flax-growing
companies in Northern Ireland whereby
they were supplied with the greater part
of all the certified seed of pure strain blueflowering varieties, so that they could.
save the seed from this year's flax crops
in order that it should be available for
sowing in 1941. These companies have
special facilities for seed saving, and in
the aggregate they should have seed from
5,000 to 6,000 acres. This would mean
that seed from this source would be available to sow 20,000 to 25,000 acres next
spring.
In addition, the Ministry of Supply
are, it is understood, prepared to purchase from farmers, other than the companies referred to above, flax crops of the
same and other varieties with a view to
saving the seed. It is not possible at
this stage to estimate the quantity that
will accrue from this source.
I will bring to the notice of the
Ministry the remarks of the hon. and
learned Senator.
Mr. LESLIE: I should like to ask the
right hon. Gentleman whether definite
steps will be taken to bring the matter
immediately to the notice of the
Minister concerned. The point is that
time is getting on, and if seed from this
crop is to be saved by the ordinary
farmer he ought to have notice at once,
and be acquainted with the terms on
which his seed will be taken over.
Mr. ROBB: I will bring the matter to
the notice of the Minister of Agriculture
at once.

CLAUSE 2 (Increase of rates of contributions).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie:-
Clause 2, sub-clause (2), after paragraph (b) insert as a new sub-clause:-
"(3) Provided that the rate of contribution shall not be increased under

Mr. LESLIE: I rather hope the Committee will not regard this Amendment
as a kind of hardy annual. I think that
this Senate, whether sitting in the House
or in Committee, is well aware by this
time that I have always opposed any
additional burden being placed on the
employer in respect of a man who cannot
obtain any benefit in respect of the unemployment contributions paid for him.
That is the proposal in the Bill before
us. The proposal is that after a man
has attained the age of 65 an additional
penny per week shall be paid in respect
of him. It is a halfpenny in respect of
the agricultural worker, but it is a penny
speaking generally, and to that principle
I think the Senate knows I have always
been opposed.
I have objected on several occasions,
and on this occasion I have something in
the nature of a new text, because in the
Bill before us we have Clause 4, a Clause
which I shall ask you to put separately
when the time comes. We have in Clause
4 a suggestion that a new class of man
shall come in, a man with an income of
from £250 to £420 a year. I am not
thinking of that at the moment, but I
am asking the Committee to recognise
that that man will some day or other
perhaps pass out of the £420 a year class,
and, therefore, his contributions will
drop. He will not get anything for
what he has paid, but that for the
moment is not the point. Why, if that is
so, should you not allow the other man
to pass out of insurance, not because he
gets £420 a year, but because he becomes
65 years of age. What is the argument?
The only excuse, so to speak, that
one can find is that in the one
he is in excepted employment, and in
case
the other he is excluded; that is using
these terms in the sense in which they
are used in the Act of 1936. In each
case, I submit, the conditions are the
same. The insured contributor
whom money is paid is the same.
man passes, has passed, or may pass out
of insurance and will get no benefit.
for
The
Surely at the present juncture the
employment of a man over 65 years of
age is not only lawful, but even laudable. If you can manage to find work
for a man over 65, surely you are
leaving free a man less than 65 in order
that that younger man may undertake
work of national importance, or perhaps
even serve in the Forces. If you can
encourage the employment of older men
surely that is a good thing to do, and
if that is so it is surely wrong to impose
anything in the way of an obstacle to
the employment of that man over 65
years of age.
Something has been said-it always
is said on these occasions as regards
that re-insurance agreement with Great
Britain. I have refreshed my memory
on the terms of that 1936 Re-Insurance
Act and I can find nothing in it which
will prevent our doing something off our
own bat, so to speak, a little different
to what they do in England. On the
contrary, Article 7 of that Agreement,
described as the Schedule in our Act of
1936, as in the earlier Acts, rather, to
my mind, points in the contrary
direction. What Article 7 says is:
-
If at any time the classes of persons
insured or of persons entitled to
apply for an unemployment allowance, the rates of contribution, the
rates or conditions of benefit or the
regulations under which allowances
are paid or the general method of
charging administrative expenses or the
methods of financing the Unemployment or Unemployment Assistance
Funds differ in Great Britain and Northern Ireland, there shall be made such
adjustment, if any, in the amounts of
the contributions from one Exchequer
to the other as the Joint Exchequer
Board, after consulting the Government Actuary, may consider just.
I submit to the Committee that that
Article plainly indicates the possibility
that variations in details of unemployment insurance may exist between the

Committee
two Parliaments and recognises, if I may
say so, that Northern Ireland has a Parliament of its own and is intended to use
it not necessarily in a slavish adherence to

equity in the suggestion that an employer
should have to pay for a man over 65
a contribution in respect of the benefit
which that man over 65 cannot possibly
get. A man over 65 cannot get

should pay a contribution in order that

that it is one to which Parliament
should not assent. Accordingly I beg to
move the Amendment standing in my
name.
Mr. ROBB: The hon. and learned
Senator has really put a case for the
Clause as it stands in what he has said.
First of all, might I remark that this is
not a temporary Measure? It is a
permanent Measure. The object of continuing the contribution of an employer
in respect of an employee after the man
has attained 65 years and ceases to pay
contributions himself is to discourage
employers from employing men over 65,
because they would thereby escape
the
contribution of 10d a week in respect of
that man.
In this code it has been
accepted both here and in England that
it is a good thing to discourage the
employment of these old men by continuing the contributions the employers
make after men have attained the age of
65 and women 60, although the employees
cease to pay their contributions.
men
an
The principle upon which that has been
accepted is that which I have stated,
namely to discourage the employment of
of that age.
If this were
emergency Bill to let off the employer
making the contribution when there are
so many unemployed young men might
be a very good argument, but this is not
a temporary Measure. It is a permanent
Measure, and is only part of what has
been the accepted principle of unemployment insurance in the United Kingdom
ever since unemployment insurance was
started.
I do not quite follow the second point
the hon. and learned Member made. It
was
with regard to the Re-insurance
Agreement touching on employment
between Great Britain and Northern Ireland. It is quite true that the Agreement
contemplated that there might be a discrepancy between the two countries, but
if there does happen to be a discrepancy
the accountants are to take account of
that and make such adjustments as will
meet the difference between the two
systems. If we make the difference it
mreans that we have to pay for it ourselves, not reinforced by the British
Government, but, as I say with regard to
this particular point-I am not now dealing with the form of the proposal-the
Amendment reads:
"(3). Provided that the rate of contribution shall not be increased
under this section in respect of any
person who had attained the age
sixty-five years at the commencement of this section."
of
If that form were adopted you would
in the future have two classes of people
of 65 years of age, those who had attained
65 at the passing of the Measure and
those who had not attained 65 at the
commencement of the section. Of course,
that is a mere matter of adjustment, but
the general principle remains, and the
adoption of the Amendment would be of
little benefit to the employer or workman
concerned and would have the disadvantage of being a departure from the
unity existing between the two countries.
This has been debated often both in
England and in this country. The principle has been accepted, and I can see no
valid reason why at this stage this Parliament should depart from that principle
which has been adopted ever since
unemployment insurance began. Therefore, the Government cannot accept this
Amendment.

Mr. LESLIE: My principal objection
to the insertion of Clause 4 is, as I think
I intimated on the Second Reading, the
application of the principle of compulsion,
that is, requiring a man whose income
derived from earnings is between £250
and £420 a year to be insured. The effect
of this Clause will be to say to that man,
"You shall be insured under the unemployment scheme whether you like it or do not.
You shall pay 10d a week, 10d a week
shall be paid for you by your employer,
and 10d a week shall be paid to the
general fund by the State. That is to
continue until you either fall into unemployment or you die, or until you get
Colonel T. Sinclair
Mr. H. Taylor
Mr W. E. Stevenson
an income exceeding £420. If you get
an income exceeding £420 you will pass
out of insurance. You will get no benefit
for your previous 10d, nor will your
employer have the satisfaction of knowing he has been contributing to your
maintenance."
I have always objected, as I think the
Senate. is aware, to the principle of compulsion as regards insurance. I have
always maintained that contributory
insurance was a far better means of meeting distress than poor relief, by whatever
name you may call it, but I always maintain that as far as possible entering into
insurance should be voluntary. I do
think that a man who is in the position of
earning between £250 and £420 a year is a
man of sufficient standing to be allowed to
exercise his own judgment as to whether
he will go into unemployment insurance.
just as he is allowed to say whether he
will insure his house against fire or himself against accident, or whether he will
insure his life. Those are ordinary
matters of insurance which people on
289 Unemployment Insurance Bill-

30 JULY 1940
Committee
that scale of income are accustomed to
consider for themselves, and I fail to see
any reason why you should make it
different in this case. It must be remembered that there are many progressive people whose income is between
£250 and £420 to-day, but who later
on may be beyond the £420 limit. So
much for that phase of the objection.

of the expense to the State. I am sure

the Re-insurance Agreement. I think that is so. The State,

Mr. ROBB: We all know that the
hon. and learned Senator objects to compulsion in toto. That objection does
not apply to these people only, but to
the whole scheme of compulsory insurance. There are people who have
objected to the scheme, but the
majority of people in the United Kingdom have decided that this is the best
way to meet the problem of unemployment here whatever it may be in other
places. The principle of compulsion has
been accepted and has been in force a
great many years. I suggest that it is
a little bit late to try to go back on that
decision.
The next point made was that even if
you are going to have compulsion you
ought not to have it upon people earning
between £250 and £420 a year because
they are progressive persons and some of
them will get more than £420 before the
end of their employment. If you are
going to apply that argument to the end
of the scale I suggest it probably applies
with greater force to the beginning of it.
If you do not increase the figure from
£250 to £420 you will find a great many
persons who are not now earning over
£250 will be doing so in a short time and
will be finished with insurance just in the
same way as other people when they
reach £420, the people for whom we have
such great sympathy. The scheme must
apply at both ends of the scale if at all.
Everybody admits that in any scheme
where you have a figure, maximum or
minimum, certain people will be adversely affected if their salary is increased
beyond the maximum or if it falls below
the minimum. That is not an argument
that should weigh very heavily with a
Committee of intelligent persons.
291 Unemployment Insurance Bill-

THE SENATE
Committee
[Mr. Robb]
In every scheme of this sort there are
people on the border-line. It is unfortunate that they should be at one side of
the border-line and not on the other. If
you have sympathy with the person who
got £418 last year and who will receive
£422 next year, then you must also pity
the man who gets £248 this year and who
will receive £252 next year.
That argument has equal weight at each end of the
scale.
With regard to the other point that
this is not a time when any extra burden
should be placed upon the State and that
under this scheme the State will have to
pay contributions in respect of a certain
number of employees, I may say I did not
know such a matter was going to be raised
and I have no figures before me as to
what it is going to cost. In England, as
well as here, the Government consider
that any cost to which the country is put
in respect of contributions for its own
employees will be well spent even at the
present time.
I think those are all the points raised
by the hon. and learned Senator-first of
all, that you should not apply compulsion at all; secondly, that there are people
who will eventually earn more than £420
and will go out of insurance, and, thirdly,
the cost to the State. I have answered
all those points. I am quite satisfied,
apart from any question of a reinsurance
agreement, that it is a good thing that
the example set in Great Britain should
be followed here and that the people
upon whom unemployment is likely to
press pretty heavily towards the end of
the present emergency should be brought
into insurance and thus be able to have
the advantage of unemployment benefit.
Mr. LESLIE: Of course that reply is
wholly speculative. I am afraid I must
invite the attention of the Committee to
a remark made by Rudolf Rassendyll in a
book entitled, if I remember rightly,
"The Prisoner of Zenda". Rudolf
Rassendyll is reported to have said that
there is more difference between £3,000
a year and nothing than there is between
£3,000 a year and all the money in the
world. I venture to suggest that that
argument may be applied to the point
before the Committee. There is all the
difference in the world between a man
whose income is £250 and a man whose
income is £400. That is a difference of
roughly £3 per week and as applied to
the class of persons within that scale it
is almost incalculable; it makes all the
difference in the world.
I cannot agree that because you have
adopted compulsion once and have taken
one step on the downward path that that
is any excuse for doing it again. You go
wrong in the beginning by introducing
compulsion and then you go on and on
and, in my view, greatly add to your
transgression. You cannot make out that
two wrongs make a right. The more you
increase your sin, if I may use the word,
the blacker that sin becomes. We have
no right to use compulsion in this matter,
and I cannot agree with the right hon.
Gentleman's argument. At this juncture
we have no right to increase the State's
expenditure except for the successful prosecution of the war. I am sorry to put
the Senate to the trouble of a repeat
division, but I cannot withdraw the
Amendment. It is not an amendment,
but I shall have to maintain my
opposition to the Clause.

Mr. ROBB: Might I make
remark? I did not intend to suggest
that the people of this country having
gone wrong once should go wrong again.
What I intended to say was that the
people of this country had decided that
compulsion was right.
in
A
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
should like the hon. and learned Senator
to consider the effect of compulsory
insurance in other directions. Compulsion
refers to insurance other than
reference to unemployment benefit.
short time ago a compulsory insurance
scheme for motor drivers in so far as
third-party risks are concerned was
passed. A large number of drivers
object to paying such insurance because
they are so careful they would not hurt
a dog or cat crossing the street. However, they are compelled to insure and
it is now necessary for all motor drivers
to take out a policy against third-party
risks. It is exactly the same with the
insurance we are now speaking about. It
really means that each person pays something into a pool. There are considerable
numbers of fortunate individuals who
never having been in difficulty did not
need to draw anything out of the pool,
but it provides for the unfortunate
individuals who through some error, some
293 Unemployment Insurance Bill-

30 JULY 1940
Committee
mistake, or even through no fault of their
own are compelled to seek support from
the pool. That is really what it amounts
to. I cannot understand why there
should be any difficulty in the matter,
because the income at which a man had
to be insured had been limited to £250
A
for a number of years. Many people are
drawing a little over £250 a year.
man with £252, £255 or £260 a year is
as much entitled to be insured as a man
who is earning £250. If a man is insured
he. is safeguarded should he unfortunately
lose his employment. If he is fortunate
enough to be constantly employed there
is no necessity for him to draw
any
benefit, but if he suffers some, set-back he
can get benefit. If you deprive these
black-coated workers of this safeguard
you will be treating them unfairly. This
Bill should be allowed to go through.
The Leader of the House has stated that
the principle has been accepted ever since
the insurance scheme was introduced.
When the scheme was first introduced
people who paid into it for a number of
years without deriving any benefit from
it thought they would be able to get something when they reached the age of 65
or 70. It was found that could not be
done.. A man with £200 a year,
although he may pay unemployment contributions all his life, may never get 1s
out of the Fund. However, he considers
himself lucky never to have had to ask
for benefit, and is quite satisfied that
another poor fellow when in need is able
to derive some benefit from the scheme.
Mr. LESLIE: I cannot agree entirely
with hon. Friend.
my
On my life I
cannot see that there is any parity whatever between motor insurance and this
class of insurance. The motor-car insurance to which he referred is third party,
and there is no third party in this
business.
Mr. ROBB: It is compulsory.
Mr. LESLIE: Yes, but it is for the

third party in this scheme, it is between

that if the man pays all his life and does
not draw any benefit he gets nothing out
of it, is one of the primary matters in the
consideration of the question of insurance. There was a scheme, as my hon.
Friend may recollect, which was adumbrated by the late Sir Walter Scott, who
died in the year 1832. He rather
fathered a scheme of this kind. When
a man who put his money into the pool
and did not draw any out it still stood in
his name and he got it by way of pension,
by setting him up in business or by buying a house for him. There is all the
difference in the world between the pool
and private insurance. I rather forget
whether Sir Walter's idea was that the
insurance should be compulsory, but I
think it was not. He ran a small scheme
of his own at Abbotsford, I think. I am
afraid it is some years since I saw it, and
I have. forgotten whether it was a compulsory scheme or not.
However, we have now to consider
whether the extension of insurance to the
new class proposed in this Bill justifies.
the application of compulsion. The
matter has not, so far as I know, been
effectively discussed in this country, and
I think the Committee ought to have an

upon it. It is a question on which
opinions do differ enormously, and it
would be extremely difficult to say on
which side the balance really rests. But
I shall continue to vote against the Clause
whatever others do.

Question to Minister.
GRAVE RAILINGS AS SCRAP.
Major DOBBS: Before asking the
question on the Order Paper I should
like to say that I am only referring to
a
the railings round the graves themselves
and not the railings round the churchyards or the graveyards. There are
great many graves in Northern Ireland
with railings round them, and if those
railings could be sent to the scrap
merchant they could be made into a
great amount of munitions. I shall, of
course, be told that you must not touch
anything in the graveyard.
It is
sacrilege. I suppose I may be even told
it is sacrilege to talk about graves and
graveyards.
It has always puzzled me why our
fathers and grandfathers erected an iron
cage, sometimes four or five feet high,
round their parents' graves, and having
done that they let these cages rot and
rust. I think most of us when we go to
church must go through the churchyard,
which is generally the graveyard, and
shut our eyes, otherwise we could not
fail to see the iron railings, some rusty,
some broken, some bent, and inside
these railings thistles growing and
dandelion and long unkept grass.
I sometimes go up to the County
Antrim War Memorial that overlooks
Belfast Lough, and what do I find
there? I find peace. I go sometimes
to the churchyard, and what do I find
there? I find desolation. Why can
not these churchyards be places of peace
and of beauty instead of monstrosities of
tangled metal and weeds and grass? I
would like to say to this House, cannot
you do something about it to clear out
these rusty, bent iron railings and make
the graveyards of our country places of
peace and beauty. I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can state
if, in response to the Imperial Government's appeals for scrap, any of the railings round the graves in the churchyards
of Northern Ireland have been taken
down and sent to the scrap merchants to
be made into munitions.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The returns of scrap metal furnished by
scrap merchants to the Ministry of
Supply are not sufficiently detailed to
show the origin of different descriptions.
of railings coming into the hands of the
merchants. There is, therefore, no
official data to show the extent to which
railings around graves in Northern
Ireland have been removed and disposed
of as scrap metal.
Suspension of Standing Order 302
Grave Railings as Scrap
13 AUGUST 1940
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of that
answer, has any endeavour been made
to get the responsible authorities to promote such a campaign. We all know, I
imagine, that the railings round the
graves in churchyards in the ordinary
sense are the property of the Representative Body, so far as the Church of Ireland
is concerned. I do not know what the
position may be as regards other communities. But it has occurred to me that
if a definite effort was made by the
Government-if we assume that the
Government is in earnest-to collect scrap
metal, clergymen and others interested
might become active agents in promoting
a campaign, and really a great deal of
good might result. There are
an immense number of railings which seem to
serve no useful purpose.
I do not know, but I imagine the
practice of enclosing graves with iron
railings dates back probably to the time
when wild beasts roamed the country and
there was some danger that the graves
might be disturbed. I think that is the
origin. That time has passed away, and
iron railings ceased to be useful. I think
they cannot be disturbed without the
consent of the responsible body, which
would probably only give it after consultation with the owner or person who
has the right to inter in the space enclosed by the railings. I would like to
ask has any endeavour been made to
secure that co-operation.
Mr. ROBB: I do not know, but I will
inquire.

Message from the Commons.
The House of Commons have passed
the Workmen's Compensation (Supplementary Allowances) Bill, to which they
desire the concurrence of this House.
303 Workmen's Compensation Bill

THE SENATE
Second Reading
Workmen's Compensation (Supplementary Allowances) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time." The
Bill is the counterpart of a Measure
recently introduced in the Imperial
Parliament, and which in respect of
Great Britain has now become law. I
may say a few words about the origin
of the Measure. Since the start of workmen's compensation in 1897 the general
structure of the scheme has, notwithstanding amending legislation from time.
to time, remained the same. There had
been repeated demands. from many
quarters for a complete overhaul and
recasting of the entire scheme, and in
Great Britain, towards the end of 1938,
a Royal Commission was appointed to
inquire into the operation of the existing
scheme, and to report on the need of its
amendment. This promised to be a long
task, and has inevitably been delayed by
the outbreak of the war, so that it will
certainly be some time before a report
can be expected. Furthermore, if substantial changes in the scheme as a whole
were contemplated by that report it
would doubtless be impracticable to effect
them during times of war.
However, growing evidence of dissatisfaction with some of the existing scales of compensation payment had become apparent,
and in consequence this interim Measure
was introduced in Great Britain, solely
as a war-time scheme for easing the lot
of injured workers pending the findings
of the Royal Commission.
The reasons for the introduction here
of what is to all intents and purposes a
copy of the British Act are these:
First, it has always been the Government's policy to maintain the same conditions of social legislation here as in
Great Britain, so that our workers may
enjoy the same benefits and privileges as
across the water. Second, as the great
majority of employers effect insurance
against workmen's compensation risks,
and as most of the insurance companies
who transact such business operate in
both Great Britain and Northern
Ireland, it is obviously advantageous and
convenient to have the same system in
both areas.
Regard has been paid in framing the
Measure to two important principles,
which are that in order to be capable of
being brought into speedy operation the
Measure must be simple and free from
administrative complications, and also
that it must not impose an undue burden
on industry. The method proposed is
that there should be an increase of the
weekly rates of compensation for any
injured worker, with a further increase
for the man with children up to the age
of 15.
I think it will be unnecessary for me
to enter into a detailed explanation of
each Clause, and that a simple illustration of the operation of the proposals
be more informative:-
may
Take a totally incapacitated workman
Iwith five children under 15 whose normal
wages were £2 10s per week. He receives
ordinary compensation of £1 5s. His
own supplement is 5s, two children at 4s
(8s), and three children at 3s (9s), making
a total of £2 7s. As the total weekly
payments, however, must not exceed
seven-eights of the average weekly earnings before the accident, the injured
workman can receive only seven-eights of
£2 10s, that is, £2 3s 9d.
It will be observed that there is no
means test attached to the allowances, and
that the allowances apply not only to
future accidents, but to accidents which.
have occurred since 1st January, 1924,
provided that weekly compensation is still
payable. If the worker is receiving compensation at a reduced rate because the
incapacity is only partial, then the supplementary allowances are reduced in
proportion.
It remains only to add that the present
proposals are the result of consultations
in Great Britain between the Government, employers, and trade unions, and
should commend themselves to all as
fair effort to meet the difficulties arising
from increased living costs. I beg to
move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time."
Question proposed.
a
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think anybody is going to object to the principle
of the Bill as outlined by the right hon.
Gentleman. As a matter of fact, he
might have defended the Bill, in so far as
it needs defence, by reference to pre-
305 Workmen's Compensation Bill
13 AUGUST 1940

Second Reading
cedents. I rather expected he was going
to do that, but he did not. But he did
follow closely the report I have seen of a
speech delivered in another place by the
Minister of Labour. In that other place
those who sit there were informed that
this was a war-time Measure, and I think
the right hon. Gentleman reiterated that
this afternoon. When we are asked to
accept this Bill as a war-time Measure
that, so far as it goes, is still in accordance with precedents, and the precedents
to which I refer are no doubt within the
recollection of the House. They are the
two Measures passed by the Imperial
Parliament in 1917 and 1919 respectively.
Compared with the effect of those
Measures, the Bill now before the House
is certainly not unduly generous to the
employee. Both these Acts of 1917 and
1919 were brought in as war-time
Measures, and the right hon. Gentleman
says this is a war-time Measure. Both
those Acts clearly expressed themselves
in terms that they were to endure for
the duration of the war. This Bill does
nothing of the kind. This Bill, brought
in under the guise of a war-time Measure,
seems to me to be an attempt to make
a permanent alteration in the law in
anticipation of the expected Report of
Sir Hector Hetherington's Committee,
and in the further expectation that when
the time comes the Imperial Parliament
will legislate accordingly.
There
We cannot gamble like that.
is no objection that I can see to making
something in the nature of a concession
to the unfortunate injured employee as
a war-time Measure. That I do not
object to at all, and I have already said
that the scheme before us is not, compared with the schemes of 1917 and 1919,
unduly generous. There is no objection
on that account, but I do think very
serious objection can be taken to introducing as a war-time Measure a Bill
which, according to its text, is not a wartime Measure at all but a permanent
Measure.
There is only one indication in the Bill
that it is intended to be reviewed at
a later date, and that is the reference
in the first Clause to "while this Bill is
in force." It is there that the unfortunate man who is injured gets his title
to receive this additional consideration.
His title depends on the fact that he is
you
in receipt of a weekly allowance during
the currency of this Bill. There is
nothing to tell us that when this Bill
ceases to operate his right to this supplemental allowance will disappear. That
can, no doubt, be corrected in Committee.
I need scarcely remind the House that
would not get over the difficulty
merely by repealing the Bill. You
would require special words in the enactment if it is the intention that a man
should no longer draw his supplementary
allowance. No doubt the right hon.
Gentleman will be able to tell us what
the intention is, if, in fact, any intention has been formed. If the House gives
this Bill a Second Reading-and I do
not suggest it should do otherwise-it
seems to me that in Committee we must
introduce words, and they will not be
very many, which will limit the currency
of the Bill to the duration of the war.
That was done, as I have said, in 1917
and 1919, and it ought to be done again
to-day if the Government is really honest
in the intention that this really shall be
a war-time Measure. If it is intended
to be a war-time Measure both Houses
of this Parliament have very good cause
to complain that that intention was not
made conspicuously plain in the text of
the Bill.
The Bill before us follows the English
Act, so far as I have been able to form
an opinion, as far as I can see
meticulously. I base that assertion not
on the Act, which I have not yet seen,
but on a copy of the English Bill which
I have seen, and I do not remember seeing in the newspapers any suggestion of
any alteration being made in the Bill.
I do not observe from the reports of the
proceedings in another place that any
attention was bestowed on the Bill there.
It seems to have been passed without any
discussion whatever, but that is a matter
which this House will scarcely regard as
being anything of a novel character.
That is not the point. The point is
whether, if we assent to the principle of
the Bill, we should not in Committee
introduce words which will definitely
limit the scope of the Bill to the duration of the war, which, I understand
from the right hon. Gentleman, is in
reality the intention.
As I have said, I do not object to the
increased allowances. The burden of
307 Workmen's Compensation Bill

THE SENATE
Second Reading-
[Mr. Leslie]
them would appear to fall, for the present
at all events, on the insurance companies
and not on the employer, because ninetynine per cent. of employers insure their
workers against accidents. I presume no
change will be made in the rates this

a
There are one or two smaller matters
that will arise on the Committee stage.
One is on Clause 4. I cannot observe
why it is suggested that these necessary
certificates should be supplied at
reduced rate. I quite see that nobody
wants to put more expense on the applicants for a certificate, whether for a birth
or marriage. That is a matter with
which we all sympathise, but it must be
remembered that these certificates have
to be supplied by registrars, and that the
fees obtainable are part of the living
wage of the registrar. Why is it suggested that he should be deprived of such
benefit as he might get from the issue
of these certificates? It does not seem
fair. At the moment a great fuss is
made about the increase in the cost of
living.
According to the Ministry of Labour
figures the cost of living to-day, although
undoubtedly higher than it was a year
ago, is not so much above the 1914 level
as was the case in 1920 or 1921. I do
not say a rise has not taken place; I know
it has, but it is not fair to give all the
consideration in respect of the rise in the
cost of living to the applicant in this
matter to the detriment of the unfortunate registrar, who is also affected by
the rise in the cost of living. That man
is entitled to fees, on the strength of
which he accepted the appointment. I
may mention, in case anybody wants to
look the matter up, that so far as I know
in our own Workmen's Compensation
Act of 1927 there is no suggestion that
certificates should be obtained at a rate
below the current price. This, therefore,
seems to be a new proposal to the detriment of the registrar, and it seems to be
an unfair proposal.
The only other point is this reference
to the 1st January, 1924. That did
occur in the English Bill, and I am
bound to say the date is not altogether
without history, and I shall ask the
House in Committee to alter it. It
seems to be rather ridiculous in the year
1940 to talk about an occurrence which
took place in February, 1924, out of
which the weekly allowance arose. It is
only with existing weekly allowances that
this Bill deals. Where the compensation
has been received in cash this Bill does
not operate, but it seems to me absurd
to carry on the possibilities of an accident
in February, 1924, to the year 1940.
The period is too long, and I do not think
Parliament ought to consent to it.
I will deal a little more with the
history of this particular date when the
Bill reaches the Committee stage. I do
not think it is desirable to dwell upon
it this afternoon. I do not desire to
oppose the Second Reading, but I want
on the Committee stage to make it quite
is
plain that this Bill
a war-time
Measure; I want to protect the unfortunate registrar, and I want further
consideration for this date, 1924.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: It
would be a great mistake if the Government decided that this is to be a wartime Measure.
Mr. LESLIE: We have been told it is.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: It is
a war-time Measure, because it has been
introduced in war time, but it is not
intended to limit it to the duration of
the war.
I understood the Leader of the
House to say that for years there has
been a considerable agitation to increase
workmen's compensation allowances. The
original Act laid down the amount which
each employee was entitled to receive. If
a person was killed I think his dependent
received the equivalent of three years'
wages as compensation. It superseded
the Employers' Liability Act, and it was
supposed to make it easier for workmen
to obtain the necessary compensation.
We have been informed that this scheme
was introduced in 1927. One would
naturally understand that the amount
then was much smaller than now, because
the three years' wages to-day would be
considerably higher than in those earlier
309 Workmen's Compensation Bill

Second Reading
days, and with the increased cost of living
it makes it even more difficult for these
people.

I am inclined to think the

Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I fail to see

Mr. ROBB: What I said in introducing the Bill was that this is an interim
Measure a war-time scheme for easing
the lot of injured workers pending the
findings of the Royal Commission. It is
an interim Measure until such time as
the Royal Commission have reported and
the provisions of this Bill shall be superseded. It is not a war-time temporary
Measure in the ordinary sense, and is not
intended to be. It is intended to be a
stop-gap until the recommendations of
the Royal Commission shall become known
and a Bill can be framed on them. With
regard to the charge for certificates, the
view that has been taken is that the
increase in business will well pay the
registrars who have not objected.
With regard to the point raised by
Senator Cunningham, where a man is not
in receipt of the maximum amount of
compensation, but in receipt of a reduced.
amount, the allowances are correspondingly reduced. Regarding the point
raised by Sir Joseph Davison as to insurance companies, the liability for workmen's compensation at present is on the
employer. It will be up to each employer
to arrange with his insurance company
where the money comes from. It will
involve almost certainly some increase in
premiums, but under the Bill it is left
as a matter of arrangement between the
employer and his insurance company.
Mr. LESLIE: And he has only a week
to do it in?
Mr. ROBB: Yes. There is plenty of
notice.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: The right
hon. Gentleman says it is left to the

also how the money is to be paid. Take

who is already benefiting to the fullest

additional £50 a year without bringing
in £50. I am speaking of persons who
are already receiving benefit under the

[Sir Joseph Davison]
existing Act, not persons who might by

Mr. ROBB: There is one point I forgot to mention; it is with regard to the

that Act those receiving compensation

the amount of compensation of those in

THE SENATE.

Thursday, 15th August, 1940.
The Senate met at a Quarter past
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.

Ministry of Home Affairs:
Public Health (Preservation etc. in

Regulations, 1940.
Joint Nursing and Midwives Council

Laid before the House (pursuant to

Question to Minister.
THE FLAX CROP.
Mr. MAGUIRE: With reference to
the question on the Paper to-day I wish
to deal with a few points. A very strong
appeal was made by the Government in
the early spring and also by the Minister
of Agriculture for farmers to grow additional flax. It was pointed out that imports had practically ceased, and it was
very necessary to grow more flax in order
to keep the spinning mills going. At the
present time some of our spinning mills
are closed down and others had to go on
short time. A good many of our farmers
responded to the appeal of the Government to grow a greater quantity of flax.
A great many of those farmers had not
grown flax before, some had not grown
it for many years and they had not the
facilities for retting it. But they
responded to the appeal of the Government on the promise that they would be
assisted by the installation of mechanical
flax pullers in the different areas in the
Province and also that the flax would be
purchased in its green state so that those
farmers who had not the facilities for
retting could secure a market for the flax
in its green state.
That promise has not been carried out.
Some of the farmers to-day have been
unable to sell the flax in its green state.
In confirmation of that statement I will
read a letter from the Ministry to a
farmer. It is dated 13th July, 1940:-
I am directed by the Minister of
Agriculture to refer to your recent
letter and to state that while. this
Ministry does not purchase flax crops
in the green state the Ministry of
Supply, through the Local Administrator, 53 Donegall Place, Belfast, is
prepared to make an offer for
green
flax provided it has been grown from
seed of one of the pure strain blue
flowering varieties. The following
firms, however, may be prepared to
purchase the crop of any variety in
the green state:-
The Flax Development Committee,
52 Donegall Place Belfast.
The Flax Production Committee,
King's Hall, Balmoral, Belfast.
Messrs. R. Stevenson & Son, Ltd.,
Moygashel, Dungannon.
The Dromara Flax Company, Woodford, Dromara.
In this connection I am to point
out that provided your crop is properly
retted and handled in the usual
manner, it is probable that you will
have a better financial return than if
you sold your dried flax straw as
mentioned in your letter.
Inquiries were made from three of
those firms, but none of them was in a
position to make an offer for the flax
in its green state. To quote an instance.
One case I am familiar with is that of
a farmer in my own district. He had
not grown flax for 20 years. He had
no dam or facilities for retting the flax.
He sowed five acres of pedigree seed and
had it ready for disposal in its green
state.
He made many efforts to dispose
of it. He approached several of the
private firms suggested by the Ministry,
but none of them would accept the flax.
The Flax Development Committee referred him to the local buying agent.
He endeavoured to get in touch with
him, but he was on holidays. I think
it is hardly the proper thing that holidays should be taken during the flax
purchasing season. There was no one
to represent him or to make any effort
to purchase the flax. Next he approached
the Production Committee. They said
they were not interested in any flax
other than that sown after May 18. I
The Flax Crop
The Flax Crop
THE SENATE
[Mr. Maguire]
think that is taking up a very fine attitude to distinguish at what period the
flax is sown. They were not interested
other than in flax sown after 18th May.
They referred the farmer to the Minister
of Supply. That Minister's agent said he
could not buy, but would be glad to see
the flax bought by a private firm. He
said he could not influence any person
whether to buy or not.
The next step was he got in touch with
another private firm, which said it was
unable to make an offer. The farmer's
position now is a very inconvenient one.
He has to pull the flax in the ordinary
way and if possible get a flax dam in
some part of the country in order to ret
it. That will entail great expense in
transport. As a last resort he advertised the flax and put it in the hands of
an auctioneer, but on the day of the
auction there was not an offer.
That is a position a farmer should not
be put in at the moment and it is hardly
the way to encourage farmers to raise
the crops necesary for the State. They
should be facilitated. I would like to
hear from the Government what steps
they intend to take to relieve this
position. With regard to mechanical
flax pulling in my area, there is not a
mechanical flax puller to be had, though
we were told that they would be installed
in all the areas in the Province.
I beg to ask the Leader of the House
whether it is a fact that large numbers
of farmers who responded to the Government's appeal to sow flax now find
themselves unable to dispose of the crop,
particularly in its green state, and if he
can state what steps the Government
propose to take to enable those farmers
to obtain a market for a crop which is
assumed to be of vital interest to the
industrial needs of the State.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The position is that the Ministry of
Supply has undertaken to purchase
scutched flax retted in the ordinary way
at fixed prices and also scutched unretted green fibre, and this undertaking
will, no doubt, be duly implemented.
With regard to crops of green flax, the
Government is under no obligation to
take them over. I understand, however, that in certain cases, with a view
to seed saving, the Flax Control, on
behalf of the Ministry of Supply, is
prepared to purchase the green dried
straw from such crops when satisfied that the crops were the produce of
pure strain seed and suitable in every
way for seed purposes. In the circumstances, no action such as suggested in
the latter part of the question appears
to be called for.
May I say, I shall bring to the notice
of the Minister of Agriculture and the
Minister of Supply what the hon.
Senator has said in asking the question.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause 1 after the word "entitled" ("of
this Act, be entitled'') insert the words
"while this Act is in force".
The Amendment on the Paper is an
attempt on my part not only to bring
the Bill into line with what I thought
from the speech delivered by the Minister of Labour in another place, and I
may add from a speech delivered in this
House, but also to bring it into line
with what I think ought to be done in
the present circumstances. First of all,
I must read to the House from
the Official Report what was said when
this Bill was introduced in another
place. The right hon. Gentleman, the
Minister of Labour, said:
This Bill
Bill is purely an interim
Measure which seeks to provide a temporary war-time scheme for easing the
lot of injured workers by providing
them with supplementary allowances.
-(Official Report, Vol. 23, 7th
August, 1940, col. 1904).
I put it to the Committee that that was
very clear notice that the Bill was a
temporary Measure. The Bill, as I
said on the Second Reading, does not
carry that out. In this House the right
hon. Gentleman (Mr. Robb) said:
However, growing evidence of dissatisfaction with some of the existing
scales of compensation payment had
become apparent, and in consequence
this interim Measure was introduced
in Great Britain solely as a war-time
scheme for easing the lot of injured.
workers, pending the findings of the
Royal Commission.
321 Workmen's Compensation Bill- 15 AUGUST 1940
Committee

think it is one on which stress should
have been laid in the other House of
Parliament.
I must press on the Committee that both
these speeches did suggest to us that the
Bill was a war-time Measure in
the sense that it was only to operate
during war time. However, the right
hon. Gentleman told us later that was
not what he meant. He meant a wartime Measure because it was introduced
during war time. That is not the same
thing.
I am bound to say that the Bill bears
out the explanation, rather than what
was definitely said in the House. I do
think when the Minister of Labour introduced this Measure he used words of
that quality, and I think the intention
was that he should be understood as
offering a a Bill for use during war time.
I do not think you can read this word
in any other way.
What is the difficulty, so to speak, in
accepting it as a permanent Measure
in war time? I said on Tuesday that
the terms offered were not unduly
generous to the employees as compared
with what was done during the last war
under the statutes I quoted, but I did
not go into any detail as regards the
novel principles brought before us in the
Bill. I think it will be agreed; in fact it
has been agreed in connection with this
Measure that no legislation of a controversial nature should be brought into
force, or brought under discussion during
war time, and certainly it is not very
desirable that anything approaching controversy should arise when the other
House of this Parliament is so seriously
attenuated by the absence of Members on
active service, and indeed that matter,
although not so acute here as elsewhere,
is one from which we suffer, as two of
our own Members are on active service.
What are the points of difficulty_that
arise in the Measure before us? I am
now speaking of Clause I. There is first
of all the introduction of an entirely
novel principle, that is to say, the dependant of an unfortunate workman who
has met with injury resulting in his
temporary incapacity should be regarded
as a fit subject for compensation. That
is quite a new principle. Hitherto, in
our Consolidation Act, 1927, the dependants of a workman got nothing unless the man was actually killed. Neither
the wife nor the children got anything.
This is an entirely new proposal, and I
It is obvious that if you are going to
allow the extent of compensation to
operate according to the number of
children a man has, you are putting a
premium on employing a man who has
no children or has a smaller number of
children. I think that is so, although it
is not a question one would like to raise
during war time, and one would not
indeed like to raise it at all. That is one
new point.
Another point arises out of that, as
the Committee will see if they refer to
paragraph (b) of sub-clause (1) of the
first Clause, that this compensation in
respect of children is limited to the
children of a male worker. If the person employed happens to be a woman
with children and she meets with an
accident resulting in temporary incapacity
her children will not get any compensation under the Bill whether there is a
weekly allowance
not. We are
distinctly limited to the case of a male
worker. If the word male had not been
there the situation might be different.
That is a proper matter for discussion
when there is time to consider these
matters. There has not been time to
consider these matters in the case of a
Bill brought in last Tuesday, and which
reaches its final stages on Thursday.
or
I do not see any particular objection.
to accepting this Bill purely as a wartime Measure-not as a Measure introduced during war time, but a Measure
to endure for war time. That would not
commit us to such an extent to the
bêtise of pre-judging the report of this
Hetherington Commission which has not
yet reported, and, for all I know, may
never report. It would not tie us up if
we limited ourselves to the duration of
the war.
If we do that we shall, during
the war,
which be of indefinite duramay
tion, not be getting out of step with Great
Britain. Although they have made it a
permanent Measure, and we have made
it a war-time Measure, we shall not be
getting out of step in that respect, at
any rate during the war, and we shall
have an opportunity of gaining experience
of how this thing works.

Mr. ROBB: The Government cannot
accept this Amendment. The Amendment, so far as this particular Clause is
concerned, is unnecessary, because the
Clause uses the words, "while this Act
is in force." Therefore, the Clause which
the Amendment seeks to amend is to
continue in operation only so long as the
Act is in force.
As regards the other point the hon.
and learned Senator made as to
whether this Bill should or should not
be expressed as a war-time Measure, I
think that, whatever may have
happened elsewhere, I made it quite
clear in this House that this Bill was
not an emergency Measure to operate for
the duration of the war, but that it
would continue in force pending the
amendment of the Workmen's Compensation Acts, based on the judgment of
Parliament upon the findings of the
Hetherington Commission.. This Bill
would not have been limited in the way
suggested, because experience has shown
that these limitations are in fact
ineffective.
In the last war there were two Workmen's Compensation Bills, passed in 1917
and 1919 respectively, and those, were
expressly enactments to continue in force
during the war and for six months afterwards. It was not possible to get rid of
them within the period of six months
after the war, and those Acts had to be
continued in the Expiring Laws. Continuance Act, 1921-22. It was thought
foolish to run the risk of having to do
the same thing again, and it is the
intention of the Government that this
Bill should continue to operate until
there is a general revision of the law
based on the findings of the Hetherington
Commission. In any event, this particular Amendment is unnecessary on this
Clause.
Mr. LESLIE: Taking the last point
first, I am afraid I cannot agree with

in force had to be connected with the
man's title, which arises from weekly
payments. I think the reference is to
while the weekly payments are in force.
We really are in danger of quarrelling
over a comma. As I read it, this Clause
does not support the theory I am putting
forward that this should be a war-time
Measure.
There are new proposals in the Bill
which I think are of importance. If you
accepted this proposal as permanent you
would almost be driven to paying a man

children he has. It is a proposal that has
always been put forward by those who
are avowed Socialists, and opposed by
those who are not. Much as I regret
causing any form of disturbance, I cannot withdraw the. Amendment.
