Mr. LESLIE: The proposal is, as I
intimated on Tuesday, to leave out the
Clause. I cannot see any reason why
these unfortunate registrars should be defrauded of part of their fees. The right
hon. Gentleman said, I think, that they
had raised no objection. I do not know
if they have been definitely consulted, but
there is the answer for the one-hundredand-one registrars around the country.
They are entitied, I think, to be consulted. I do not think we ought to introduce as a new principle a compulsory
reduction of these fees.
The country registrar does not make
much out of it. He has to work pretty
hard when he has anything to do at all,
which is, perhaps, not as often as it might
be, but he has a right to the fees prescribed, not by choice or by the Ministry,
but, if I remember rightly, by Section
twenty something or other of the Act of
1880, which I do not think has been
altered. Anybody registering a birth
can get a certificate for 3d. If he asks for
a proof of registration at the time of
registration he can get it for 3d. I do
not think it is a question of the actual
fee which, I think, is 3s 7d. This amount
includes the search fee.
I
What is to happen to the search fees
under this Bill? Is a man beside losing
the difference between 2s 6d and 6d to
lose the 1s search fee? That fee amounts
to something in the course of a year.
do not think we ought to do this
thing. We have not heard what the
registrars have to say, and there has
not been time for any wide consultations
on the point. We have done something of this sort in other cases.
think the workmen's industrial insurance
is one instance. In these cases you are
dealing with money paid by the State;
here you are interfering in what is a civil
matter, because the question of work-
I
men's compensation is something between
a man who is injured and his employer.
That Parliament should intervene to
the detriment of the registrars whose case
we have not heard is not fair, and consequently, I propose to ask the Committee
to agree that we ought to leave out the
Clause. When the general settlement, if
it ever does come about, on the report of
this Heatherington Commission is made
the matter can be reconsidered, but
during war time the registrars have to
face the increased cost of living, increased
work, and their regular incomes are being
depreciated.
I do not know whether it is expected
that the unfortunate registrar should
supply, at his own expense, the forms
referred to in sub-clause (2) of the Clause.
We are not told. He may have to get
them printed. Perhaps the right hon.
Gentleman can tell us, or are they going
to be supplied to him by the Registrar's
Office at the cost of the State? It is unfair to deprive the registrar of his fees
in order that an applicant may obtain.
an advantage which he or she has not
hitherto enjoyed. I beg to move the
deletion of the Clause.
Question proposed: "That the Clause
be left out."
Mr. ROBB: The Government cannot
accept this Amendment. It is not really
unfair to the registrars. This Bill is going
to give them possibly a large increase in
their work, and if they get paid the fees
set out in the Bill they will be very well
paid. It will be extra remuneration for
them, because there will be an extra demand for certificates. With regard to
the search and so on, all they are going
to have to do is to make out a certificate
of the birth of a child under fifteen years
of age.
In practically every case the
parents of the children, at least the
mother, can tell the date. Therefore,
there will be no search. The registrars
will be very well paid for all the work
they will have to do.
We
This is not a new principle. The hon.
and learned Senator started by saying we
were introducing a new principle.
are not. This principle has been in
operation with regard to widows' pensions, unemployment insurance, and the
Factory Acts for many years, and the

Committee
Mr. LESLIE: I will not move that
Amendment as it is practically covered

Amendment not moved.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Subclause (5), to leave out the words
"twenty-four" and insert the words
"thirty-five". This is really a matter of
comparatively small importance, but it
does raise some sort of principle. I
mentioned it the other day and the right
hon. Gentleman directed our attention
to the Act of this Parliament of 1923,
and also the Act of the United Kingdom
of 1923 dealing with the same
matter. Both have the same date-
1st January, 1924-for the commencement of the Act. It went on very
nicely until 1925 when in the United
Kingdom they put a Consolidation Act
into force on 1st January, 1926, and they
retained the old date of 1924. When we
undertook to consolidate the law of workmen's compensation in 1927, we were
faced with the duty of consolidating, and
this Parliament, I think properly, maintained the old date of January, 1924. It
might be there would be some cases in
which the date would be of importance.
a
Now we have arrived at 1940. I do
not think we can carry the old records
back much longer. One of the objects
of the Bill before us is to secure
supplementary allowance for legitimate or
illegitimate children of a male workman
-I think the expression is male workman-up to the age of 15. If the accident
happened in 1924 that child of 15 would
be out of it. In any case the time has
come when we ought to get rid of that
1924 date and insert some other one. We
cannot, I think, agree that we are to be
bound to this 1924 date for ever and
ever. You have the question of the
weekly payment being still current. It
is unlikely it would be current for a
period stretching from that time. This
would be a suitable opportunity to put
in some more recent and more manageable date.
I
I have suggested 1935. That would
give you between five and six years.
think that is long enough. If we do
not make some stand now after this Commission's Report, if we ever act on that
Report, we shall be faced again with the
question of maintaining the date of 1st
January, 1924. I do not think it is a
desirable thing to do. It is purely a
matter of discussion whether we should
not leave out 1924 and insert 1935, or any
more recent date than 1924, if supported,
would meet my views. However, I do
think the time has come when we ought
to bring ourselves a little more up to date.
We cannot go on for ever on the date
inserted so long ago as 1924.
Question proposed, "That the words
'twenty-four' be left out and the words
'thirty-five' inserted."
Mr. ROBB: The explanation for the
year 1924 is that any workman receiving
compensation for an accident which happened before 1st January, 1924, is receiving payment at a higher rate. There is
some point in what the hon. and learned
Senator says about a child being bound
to be 15 years of age now. However,
this is the date which appears in the
English Act, and it is the logical date in
the sense that a workman receiving compensation for an injury sustained before
1st January, 1924, is, in fact, receiving
a higher rate and, therefore, does not
need this supplementary allowance.
Mr. LESLIE: Any good that can be
effected will be effected by the mere fact
that the matter has been mentioned during discussion. When we deal with the
matter again the point will be reconsidered. It is, of course, as I reminded
the Committee, quite possible that a
weekly payment may still be running
from 1924, but it is unlikely, and so on
down the years I think we ought to deal
with the matter. It is not necessary to
Ideal with it to-day. Therefore I ask
leave to withdraw the Amendment. The
right hon. Gentleman will remember the
point.

Mr. ROBB: Before moving the Third
Reading of this Bill I should like to
answer a question raised on the Second
Reading by Sir Joseph Davison, whom
I am now glad to be able to refer to
as my right hon. Friend. He expressed
the fear than when a workman who is
already injured becomes entitled to the
additional allowances the employer's
insurance company may not pay the
additional sum unless the employer reimburses them by a similar amount.
been
Companies will, of course, have to recoup themselves for the increased
liabilities created by the Bill, and this
will be done by a percentage increase in
premium rates or adjustment of rebates.
or commission. Inquiries have
made from three typical companies-one
representative of the tariff companies,
one representative of non-tariff companies and one of the mutual associations and these show that the companies in return for the increased
premiums or adjustments will indemnify
their clients for the increased liability
not only for accidents which may occur
in future, but also for those which have
occurred already. The percentage of
the increase in premium or the nature
of the adjustment may vary from company to company, but these will be dealt
with in circulars which they are preparing for issue to their clients.
I may say that in England at a conference between representatives of the
insurance companies and employers a
gentleman's agreement was arrived at
with which the employers were satisfied.
This refers particularly to industrial
employers; the ordinary private householder with a comprehensive policy
covering his domestic staff is not likely
to have his premium rates disturbed by
this Act. It was a proper question to
raise and that is the reply the insurance companies are prepared to indemnify employers with regard to the past
as well as to the future at a rate with
which the employers in England did not
quarrel. I beg to move, "That this Bill
be now read the Third time."
Mr. LESLIE: We are asked to pass
a Bill imposing on employers in this
country a burden we are not able to
assess. We do not know the charge,
but it will probably be the same as in
England.
Mr. ROBB: They are the same companies.
Mr. LESLIE : There are domestic
establishments which contain men servants, but fewer are now employed.
There is also the farm worker in the
country. There are many small country
jobs which can scarcely be called industrial and they will be affected. The
adjustment of the premium may, perhaps, be a matter of some little difficulty,
although I cannot imagine-I do not
know what the rate of premium is
likely to be. This is one of the matters
we should have more time to consider.
I should have liked to consult my own
insurance company before speaking on
this Bill, but I cannot oppose the Third
Reading now. We have spent nearly
two-and-a-half hours discussing this Bill
-we have not spent two-and-a-half
hours, but we have spent some time on
it. I can only renew my protest at
being asked to deal with a Bill, which
is not altogether a simple one, at such
express speed. I hope in future. we
shall receive consideration in a matter
of this kind.

THE FLAX CROP.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That
this House do now adjourn until such
date as Mr. Speaker may appoint."
Mr. MAGUIRE: The object I have
in raising this matter on the Adjournment is that the Leader of the House
may be able to give some assurance to
farmers in regard to the sale of the flax
crop. The right hon. Gentleman stated
that the Ministry of Supply were under
no obligation to purchase flax in the green
state. In face of the letter I read from
the Minister of Agriculture that is
hardly consistent.
Mr. ROBB: Would you read it again?
Mr. MAGUIRE: Yes. It reads:
I am directed by the Minister of
Agriculture to refer to your recent
letter and to state that while. this
Ministry does not purchase flax crops
in the green state the Ministry of
Supply, through the Local Administrator, 53 Donegall Place, Belfast, is
prepared to make an offer for green flax
provided it has been grown from seed
of one of the pure strain blue flowering
varieties. The following firms, however, may be prepared to purchase the
crop of any variety in the green state.
Then follows the names of the four
firms.
In face of that letter I am surprised that
there was no obligation on the part of
the Ministry of Supply to purchase the
crop. The. Leader of the House
mentioned that the farmers were misled.
If that is so it is up to the Government
now to endeavour to put the farming
community at ease on this matter and
clear up any misunderstanding that arose
in the early stages when farmers were
told to grow more flax.
With regard to mechanical pullers, as
I said none were installed in my district.
The Leader of the House stated that
there was difficulty in getting supplies
from the manufacturers. I understand
local engineers in the city have been
manufacturing mechanical flax pullers.
If that work was expedited, perhaps even
at this late. hour the Ministry might be
able to install a greater number in districts which are without flax pullers at
the moment. I have been in touch with
farmers who have grown flax, and it is
their opinion that they will cease growing
flax and make no effort to grow it next
year owing to the great inconvenience
and disappointments they have suffered.
The farmer I referred to in putting this
question spent almost 15s in telephone
calls in getting in touch with different
departments. I would like the Leader
of the House to give an assurance that
will be of satisfaction to the farmers.
Mr. ROBB: As far as I can gather
the difficulty that has arisen was due to
the fact that it was not apprehended by
everybody that what the Ministry of
Supply were prepared to buy was flax
retted and scutched, and that they never
made any offer to buy green crop. The
Ministry of Supply were going to deal
with the manufacturers of linen and
were to take from the farmers the
raw material for the manufacture of
linen. That was scutched flax. They
never contemplated, and it was never
contemplated, that they were going to do
The Flax Crop
The Flax Crop
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
the farmers' work-pull, steep and scutch
the flax. It was contemplated that they
would take the raw material for linen
manufacturers, and that they have done
and are prepared to do.
Difficulties did arise, it is true, about
the supply of mechanical flax pullers,
but those difficulties arose through a
shortage of supply. Seventy machines.
were obtained and these were distributed
as well as the Ministry could judge the
need to be from the applications
received.
It ought, however, to have been
possible to have the flax pulled by hand.
There is in fact in Northern Ireland at
the present time as large a population
in the county districts as in 1919-I
forget the exact year, but I think it
was 1919 when there were 110,000
acres of flax. This year there are only
45,000 acres. The farmers could have
got the flax pulled by hand without
mechanical pullers at all. The difficulties apparently have arisen because
people depended on selling the green
crop. The Government never undertook to buy the green crop or buy anything but the scutched flax.
Further, the farmers relied on being
supplied with mechanical pullers. These
machines were not forthcoming in
sufficient numbers to do all the work.
As I say, it ought to have been possible
to have the flax pulled by hand. The
difficulties that have arisen will, of
course, have opened the Government's
eyes to how people may misapprehend
undertakings, and I should hope that
these misapprehensions, if any should
occur in the future, will not be due to
any lack of definition of the offers the
Government makes.
With regard to
the letter which
Senator Maguire has read, it says that
the Ministry of Supply were prepared to
buy green crop grown from selected flax.
It goes on to say that if it is not from
selected seed, possibly four firms might
deal with it. These people declined, I
do not know for what reason, but, at
any rate, if it was grown from approved
seed, pure strain seed, which is the correct technical expression, then the
Ministry of Supply were prepared to buy
and are still prepared to buy it. If it
does not come within the offer the Ministry of Supply made, the farmers may
be under a misapprehension. They
have no grievance against the Ministry
in respect of what they have failed or
not failed to do.
I hope this discussion will direct
attention to the difficulties that have
arisen and that in the future these
difficulties may be cleared away, possibly
by a clearer definition of the liabilities
of the Government.
Mr. STEVENSON: Might I suggest
that the Government should do something to assure farmers in the outlying
districts that their flax, when saved, will
be bought by the Ministry of Supply.
The trouble has arisen in outlying districts where flax has been sown and
there is no co-operation and no machines
to pull the flax. There will be a ready
sale for that flax if it is saved, and the
Government might well give those
farmers an assurance that if their crop is
saved, even in the green state, there will
be a ready market and a good price.
That would certainly help to increase the
acreage.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I do
not want to be critical, but I think something should be done to remove the misapprehension and the disappointment
that exists amongst farmers on this flax
question. Surely it is possible for the
Government, even at this late hour, to
arrange with some of the companies
referred to to gather the flax, even if
they have to pay a little extra for it.
Flax is not something that can be done
without. The position is worse. now than
when the Minister of Agriculture
appealed to the farmers to grow flax.
Then it was thought that Belgium might
be in a position to supply some flax, but
now she cannot do so.
When you take into consideration the
number of women who are unemployed
as a result of the closing of some linen
mills and others being on short time
something should be done to relieve the
position. Surely the Government could
arrange, through the linen associations for
this flax to be gathered and see that it
is retted, either in the old or the new
way. I would like to emphasise that it is
necessary to save this flax both to provide
employment and to improve our exports.

Questions to Minister.
THE PRISON SHIP "AL RAWDAH.”
Mr. SPEAKER: Before the hon.
Member (Mr. McLaughlin) puts his
question I should like to point out that
the name of the ship to which he refers
is the "Al Rawdah”, and not
"Aldawdah" as printed in the question.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: This ship has
been called so many names that it is difficult to know what its real name is.
Mr. SPEAKER: If you substitute, the
letter "R" for the first "d" you will
be correct.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can give particulars of the
new prison ship, the "Aldawdah",
which the. Government have set up in
Strangford Lough, her original cost or
cost to Government; the name of the
firm from which she was purchased;
the cost of refitting as a prison ship;
the number of her crew, the weekly
wages they receive; the number of
warders and guards and the wages paid
to them; whether he can give the
details of the dietary, the number of
men confined in each cage or room,
with the dimensions of same; whether
he has made any provision for protecting the prisoners from war attack,
submarines or aircraft, and if so, how
and whether it is in accordance with
international practice to place the lives
of untried prisoners in special peril
such as a prison ship, during the course.
of the world's greatest war.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: As representing the taxpayer I am entitled to an
Oral Answers
Oral Answers
24 SEPTEMBER 1940
answer to this question. It must be
remembered that it is not the members of the Government as individuals,
but the Government as representing
the taxpayers who are responsible
for the cost of the ship, its upkeep
and the wages of warders, sentries,
etcetera. Therefore, they are entitled to
know and to be kept informed of the
cost. The taxpayer, the public, as well
as the friends of these internees, are
entitled to know all about the treatment
these men receive, their dietary, number
confined in each room, or cage. I hope
their treatment will be better than that
meted out by this Government to the
internees on the last prison ship, the
"Argenta."
We all know that on that ship the
unfortunate internees-you cannot call
them prisoners because no charges were ever
brought against them, so that they could
be tried before a judge and jury-were
obliged to sleep in cage-like beds, one
above the other. Forty men were often
herded together in one cage of wire 45 feet
by 15 feet. In that they had to eat and
sleep, most of the time minus tables.
They just squatted on the ground like
Chinese, with their plates of porridge on
their knees. They were not provided with
a bit of sheet to cover them, or to lie. on.
Old shoddy dark brown rugs cast off by
the British Government were provided for
blanket and sheet. They had no pillow
case, in fact nothing white or cleanlooking upon their so-called bed, and in
eighteen months they had not even a
change.
There was the smell of rotting water
continually coming from the hold of the
"Argenta". Sometimes it was pumped
out, but more often it lay for weeks on
end. When all the men were closed in at
night there was a disagreeable stench. It
was felt in a striking way when the men
left the fresh air of the upper deck and
descended at night below the hatches.
After about an hour's saturation they
settled down to accept it, as their lungs
had by then become filled with the fetid
atmosphere.. Hardly a day passed by
that a man was not transferred to
hospital, ill with the conditions. Sometimes he was discharged if it was clear
that his health was impaired. If it was
not he was brought back for another
course. On one occasion a passing cargo
steamer almost rammed the. "Argenta".
Mr. SPEAKER: I am sorry to interrupt the hon. Member, but is he referring
to the ship at present in Strangford
Lough?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am referring
to the "Argenta," and I am expressing
the hope that the internees on this latest
prison ship will not get the same treatment as did those on the "Argenta.'
After that incident the "Argenta" was
taken from Belfast and brought to Larne.
Any ship fixed stationary to-day is in
grave danger of being bombed and
machine-gunned from the air. She becomes a fixed target for airmen. Surely
the Government cannot suggest they can
guarantee to safeguard a stationary ship
from air attack. As for Sir Dawson
Bates's undertaking to Alderman Byrne
on the 2nd inst., "that anyone interfering with the 'Al Rawdah' will get a rude
awakening," it is all moonshine. The
Government know quite well that they
not alone cannot guarantee the safety of
this exposed stationary ship, but they cannot even guarantee their own safety or
the safety of this building from the air.
Here is an extract from a letter I received
this morning from an ex-serviceman:-
"We, the citizens of the Six Counties,
protest strongly against the internees
being placed on board the ship 'Al
Drawdah,' as it is against international
law to have them interned on a
stationary ship during war time. Also
it will injure them mentally and physically to think of these men and boys
locked up at night and, perhaps, they
will be bombed from the air. The enemy
gives our British prisoners camps to
stop in, not a floating prison ship. I
have a son in this prison ship. I am
an ex-serviceman, who fought in the
Dardanelles and France, and I have a
son serving in His Majesty's Forces.
So now I call this a disgrace to have
Irish men and boys in such danger.'
""
I suggest that if and when this Government bring these men and boys to trial
the only charge they will be able to formulate against them will be "they are
Irishmen."
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): The
ship to which the hon. Senator refers has
been obtained on charter by the Government of Northern Ireland from the
Ministry of Shipping, but the terms of
the charter have not yet been finally

CONTROL OF TERRITORIAL
WATERS.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House
Whether the Six-County Government
have entire control of the territorial
waters of Northern Ireland including
Strangford Lough, and, if so, by what
instrument were they conveyed.
over
Mr. ROBB: The Government of Northern Ireland have jurisdiction, for the
purposes of peace, order and good government,
the territorial waters of
Northern Ireland, including Strangford
Lough. These powers are derived from
the Government of Ireland Act, 1920, and
other statutes passed by the Imperial
Parliament.
ADVISORY COMMITTEE ON
INTERNED PRISONERS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can give the names of
the Advisory Committee on Interned
Prisoners, and whom they represent;
whether he can state how soon after an
internee applies to appear before the
Advisory Committee the Committee sits
to consider the case and have the internee
before them; and whether the internee is
allowed to have legal advice, or a legal
man or friend to appear before the Committee on his (the internee's) behalf.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In putting this
question I think it only right to point
out that these interned young men are
all respectable, upright, decent fellows
whose characters are above suspicion. I feel
quite sure that charges would have been
brought against them long since had the
Government been in a position to frame
any, or had they any evidence on which
to base a charge. Meantime they have
been deprived of their liberty, and because they would not sign an undertaking practically admitting that the
Government were right in arresting them
they have been interned and placed on a
stationary ship, the "Al Rawdah," in
Strangford Lough. Everyone knows
that one cannot get on a ship the
necessary exercise to keep one fit and in a
normal state of health. Surely the public
and the friends of these men are entitled
to have a visiting committee representative of all parties to look after the
internees' welfare and to report from
time to time. Surely these men are
entitled to the privilege of even an
ordinary prisoner, who must be charged
and brought before a court of summary
jurisdiction within fourteen days.
This
I have known of cases where some of
these men applied to have their cases
considered. Weeks went by before they
even got a reply, and more weeks went
by before they were directed to appear
before the Advisory Committee.
Advisory Committee was set up by the
Government. It represents only one
party, the Government, and nobody else.
When these men appear before this Committee I understand they are not allowed
legal assistance or a friend to appear on
their behalf. They have not got one
friend in court, but, without any experience and without a friend to advise
or comfort them, they must answer all
questions put to them. They are at the
mercy of this hostile Advisory Commit-

tee, the members of which can put any
questions they please, and treat them as
they think fit. No matter what happens
these men who, mark you, have not been
charged with an offence, have no redress
or means of bringing their plight before
the public as is the case even with the
ordinary criminal.
Again it is a well-known fact that
ninety-nine per cent. of these internees.
are Catholics. This alone is creating a
very disturbing atmosphere. I have
known cases of Catholic families living
in Protestant districts whose sons
amongst those interned, for what reason
the parents, friends, or the men themselves do not know.
are
Mr. ROBB: The Advisory Committee
to which the hon. Senator refers has
been appointed by the Minister of Home
Affairs for the purposes of Regulation 23
(b) of the Regulations made under the
Civil Authorities (Special Powers) Acts
(Northern Ireland). The membership
consists of His Honour Judge Begley,
K.C., who acts as chairman, together
with two gentlemen who have been
appointed to the Commission of the Peace
for the County Borough of Belfast.
These gentlemen have been appointed as
impartial advisers, and not as representatives of any section of the community.
It is not possible to fix a time limit
within which representations made by
interned persons can be brought before
the Advisory Committee, as each case has
to be prepared and presented for consideration separately, but it is the
practice of the Ministry of Home Affairs.
to deal with all applications as expeditiously as possible.
In regard to the last part of the hon.
Senator's question, interness may not be
legally represented before the Advisory
Committee, nor may friends appear on
their behalf. I would point out that the
functions of this committee are to elucidate the facts of each case brought before
them, and that this can best be done by
direct conversation between the members
of the committee and the internee himself.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: The Leader of
the House has suggested that an internee
is allowed to prepare his case for submission to the Advisory Committee.
Mr. ROBB: I believe that is so.

351 Agricultural Wages Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time". The
reason for the hurry in connection with
this Bill is that it deals with the regulation of the wages of scutchers, and as the
scutching season is upon us as little delay
as possible is desirable. The situation is
this; that the price of flax has been
fixed, but there is no machinery at the
moment for fixing the, wages that are to
be paid to flax scutchers. When that was
brought to the attention of the Government they consulted the farmers and the
trade union which represents these men,
and they were quite agreeable to the
course the Government proposed.
was that instead of setting up a separate
Trade Board to regulate the wages and
conditions of flax scutchers, flax scutchers
should be brought under the control of
the Agricultural Wages Board. That is
what this Bill does in the first sub-clause
of Clause 1.
That
The second sub-clause of Clause 1 deals
with this matter. A person who is
employed in the scutching of flax and is
insured under the Unemployment and
Health Insurance Acts is to continue to
be considered under the ordinary scheme
of unemployment insurance and not
under the agricultural insurance scheme..
The second Clause deals with a matter
which is brought before Parliament at
the request of the farmers and farm
labourers. The. law at present is that an
employee is to get a holiday of half a day
a week, but it was pointed out that in
some cases this did not suit the desires or
convenience of workers in agriculture.
Accordingly, Clause. 2 says that in the
exercise of the powers of the Board to
regulate these matters, the Board shall
secure either a weekly half-holiday or,
if the employer and the worker so agree,
a holiday for the worker of one day in
respect of each period of continuous employment of two weeks' duration; or in
lieu of a weekly half-holiday, such other
arrangement of the number of working
hours as will suit the convenience of the
employer and the worker. It is a matter
of arrangement as between a weekly halfholiday, one day a fortnight, or a diminution in the daily number of hours worked.
Those are the two matters dealt with in
this Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: I am afraid I feel it
right to move the rejection of this Bill
by making the customary Motion as
regards leaving out the word "now", and
inserting at the end of the Motion moved
by the right hon. Gentleman the words,
"this day six months". It seems to me
that this is a very much more difficult
Bill than appears on the surface. As the
right hon. Gentleman explained it to us
it seemed a very simple matter, that is
to say, it seemed to deal purely with the
alleged necessity for raising the wages
paid to people engaged in the scutching
of flax.
Mr. ROBB: Not raising, regulating.
Mr. LESLIE: Yes. I am afraid I
shall have to refer to the word
"regulating" again. That is surely the
proper term to use, and the term on
which I hope we shall examine the Bill
before us-on the basis of regulation
rather than standardisation.
If the effect of this Bill is going to be
to raise the wage paid to the scutcherand I am afraid that is the necessary
effect and intention, and in spite of the
fact that the Bill purports to regulate I
believe the intention is to raise the wages
of scutchers from the present rate, which
varies in different places, to a higher
rate. I think that is the intention of the
Bill, however it may be expressed or
camouflaged. If there is to be a rise in
the scutchers' wage I think we are entitled
to inquire who is going to pay that rise.
Is it going to be the farmer who produces
the flax? Is that extra, whatever it may
be, to come out of the pocket of the
farmer? If I am right-and I hope the
right hon. Gentleman will correct me if
I am wrong the price to be paid for flax
grown during the current year, 1940, has
already been fixed.
Mr. ROBB: That is so.
Mr. LESLIE: Is it then the intention
that if the Wages Board decided that the
average rate at present in force should
be increased, that increase is to come out
of the pocket of the farmer; that is to
say, out of the price already guaranteed
to him, and I am speaking of the 1940
crop?
Mr. ROBB: What was feared was, not
that the wages would go up, but that the
scutchers would demand wages that the
farmer could not pay. The object of this
Bill is to regulate those wages so that
Agricultural Wages Bill— 24 SEPTEMBER 1940
Second Reading
the
wages that are to be paid will be fixed
not by means of a demand, supported
possibly by a strike, but by the Board.
The object is to keep them down, not to
raise them.
Mr. LESLIE: I am not endeavouring
to conduct this debate on the basis of the
wage to be paid; it is the regulating`
basis I am endeavouring to attack. If
the wage is to be paid out of the price
already guaranteed to the farmer it must.
surely mean that if that wage rises the
farmer will get less for his flax. In fact
I think the Government are putting the
cart before the horse. The question of a
rise in the scutchers' wage ought to have
been considered before the price of flax
was fixed. There is no getting away from
that. At the moment I believe the
farmer expects to get from the 1940 crop
£1 a stone. There is a question of a
half-crown rise,, but does that apply to
1940 or 1941 ?
Mr. ROBB: The price for 1940 is from
20s to 25s, according to grade.
Mr. LESLIE: That is the price for unscutched flax?
Mr. ROBB: Scutched flax.
Mr. LESLIE: You are quite right.
It may be remembered that we discussed
this question some few weeks ago. The
price to be paid to the farmer for scutched
flax is not half-a-crown extra, but 20s to
25s a stone. If that is so it follows that
if there is any rise in the wage of the
scutcher, with the resultant rise in the
cost of scutching, that 20s to 25s will be
reduced. That must be the answer.
Mr. ROBB: That is so.
Mr. LESLIE: Therefore the farmer
who has got his price of 20s to 25s is going
to be faced with a net reduction in that
price. I do not think the farmer is going
to like that very much. It seems to be
agreed that the price is fixed and that
the extra cost of scutching, if any, is to
come out of that price.
I should like to ask whether the Board
is, so to speak, tied up. Is it the intention that the Agricultural Wages Board
should raise the existing rates for scutching flax, because if that is so-—and I cannot believe it is otherwise the Board has
to go into this consideration with its hands
tied? They have been told in effect, or
the intention is that the Bill should tell
them, that they are to raise the wages of
scutchers, and they are, therefore, not
completely free agents, as I think they
ought to be.
Passing from that, one wants to know
why there is this hurry about the Bill.
The great crusade was commenced some
months ago to ensure a greater area under
flax in Northern Ireland. That is,
several months ago, when that crusade
was launched-if you do launch a
crusade, or when the standard was put
up, it must have been well known that
flax would not have been any good unless
it was scutched. It must have been in
the mind of the Ministry of Agriculture
that flax would require to be scutched.
Why did they not take into consideration then what arrangements would be
necessary for that scutching. At that
time, moreover, the Agricultural Wages
Bill was under consideration or had
passed, and nothing was said about
scutching flax. Surely the farmer is
entitled to complain that the Ministry
were entirely forgetful of this necessary
scutching, and he is entitled to say he
ought to have been told that the scutching price would probably rise-rise by
control and not by agreement.
That is the question to which I think
the Government should address themselves. They ought to give us an explanation as to why they did not think
of this before. I am not myself even in
favour of compulsory uniformity in
wages, or for that matter anything else
if it can be avoided. I cannot see why,
looking to the different conditions which
prevail throughout Northern Ireland,
not
by counties, not even by rural
districts, but in the smallest locality you
can mention, the circumstances are
always different, frequently from personal considerations, and I repeat I am
not in favour of compulsory uniformity.
I am not concerned with the actual wage.
That is not really why I am attacking the
Bill, because there is nothing about the
rate of wages in the Bill. It is to regulate, not to fix, as the right hon. Gentleman reminded us. What I am concerned
with is the effect of legislation of this
kind.
The Bill is really an amending Bill.
It describes itself as a Bill to extend the
Agricultural Wages Act, but in addition
Agricultural Wages Bill--
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
to being an extending Measure it is also
to some extent an amending Bill, and it
is not so drawn as to allow any general
amendment of the provisions of the
Agricultural Wages Act. Such amendment may be necessary. Personally, I
think, it is very necessary, but it
scarcely arises on the Bill before!
us. It is really, as I think I have said,
the implications of the Bill I object to.
I say that the scutching of flax is not
the work of an agricultural labourer, and
that if any regulation is necessary of the
terms on which that scutcher does his
work the decision ought not to be committed to the Agricultural Wages
Board. That Agricultural Wages Board
is a Board recently established, and I
am afraid I must say it is a Board which
so far has failed to command the confidence of those concerned. The newspapers are full of complaints. One hears
complaints in one's own area,
and possibly other hon. Members suffer under the
same liability. One hears also the suggestion that this Agricultural Wages
Board has gone entirely into the hands
of the Socialists, and that representations
put forward by the farmers' representatives go unheeded. I believe I am correct in saying that one of the farmers'
representatives has already resigned his
seat on the ground that the farmers'
representatives do not receive proper consideration.
At the moment, then, your Agricultural Wages Board is one man short. I
have not seen an announcement of the
appointment of his successor. Therefore there is one man short on the
employers' side and I have not yet heard
that there are any members of that Board
who have any knowledge whatever of flax
scutching. That may or may not be the
case, but as far as I know there is nobody
on that Board who has any definite
knowledge of flax scutching. If you
allow this Bill to pass and consent to the
operations of the Agricultural Wages
Board extending to flax, where are you
going to stop?
not
It is all very well to say that flax
scutching is in a sense ancillary to agriculture, but flax scutching is
the only occupation which is ancillary
to agriculture. It is part of agriculture to produce sheep and beef and
mutton. Are you going to consider the
butcher who kills sheep and cattle as an
agricultural labourer? Then, again,
there are threshing machines that go
round the country. Are the men to be
regarded as agricultural labourers the
blacksmiths who shoe farm horses.
Where are you going to stop? I may
remind the House that I am not considering the wages to be paid. What I
do want the House to consider is the
body that is to regulate the wage. I do
not think the functions of the Agricultural Wages Board should be extended
either to the scutching of flax or the
shoeing of horses, or to the men who work
the threshing machines or to the butchers
who kill the cattle. Are you prepared to
commit this question of the regulation of
wages to that body?
con-
I do not think I ought to say anything
about the reasons why that body has not
succeeded in obtaining . universal
fidence because I think it would be outside the Bill before us, but opinion is
forming. I do not think myself that the
House ought to pass a Bill which has that
unfortunate result, although I think it
was an unlooked for resuit. I think
that in introducing the Bill-and I am
speaking particularly of the first subclause the intention was to meet the
difficulty, and attempt to meet it by
compulsion instead of by agreement. I
am always in favour of agreement rather
than compulsion, and I am not disposed
to accept anything in the way of unnecessary compulsion.
The second clause deals with the matter
of the half-holiday. I do not think that
is a matter that need detain us very
seriously. The House is aware that if he
takes a whole holiday every fortnight the
farm labourer will get 32 days' holiday on
full pay in the year-rather more than a
calender month in the year. However,
Parliament has let itself in for that, and
I do not think this House is the appropriate Chamber to make any suggestions
about that. At the same time it is well
to point out to the public that your farm
labourer under this Bill will be getting
32 holidays in the year on full pay. We
may pass from that and return to my
main attack, which is that the Agricultural Wages Board is not the proper
authority to deal with a matter of this
kind. For that reason I must move my
Motion.
357 Agricultural Wages Bill

Adjournment
Question put, "That the word 'now'
stand part.
""
Mr. ROBB: The position is quite simple
and it is this. The farmer has to get a
fixed price for his flax. The farmer has
to pay the flax mill owner a fixed price for
scutching, but there is nothing at this
moment to regulate what the scutch millowner is going to have to pay for scutching.
Mr. LESLIE: Do I understand you
to
say that the extra money, if there is extra
money, will come out of the pocket of
the scutch mill owner and not out of the
pocket of the farmer?
Mr. ROBB: The intended effect of this
Bill is that the amount a scutcher can demand for scutching flax shall not be such
an amount as he can enforce by strike,
but such amount as the Agricultural
Wages Board shall consider fair. When
you talk about extra money the intention
is that this money shall be less, not more..
This Bill is intended to regulate wages
and to prevent the exaction, by strike or
otherwise, of an undue amount of money
out of the bulk sum paid for the. flax by
scutchers.
The hon. and learned Member objects
to the principle of regulation. That principle has been accepted by Parliament
over and over again in different directions. Apart from that, the hon. and
learned Member objects that, whoever is
going to regulate the wages it should not
be the Agricultural Wages Board. My
answer to that is quite simple, and it is
this. The farmer, the scutch mill owner,
and the scutcher agreed that it shall be
the Agricultural Wages Board. I think
that is a complete answer to every point
the hon. and learned Member made
except one. That is, why did we not
think of this in December last.
Barclay, Mr. W.
Boyle, Major J. C.
Cunningham, Mr Joseph
Davison, Sir Joseph
The
answer is that the circumstances had not
arisen then. The extent to which wages
might rise had not become apparent then,
and now that it has this is the method
by which we propose to deal with it, and
the method to which the parties concerned agreed.
Mr. LESLIE: The right hon. Gentleman has said the farmer has agreed.
Mr. ROBB: Those farmers who were
represented by the Ulster
Ulster Farmers'

If you allow the Agricultural Wages
Board to regulate this particular matter
why are you not going to allow them to

Mr. ROBB: I will deal with those

Mr. LESLIE: But you ought to know
that if you once let in the thin end of the

once get their foot in there they will continue until they have completed the ruin
of the small farmer in Ulster which, but

express our opinion, the point being the
extension of the powers of the Agricultural Wages Board.

Finance Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time". The
Measure really deals with three matters.
Clause 1 provides for a reduction of the
tax payable on motor vehicles for goods
purposes where those motor vehicles are
driven by gas. The taxation of motor
vehicles used for goods purposes is upon
the weight of the vehicle. The change
over from petrol to gas increased the
weight of the vehicle, and this Clause
provides that such increased weight shall
not be calculated as part of the weight
of the vehicle. for taxation purposes.
Clause 2 deals with the issue of a
permit for the use of motor vehicles by
members of His Majesty's Forces on leave
from overseas and afloat. It is a 10s
licence for a car for three weeks, and 2s
for a motor cycle.
Clauses 3, 4, 5 and 6 are an attempt
by legislation to stop evasions with regard
to estate duty. They are very highly
technical, and I do not myself profess to
understand them fully, although I have
endeavoured to do so. Clause 7 extends
to merchant seamen the exemption from
death duties that is given to members of
the Forces if on active service. The other
Clauses are supplemental.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed."(Mr. Robb.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Tuesday next.

Mr. ROBB: This is the Bill that
provides for the changes in taxation
announced by the Minister of Finance
in his Budget speech. Clause 1 deals
with the increase of estate duty, Clause 2
with the increase of rates on certain sales

and mortgages, and Clause 3 the increased rates of entertainment duty. The

Mr. LESLIE: I should like to have
some assurance that the provisions of
this Bill dealing with the increase of
estate duty are only intended to operate
during the course of the war. The point
is, as, of course, the House is well aware,
that estate duties have been forced up to
an almost impossible height. One can
only speak in an altruistic spirit, because no living man pays estate duties.
I would ask the House for the sake of
argument to refer to the last item in the
First Schedule, the case of a man who
has an estate of over £2,000,000. If
£2,000,000 was invested at 4 per cent.
it would produce £80,000 a year, quite
a sizeable income no doubt, but when
estate duty is taken off what income is
going to be left to the heir, assuming
the whole estate passes to one man?
think I am right in saying that that
£80,000 would drop to £28,000, which
is a colossal drop.
I
I hope the public will take due note
of the very heavy burden cast on estates
passing on death, and that they will also
consider the effect on the country
generally of such a colossal disturbance
of the incomes of the inhabitants of the
country. After all, the man who
possesses £80,000 a year cannot possibly
spend it all on himself. We know he
does not. He is, as a rule, a very large
employer of labour, and of great
assistance to his country. The passing
of a Bill of this sort, if it becomes
generally effective, must to my mind.
have a very deep and disastrous effect
on the welfare of the country generally.
That, of course, is particularly so in the
rural districts. There are probably very
few Irish landlords left whose estates are
worth £2,000.000, but there are people
lower down the scale, and the effect on
their estates is likely to be relatively
equally disastrous.
Mr. ROBB: I sympathise very much.
with what the hon. and learned Senator
has said, but I cannot give him the
assurance he has asked.

Truck Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This Bill has a curious
origin. Under the Truck Acts, the
main Act of which was passed in the
year 1831, it was provided, generally
speaking, that the wages of the class of
persons included within the ambit of
these Bills, generally called artificers,
must be paid in current coin of the
Realm. A case arose in England where
the employee of a firm of drapers or
warehousemen when being engaged in his
employment asked the manager what his
wages would be. The manager said 53s
a week. They would give him his dinner
and tea and he would regard that as
worth 10s a week. From 1920 to 1935
the man worked for that firm, and
leaving out small variations that were
made under other arrangements he received his 53s and got his dinner and
tea. After leaving the employment he
brought an action against his employers
and claimed from them 10s a week for
every week he had been employed by
them, because, as he said, his wages were
really 53s a week and his food, and the
Truck Act set out that that was an
agreement that could not stand. Under
the Truck Act, unless there was an
agreement in writing under Section 23,
his wages must be paid in current coin
of the Realm.
The case was brought to the House of
Lords, and it was held that the workman was entitled to get a verdict for
£397 10s Od. There were thousands of
people in the same boat. It was
obviously an immoral claim. The man
had never thought of any such thinghe had been perfectly content with his
wage and food while with the firm-and
it was only afterwards somebody pointed
out to him that he had a claim, because
any bargain of that sort should have
been in writing. The employer could
have made an agreement with him in
writing to give him 63s a week and
Truck Bill
Adjournment
26 SEPTEMBER 1940
deduct 10s for his food. That, however,
had not been done in this case. There
were thousands of cases all over England.
It was alleged that this particular
firm of Cooke & Son-the case was called
Pratt v. Cooke owed their employees,
in respect of claims which the employees
never thought of making, between
£25,000 and £30,000. There was the
natural difficulty that these claims might
be made all over the country, legal
claims which would have been perfectly
immoral, and the Government announced
at once that they would introduce a
Measure in England to say that no such
claims should be maintained. Accordingly an Act was passed in England, and
to-day we were seeking to pass a similar
Measure here.
I should like to point out that the Bill
does not alter the law except in one
respect. It says with regard to what has
passed that no action shall be brought,
but it does not alter the law. It is well
that all employers should know that if
they are going to make a bargain of that
sort in the future they must comply with
the conditions laid down in the Act of
1831 that such agreement must be in
writing. That is all this Bill does. I beg
to move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time".
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

Resolved, "That this House do now
adjourn until Tuesday next".-(Mr.
Long.)

Minutes before Four of the
Clock until Tuesday next.

Tuesday, 8th October, 1940.
The Senate met at a Quarter after

Question to Minister.
VISITS TO INTERNEES.
The following Question stood on the
Order Paper in
Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :-
To ask the Leader of the Senate
whether he can state how many times
per month relatives will be allowed to
visit internees; whether, if an internee has a business or farm of his
own, the person managing that farm
or business during internee's absence
will be allowed to visit him; whether
internees or persons detained, wishing
to have their cases considered by the
Advisory Committee, will be allowed
to have legal advice or assistance in
preparing their case to put before the
Committee.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In asking this
question I should like to point out that
a prisoner awaiting trial has visits every
day from his relatives during regulation
hours. I understand that even the interned Germans can have their friends
visit them every day, whilst the internees
on the "Al Rawdah" must wait for three
months at a time for a visit from one of
their relatives. Here is an extract from
a letter from the mother of a boy 18
years old interned on this ship :-
It is now more than five weeks since
my boy went on the ship. He is
anxious to see me, and I am dying to
see him. He has applied for a permit
for me to visit him, but when he gets
the permit the way things are it would
be two months before my turn would
come to see him.
Surely this state of affairs should not
exist, and in the name of justice I demand that these men on the ship should
be allowed reasonably frequent visits
from their dear ones. The latter part of
the question speaks for itself. On the
23rd ult. the right hon. Gentleman, the
Leader of the House, gave me to understand that he would inquire into that
matter and would give me an answer.
Not having got this, I have put down
this question.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
By direction of the civil authority, each
internee is allowed one visit per week of
not more than two people, and visits are
confined to close relatives of internees.
If an internee wishes to be visited by any
person not falling within the category of
a close relative, with regard to business
matters, he should make special application to the Ministry of Home Affairs.
An internee is allowed to have legal
advice or assistance in preparing his case
to put it before the Advisory Committee.
For the present, however, not more than
64 visits may be given within each period
of a fortnight in respect of persons interned on H.M.T. "Al Rawdah."
Mr. MCLAUGHLIN: That is the
point-that there can be only 64 visits
within a fortnight. I understand there
are something like 200 men on board this
ship, so that the mother of the boy to
whom I referred is quite right when she
says she will not be able to visit her boy
for the next two months. It is really too
bad that an interned man, against whom
no charge has been made, is not allowed
a visit from his relatives for two or three
months. This mother's letter states that
her boy has been on this ship for five
weeks, and that when he asks for a permit
she will have to wait two months before
her turn arrives for visiting him. Surely
that state of affairs should not exist.
Mr. ROBB: The limitation is due to
the difficulties of transport to and from
the ship. Many people apply for visiting
permits and only a certain number can
be transported on any one day. That is
the reason for the present restriction on
visiting.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I do not see why
there should be any difficulty as regards
transport when there are comparatively
few men on the ship. These are untried
men, men against whom no charge has
been proven, and it is a scandalous thing
that a question like this should have to
be put on the Paper. I hold very strong
views on the sanctity of the liberty of the
subject and I am sure the right hon.
Gentleman, the Leader of the House,
Question to Minister
Business of the House
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
identified with the law, as I am, in his
own private judgment holds a similar
view.
There is no justification for detaining a
person, whether you call him an internee
or anything else, without giving him the
right of trial by his peers. That is what
the right hon. Gentleman and myself
learned in our youth, and the more we
have seen of the administration of the law
in this part of the country the more
horrified we are to think that men can be
treated in this way, without any trial,
without the public knowing what the men
are charged with, what the evidence is
against them, and the reason for their
detention. Such a thing cannot be
justified in any civilised country.
We are supposed to be fighting against
dictatorship. That is what we read day
by day, but could any dictatorship be
worse than the practice of taking a man
from his home, his family and his business, and detaining him on a ship without trial and without the public knowing
what evidence there is against him. Men
may have been detained as the result of
an anonymous letter from some vicious
neighbour. Many of the men on this
ship may have been detained as a result
of information from that source alone. I
am not here to defend any man who is
proved to have broken the law, but I
am here to defend the rights and liberties
of all untried men, and I would criticise
any Government that adopted powers
such as these, powers which make them
more despotic than Hitler himself.
Mr. MAGUIRE: Could the Leader of
the House give us any information in
regard to the 44 internees who are still
held in Crumlin Road Gaol? They have
been held there for over four weeks and
were not transferred to the ship. Could
the right hon. Gentleman also tell us how
many appeals against internment have
been granted by the Advisory Committee, and how many men have been
released up to the present?
Mr. ROBB: I would require notice
of those questions, as I have not got the
information.
Business of the House.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That
Standing Order No. XXV be suspended
for this day's sitting for the purpose of
taking all stages of the Local Government Inquiries Bill, when it is brought
from the House of Commons." The
reason for this Motion is that the Belfast
Corporation requested the Ministry of
Home Affairs to hold a sworn inquiry
into certain irregularities they said had
arisen in connection with the administration of the sanatorium at Whiteabbey.
It was found to be doubtful whether the
Ministry had power to hold such an
inquiry. The Corporation are pressing
the Ministry to hold this inquiry and
this Bill gives us power to do so. The
matter is pressing, and accordingly we
ask the House to complete the consideration of this Bill to-day.
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me that
this proposal requires really more explanation than the right hon. Gentleman has given us. He has instanced a
salient case in which application has been
made from the Belfast Corporation for
an inquiry into the administration of a
sanatorium which I presume is under
some sort of joint management. But is
not the right hon. Gentleman asking a
great deal more than that? The Bill
seems to provide for a very general
change in the law, a change which will
be general and not limited in its application to the case which he has
mentioned. It is very difficult to speak
on this Motion without making a Second
Reading speech.
I feel that as things are I must say
I do not like the Bill itself. That is
without prejudice to being perfectly
willing to support any arrangement
whereby the inquiry referred to can be
held. I do object to a general alteration
being made in the law at such extremely
short notice. The proposal is to rush the
Bill through the House in one day. The
House will very naturally require to be
informed whether the Association of
County Councils has been consulted,
because the Bill is going to be of general
application. I should have preferred
that the matter should have
approached on the lines of an amendment
of Section 101 of the Local Government
Act of 1898. I think it could have been
done that way and then there would
have been no difficulty.

Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Local

Local Government Inquiries Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time".—(Mr. Long.)
a
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, “That this
Bill be. now read a Second time". As I
have explained, this matter arose through
a request of the Belfast Corporation that
the Ministry of Home Affairs should hold
sworn inquiry into certain alleged
irregularities that had occurred, they
said, in connection with the administration of the Tuberculosis Sanatorium at
Whiteabbey. It had always been assumed
that the Ministry of Home Affairs had
power to hold such an inquiry, but when
it was looked into it was found to be at
any rate doubtful whether there was such
a power.
The Tuberculosis Act is a
different code from the Public Health
Acts, and there was not specific power
given the old Local Government Board,
the functions of which were transferred
to the Ministry of Home Affairs, to hold
these inquiries, and it was doubtful
whether a general power to hold such
an inquiry existed. Accordingly the
Government, in assenting to the request
of the Belfast Corporation, agreed to ask
Parliament to confer upon them power to
hold this inquiry.
At the moment this is the only inquiry
this Bill touches, but there may be other
occasions where irregularities in matters
of local government administration may
suggest the propriety of holding such an
inquiry. Accordingly this Bill has been
drawn in general terms to cover any cases
that have been omitted from the previous
codes, if there should be any such, and
it is in the form of a general power to
the Ministry to hold inquiries in matters
of local government. It is quite a simple
Bill. In most matters the Local Government Act covers the question of inquiries,
but in addition to this tuberculosis code
Local Government Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
there might possibly be other cases where
it is found that a hiatus exists in the
legislation, and accordingly the Bill is
drawn in a general form. The second
sub-clause of Clause 1 deals with the
consequential application of enactments
to this form of inquiry, and Clause 2 is
merely formal.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: As I intimated a few
minutes ago, it does not seem to me that
the Bill is quite as simple as has been
made out. It is a matter, I think, on which
the House may very well be divided from
different points of view. The right hon.
Gentleman speaks of the desirability of
taking a general power of inquiry into
the proper carrying out of any enactment affecting the local government law.
I think it is fair to put it in that way.
What is the position at the moment?
I took the liberty of raking the library
for the Public Health Act of 1878 in
order that I might have at hand the few
sections to which it is necessary to refer.
The Bill refers to Sections 210 and 211,
but the one I want to read to the House
is Section 209 of the Public Health
Act:
The Local Government Board may
from time to time cause to be made
such inquiries as are directed by this
Act, and such inquiries as they see fit
in relation to any matters concerning
the public health in any place, or any
matters with respect to which their
sanction, approval, or consent is required by this Act.
That is the section which so far as I know
still governs inquiries under that Act.
The House will notice that that is
limited to matters arising under that
Act. That differs, I suggest to the
House, from the Local Government Code;
that is the code relating to the administration of the Act of 1898 under which
county councils function. The main
point of the Local Government Code is to
be found in Section 101 of the Act of
1898. I need not read all of it:-
Nothing in this Act shall affect any
powers of the Local Government Board
with respect to guardians or the officers
of guardians; and the Board may, on
the application of any council, exercise
in relation to the council and the
officers of the council all such powers
of holding inquiries on oath
and so on.
That is the broad difference between
the two codes. Under the Public Health
code the Ministry can order an inquiry at
its own sweet will, so to speak. One would
assume that it would not act without some
sort of provocation, but under the Local
Government Code as defined by the Section
I have just read, and which is amplified
by paragraph 32 of the Application of
Enactments Order, you have got to have
an application of the Council; in fact,
you have got to have such an application
as has been received from the Belfast
Corporation in this particular case.
But the difficulty here is that the power
of the Ministry is doubtful. That is because of the introduction of the tuberculosis code, but what I think the House
will want to know, or ought to want to
know, before the Bill becomes an Act is
this. Section 209 of the Public Health
Act of 1878, which I have just read, was
in existence in 1898 when Section 101 of
the Public Health Act was passed. Why,
if it was desired to assimilate the two
codes, was it not done then? One cannot
assume or believe that the draftsmen of
the 1898 Act had not got the 1878 Act
before them. They had, and for some
reason or other they did not follow it in
this respect. One wants to know why,
and one ought to be assured that the
Association of County Councils has been
consulted and that they are prepared to
acquiesce. We ought to have that information before us; otherwise we cannot
regard this Bill as other than a Bill to
infringe on the functions of democracy.
The 1898 Local Government Act was,
in fact, a commission to the democracy
of the powers formerly exercised by Grand
Juries. Whether the change was for the
better or worse is not before us this afternoon. A change was made, and is not
this Bill an attempt to get behind it and
substitute rather more autocratic powers
than the Ministry at present possess? The
influence of the Ministry on these local
government boards is very helpful generally, but I think we must keep it in due
bounds, and unless the county councils
can be induced to accept an abatement
of their Charter I do not think this
House ought to pass this Bill.
377 Local Government Bill-

8 OCTOBER 1940
Second Reading
Coming back to the particular question
of the Belfast Corporation and the
sanatorium, it is not unfair to remark
that when the 1898 Act was passed,
although the 1878 Act was before them,
I do not think the question of these joint
councils was before them. What the
exact arrangements are as regards this
particular sanatorium I do not know, but
it is obvious that the Belfast Corporation
has a distinct say in the management.
They probably pay a good deal of the
cost, and I imagine this sanatorium is
under the joint management of the
Belfast Corporation and some other
bodies, but that does not matter. It
would be quite fair to amend the Local
Government Act, Section 101, in order to
provide that reference there to the county
council shall include reference to joint
councils in this Clause, and that could
reasonably be done in one day if necessary.
But I do not think that without a conference with the county councils and an
assurance of their agreement we ought to
consent to such a general abatement of
the powers conferred on them by the Act
of 1898 as the Bill before us suggests.
Accordingly, I think I ought to make
the customary Motion to leave out of
the Motion before the House the word
"now", and to add at the end the. words,
"this day six months". I hope it will
not be necessary to pursue that to its
logical conclusion, but I hope it will lead
to an amendment of the Bill so as not to
prevent the holding of this particular
inquiry. We do not want to restrict the
proper powers of the Ministry, but we do
not want to let them go too far ahead
and put local authorities and county
councils under the heel of the Ministry
to an extent which is not really necessary.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I support the
arguments put forward by the hon. and
learned Senator, and in doing so I would.
point out that we have heard only one
side of the story. Owing to lack of the
necessary information, I am unable to
make up my mind whether the principle
involved in this Bill should be supported.
There is a principle involved, because
when you propose to confer powers on the
Government in connection with local
government matters you must have some
justification for it. There may be some
justification for introducing this Bill; I
do not say there is not, but I would like
to know the views of the local government bodies affected. They, no doubt,
know from their wide experience whether
such a Measure as this is necessary and
should be accepted, or whether it is a
violation of the Local Government Code
as administered by them. From all the
information I have I do not know
whether this is a good or a bad Bill or
even a necessary Bill.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: It

that if the law requires to be altered to
meet the particular case that has cropped
up in connection with the Belfast Corporation it is right that the law should
be altered so that if a similar case
occurred in any other borough it might
be dealt with without any special legislation. I take it that this is a general
provision in case something like this
occurs again, and I do not think it does
a terrible injustice to any democratic
body. I have heard it said, even by
extreme Labour men, that the people
require something to protect them from
themselves, and I think the Government
serves a good purpose by exercising some
jurisdiction over these local government
boards. I feel that this is a proper course
for the Government to adopt, and I will
support it.

Notice of Questions and Amendments.
Mr. SPEAKER: I should like to
remind hon. Members who have questions
to put or amendments to move in Committee that they must ensure that these
reach the Clerk of the Parliaments by the
first post on Friday, otherwise there can
be no assurance that they will be printed
on the Order Paper. When they arrive
late it means that the Order Paper has
to be reprinted, and this cannot be done
in future owing to the very serious
shortage of paper.
Questions to Minister.
CIVIL SERVANTS AND CIVIL
DEFENCE SERVICES.
Mr.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of
Taylor :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government will arrange
that civil servants and other Government employees who have volunteered
for National Service, and are members
of A.R.P., Local Defence Volunteers,
Special Constabulary and other civil
defence services, will be available for
duty with their various units in the
event of an emergency arising during
official hours, especially in view of the
urgent necessity of these services being
manned in such circumstances.
Mr. TAYLOR: I am sure the House
is fully aware that a very large number
of civil servants and other Government
employees have loyally responded to the
call for National Service, and are already
enrolled in the various civil defence units,
many of them holding important key
positions in the Local Defence Volunteer
Section, A.R.P., and Special Constabulary. They are already trained
and equipped and prepared for any
emergency that may arise.
Recently a question arose that is disturbing the minds of some of these men,
as to whether a civil servant, who is a
member of any of the civil defence units,
would be available for duty during
official hours. Should a period of grave
emergency arise a civil servant, who is
a group warden, section leader, or officer
of the Special Constabulary, might
cause serious inconvenience or disaster
if he were unable to take up immediate
duty with the force of which he is a
member.
Normally a civil servant is not allowed
to engage in or undertake work which
would interfere with the performance of
his official duties, and in face of this
regulation I can appreciate the position
of these men, who do not want to
jeopardise their position in the civil
service by acting contrary to regulations.
For that reason I think their position in
connection with civil defence should be
made clear by the Government, and it is
for that reason the question is on the
Order Paper in my name.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
am glad to say that a very large number
of our
officers have been anxious to
volunteer for different kinds of national
service, including L.D.V. and A.R.P.
work, and the Government is, naturally,
anxious to facilitate them as far as possible.
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
15 OCTOBER 1940
In order to ensure that an officer is
available for the work which he wishes to
undertake it has been found necessary to
regard volunteers from the Government
service as falling into three classes:-
(a) Those whose duties as civil servants
would become so heavy in an emergency
as to require that they should be completely freed from other engagements;
(b) Those who can only be spared for
L.D.V. or A.R.P. duty at the offices
in which they are employed;
(c) Those who can be allowed, without interfering with their official duties,
to engage in outside L.D.V. or A.R.P.
duty. While this duty will usually be
performed after office hours it is recognised that occasions may arise when the
duty will be required during such
hours, and possibly even on a full-time
basis.
Officers placed in class (a) would be
required to resign from any organisation,
whether directly connected with their
Department or otherwise, to which they
now belong; officers placed in class (b)
will be restricted to joining Departmental
units for L.D.V. or A.R.P. purposes;
while officers placed in class (c) will be
permitted to engage freely in full-time
L.D.V. or A.R.P. duties, with a contingent liability for full-time service. Each
case would have to be dealt with on its
own particular merits.

appeals have been heard from these
men, and how many men have been
released; whether there are
internees who are prepared to give the
necessary undertaking still held in
prison, if so, how many.
any
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In the absence
of Senator Maguire I should like to put
this question. I think it speaks for
itself. I have myself asked questions
about these internees on several occasions.
I have been very much concerned about
the number of internees who have requested that their cases should be dealt
with by the Advisory Committee, and
who have been kept waiting for a considerable time before their cases were
heard. I understand that a number of
them are still waiting for their appeals
to be heard. The men whose cases have
been taken are prepared to give an undertaking that they will not belong to any
political organisation, and to give bail
for their future good conduct, and yet
they have not been released. I cannot
see any reason why these men are deprived
of their liberty unless there is some malice
behind them being kept in Belfast Gaol.
I can seen no reason for it other than
malice. Here are men against whom the
Government refuse to bring any charge,
and they are deprived of their liberty
even though they are prepared to give
bail for their future good conduct. Unless
there is malice behind their detention they
should not any longer be kept in gaol. If
the Government are not prepared to bring
a charge against these men I say with all

to undertake to

Mr. TAYLOR: I would like to ask the

Mr. ROBB: The number of persons
interned in H.M. Prison, Belfast, has
varied considerably since 1st September,
1940, but the total number at present
under internment in the prison is 70.
Sixty-two men who were interned in
the prison during the period made representations against internment and,
following the hearing of their appeals by
the Advisory Committee, 25 have been
released. Of the remaining 37 appellants
who expressed their willingness to enter
into undertakings as to their future
behaviour, 32 have been informed that
they cannot be released and the remaining five cases are still under consideration.
