Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
Five other interned persons, who also
made representations with a view to
securing their release, eventually decided
not to appear before the Advisory Committee. During the period under review,
a total of 72 interned persons were
transferred from H.M. Prison, Belfast,
to the "Al Rawdah".
Mr. MAGUIRE: I am very sorry I
was not able to be present to ask this
question myself. Since I put it down the
circumstances have somewhat changed,
because I understand that several of the
internees have gone on hunger strike. Six
weeks ago there were about 44 internees
in Crumlin Road Prison who were not
transferred to the prison ship. These 44
internees, I am informed, were prepared
to appeal to the. Advisory Committee, but
I am told it was only recently that their
cases have been heard. There has thus
been a delay of four or five weeks, and
why that should have, occurred I do not
know. I have been informed that the
reason given was that the chairman of
the Advisory Committee was busily
engaged at the Law Courts and had not
time to deal with these, cases. If that is
so, surely a substitute could have been
provided at the Law Courts to release the
chairman to hear the appeals of these
internees. I am further informed that
the 44 men were prepared to give bail
to keep the peace, and also to sign all
documents necessary to satisfy the
Government.
It was generally understood that any
of these internees who were prepared to
give bail and sign any undertaking would
be released, but that has not been done.
Over 50 per cent. of them are married
men with families depending upon them.
They have lost their employment, and it
is a great hardship on their dependents.
They have nothing coming into their
homes, and it is only by the charity of
neighbours that they are able to buy the
necessaries of life. I think it is a crying
shame that men who are prepared to
give every undertaking that has been
asked of them, and, in addition, to give
bail, are not released.
What is the reason the appeals of these
men have been turned down? Suppose
any Member of this House had been
under suspicion and interned. How
would we feel if we were prepared to give
any undertaking required by the Government and yet were kept in gaol. I think
we would rightly rebel against such treatment. On Friday last I happened to be
visiting Crumlin Road Gaol, and I was
speaking to a chaplain. He told me that
after their appeals had been turned down
these internees were in such a state of
mind that he was afraid to approach
them. In continuing to detain these
men the Government are acting very
drastically and are not acting fairly and
justly towards them. If they were
released on bail and on the undertakings
they are prepared to sign the police would
keep a very careful watch on the movements of the men, and I cannot see the
slightest reason why they should not be
immediately released.
Mr. MCALLISTER: The Government
of Northern Ireland may think this is a
small matter, but a more vital question,
which goes to the very fount of justice,
could not be raised in this House. I do
not know a single individual detained
under these Orders, nor, so far as I am
aware, do any of these men know me.
I do not care what a man's political views
or religion may be, but as long as I am
in a position where I can take any action
to ensure a fair, just, and equitable
administration of the law I shall conceive
it my duty to do so. By this insidious
legislation the Government of Northern
Ireland have brought discredit upon
themselves and the whole people of
Northern Ireland.
I do not care what a person's political
or religious views may be, he is entitled
to be tried by his peers.
I do not care
whether he is a member of the Irish
Republican Army or the "B" Specials,
it is all the same to me. One and all
are entitled to justice, and the only way
you can ensure a fair and just administration of the law and the confidence of
the people in that administration, is by
taking the public into your confidence.
It is not by setting up these starchamber courts behind closed doors
where the public are debarred from
knowing what is going on, and what
undertaking has been given by these
men, and who are debarred from
knowing how they are humiliated and for
what reason they are being kept in
prison, presumably for life, at the will
of their political opponents.
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
15 OCTOBER 1940
In no other country in the world could
the machine of the political opponents
of the minority consign their opponents
to prison and detain them without trial
behind the backs of the people and deny
them their elementary rights of justice.
Surely the very foundation of democracy
-and it is democracy we are supposed to
be fighting for now-is the liberty of the
subject. It is a well-established principle that when the administration of
justice is undermined you destroy all confidence in justice.
For myself I have always demanded
that a man should have the right and the
opportunity to prove his innocence, and
that men should be imprisoned, without
trial and without any charge being
formulated against them, by their political opponents is a shocking thing. If
the Imperial authorities have any control
over the administration of the Six
Counties, and they do not take notice
of this example of the unfair treatment
of a minority, how are they going to
talk international politics and hold themselves out as champions of democracy.
Sir EMERSON HERDMAN: I do
not think this is a matter of politics at
the present moment. The nation is fighting for its very life, and these men are
against not only our Government, but
the British Government. Therefore I
think there is nothing to do but to keep
them where they are until this terrible
war is over.

against these men, and on that something they think it is not desirable in
the public interest that they should be

having so far submitted themselves as to

be brought to trial as soon as may be.
For the moment they must, no doubt,

If the authorities really have something
against them and I assume they must

may be. In the meantime I suggest

have their cases reviewed. Perhaps the
right hon. Gentleman can assure us that
there is some power of review.

In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the, Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Power of Ministry of
Home Affairs to direct local inquiries and
investigations).
(1) The Ministry of Home Affairs
may from time to time cause to be
made such local inquiries and investigations as the said Ministry sees fit in
relation to any matters concerning the
administration of any enactment which
relates to the functions of the council
of any county, county borough or
county district or of any committee or
joint committee wholly or partly
appointed by any such council; and for
the purposes of any such inquiry or
investigation sections two hundred and
ten, two hundred and twelve and two
hundred and thirteen of the Public
Health (Ireland) Act, 1878, shall apply
as if they were in terms made applicable to those purposes.
(2) The powers conferred upon the
Ministry of Home Affairs by this Act
shall be in addition to, and not in
derogation of, any powers exercisable
by the said Ministry under any enactment other than this Act.

is nothing under this Bill to prevent the
Ministry of Home Affairs ordering an
inquiry for no particular reason and
without any request from anyone.. We
discussed that rather fully perhaps on the
Second Reading, and it therefore seemed
to me that the insertion of some such
words as those I have on the Paper might
serve to remove any idea that the county
councils were expected to administer the
Local Government Act with a policeman
round the corner. It was not the intention of the Local Government Act of 1898
that the county councils should perform
their very important work with fear, as
I say, of the policeman round the corner.
Therefore, I think it might be worth
while to insert certain words in the, text
of the Bill before us. Whether or not
those words would make any difference in
practice under the present regime of the
Ministry of Home Affairs I think on
general grounds they ought to go in, and
therefore I move their insertion.

Expiring Laws Continuance Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." It is
a very short Measure, and its object is to
continue in force the three Acts mentioned in the Schedule. The first is the
Harbours, Docks, and Piers (Temporary
Increase of Charges) Act which enables
public authorities and companies to indues on harbours, docks, and
piers. The second is the Local Government (Emergency Powers) Act which
enables the Ministry of Home Affairs to
appoint a Commissioner in the case of
crease
a
recalcitrant local authority. It is
desired that this power should be continued for the present. The third is a
war emergency Act which postpones the
election of members of certain local
bodies and the provision of jurors' books.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Whole House for Tuesday next.

Questions to Minister.
COUNTY COURT JURISDICTION.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if any representations have been received from the
Incorporated Law Society, suggesting
that, as a war-time measure, the limit
of County Court Jurisdiction fixed in
1877 should be increased; and, if so,
whether it is proposed to take any
action in the matter.
Mr. LESLIE: The matter to which
this question is addressed has been
occupying public attention for some time.
There has been something approaching a
clamant demand for a reform of our
county court system, and the present war
has rather brought that into greater prominence. There is distinctly a feeling
that, at all events, what I may call the
£50 limit referred to in Section 50,
appropriately enough, of the Act of 1877,
should be extended, and perhaps possibly
the £30 in relation to land which appears
in divers passages of the same Act.
I do not know that the public demand
goes further than that, because if it went
too far it might mean raising your £50
jurisdiction to a limit which might be
thought fantastic. However, those matters
are not for this afternoon. The point to
which the question is addressed is as
stated on the Paper.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The Government have seen the resolution
from the Incorporated Law Society of
Northern Ireland, urging them to introduce a Bill increasing, during the war,
the limits of the jurisdiction of county
courts. The matter is at present under
consideration, but no decision has yet been
taken.
INTERNEES ON "AL RAWDAH."
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Northern Government has
received any representations from the
British Government on the subject of
the prison ship, "Al Rawdah";
whether he can state at what date the
Northern Government decided to alter
their policy in regard to internment of
this kind; whether it was at the
request of the Northern Government
that the "Al Rawdah" was sent as a
prison ship, or did the British Government direct the Northern Government
to requisition the ship for the internees; whether the internees, on
their release, are obliged to give an
undertaking to reside only in one
portion of the Six Counties, or to remain out of a scheduled area; whether
he can state the nature of the undertaking they are obliged to sign, and
the amount of bail required from each
surety; whether any of them are
asked for information about their
fellow internees, comrades or neighbours; whether it is a fact that, in
February, 1924, an internee was
refused release until a few days after
the British Government had directed
the Northern Government to release
him; whether it is a fact that the internee refused a first-class ticket to
Newry, and also refused to reside out
of or keep away from Fermanagh, and
in spite of this he was, on the direction
of the then Prime Minister of Great
Britain, released on February 11, 1924;

whether on refusing to leave Fermanagh he was rearrested on 13th
February, 1924, and on the orders of
the Imperial Authorities, on the evening of the same day (13th February,

these conditions; whether the Government can now give particulars of the

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: From the discussions which took place in the Northern
Parliament and the Imperial Parliament
on Tuesday last, October 15th, in connection with the "Al Rawdah" prison
ship, it is clear that there is a serious
discrepancy between the two stories. It
ought to be cleared up at once. The
Parliamentary Secretary of the Northern
Home Office said:- 66 6
:- 'Al Rawdah' was
established as the result of a consultation
between the Prime Minister, the Minister
for Public Security and the Minister for
Home Affairs on the one side, and the
General Officer commanding in Northern
Ireland on the other. Although the
Minister for Home Affairs and he (Mr.
Lowry) did not agree with it, they had
acquiesced." That was the story told by
Mr. Lowry, K.C.
On the other hand, the Parliamentary
Secretary for Shipping in England, Mr.
A. Salter, replying to Mr. McGovern,
M.P., at Westminster, said that "the
'Al Rawdah' was placed at the disposal
of the Government of Northern Ireland
at their request and at their cost." He
added, "An urgent request had been
sent to the Government of Northern Ireland who had stated that they had no
alternative accommodation."
Where is the truth between these
spokesmen? One of them was telling a
lie. The public would like to know which
Government it was which first suggested
the new prison ship. Did Lord
Craigavon, behind the backs of his
""
Cabinet, arrange the matter himself, as
he so often did? In view of what had
taken place in February, 1924, in the
case of one internee on board the
'Argenta" the public will not readily
accept any statement emanating from the
Northern Home Office, unless confirmed
by reliable independent persons. On the
afternoon of the 13th February, 1924, at
3-30 p.m., it was stated by the Home
Secretary in the Imperial Parliament, in
reply to a question by Mr. W. R. Pringle,
M.P., that according to information from
the Northern Home Office the Member
for Fermanagh and Tyrone (Mr. Healy)
had been released.
The
The statement appeared in the evening
papers published at six o'clock. Yet at
9-15 p.m. that same day Mr. Healy was
still in custody in Derry Prison and the
Commissioner for Police (Mr. Connor)
was endeavouring by the usual means to
get him to agree to exclusion from
Enniskillen. The Northern Government
were lying that time, in any case.
instructions to the Commissioner in
Derry was a last minute effort to save
the face of Sir Dawson Bates with the
extremists of his party. He had already
promised in the Northern Parliament
that Mr. Healy would not be released
unless he went before the Advisory Committee, but the intervention of the
British Labour Government, faced with a
motion by Mr. Pringle, put Sir Dawson
in a fix. So he had to swallow his
promise and act without the Advisory
Committee. The reply from the British
Ministry of Shipping has now placed him
in another dilemma as to the person who
suggested the prison ship internment.
It will be found that the real cause of
the end of the "Al Rawdah" was a communication sent from the Washington
Government to London and transmitted
by them to Belfast. It was not for love
of the internees but a fear that if any
attack was made upon the "Al Rawdah"
from the air by any of the belligerents,
it would cause an upheaval of public
feeling in America, where, at all costs,
friendly relations must be maintained.
That was the real secret of the abandonment of the prison ship. In an affidavit
made in 1923-
Mr. ROBB: On a point of order, it is
putting me in a very embarrassing position when the hon. Member proceeds to

22 OCTOBER 1940
Ideal with matters that are not contained

Mr. McLAUGHLIN:
I do not think
the right hon. Gentleman can be under
any misapprehension with regard to the

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Then I will not
refer further to that particular matter.
Many people are interned to-day who are
there on lying stories of neighbours, or
"B" men, procured probably in like
manner. As a layman, I have always
understood it to be an axiom of British
criminal law that a person was innocent
until proved guilty.
That being so, I
cannot see why these internees should be
treated as criminals, or, as stated by a
member of the Government, as "potential
criminals," in which case they are at
least entitled to be granted the privileges
of political prisoners. Surely it is unjust
to place these internees in criminal
prisons, or place them with criminals and
treat them as such.
We have been told this ship is what
might be termed a "luxury ship." Well,
I have visited the ship, and I do not consider it any such thing, and I hope that
for the sake of the men's health the ship
will be evacuated as soon as possible.
Surely any reasonable-minded person will
agree that it is not right to have 50 men
in one mess, measuring about 45 feet by
25 feet by 6 feet, where they have their
meals and are confined from 4 p.m. until
10 a.m.-18 hours at a stretch. In that
mess they are forced to sleep in hammocks
suspended from the ceiling with all the
portholes closed from 7 p.m. to 7 a.m,
There they have to depend for air on
ventilation from the ship's ventilators,
and I have no hesitation in saying that
this state of affairs is bound to injure the
health of these men. Physically I should
think they could not stand it for very
long, and I trust their health, for this
reason alone, will not be permanently
impaired.
recreation.
Again, we have the barbed wire walks
where men are obliged to take their
There were 183 men, all
Catholics, on the ship the day I visited
it, taking walking exercises in a stretch of
about 160 feet, all of which had barbed
wire on each side and overhead. In fact
in most parts the men had to walk in
single file lest their clothing should be
torn if they brushed against the barbed
wire on either side. If two of them
walked alongside they were liable, not
only to get their clothes torn, but to get
injured if either of them happened to
jostle against the other. Surely that is
not fair treatment? In fact, on this ship
you have barbed wire all around you. I
myself almost got my coat torn. I do
know of one mother visiting the ship,
who got her coat torn by this barbed
wire.
We have also been told that all untried
prisoners, including these internees, are
entitled to weekly visits from their
relatives; but, owing to conditions created
by the Government, it is physically impossible for them to receive these weekly
visits. Under what authority is the
Government entitled to create conditions
which on the Government's own admission
deprive these men of weekly visits from
their relatives? It has also been stated
by the Government in another place that
the men on this ship petitioned, or, if
you wish, requested to have this ship
kept on. This statement the internees
emphatically deny, and I believe them.
What they do demand is that, until they
are charged with some offence and
brought to trial, they should be treated
not as criminals or as potential criminals,
but as political prisoners. As a public
representative I agree that their demand
is only reasonable and should be granted.
Lastly, we have the men on hunger
strike. Let us hope the Government
will not allow themselves to be responsible for the death of any of these
innocent men, who went on hunger strike
against being detained in prison without
any charge being preferred against them,
and who have agreed to give any bail
required and sign any undertaking to
obey the law and keep the peace.
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
Mr. ROBB: The hon. Senator's
question falls into two sections, that
dealing with the "Al Rawdah," and that
concerning an incident which took place
sixteen years ago.
As regards the "Al Rawdah," when
it became necessary in June of this year
for reasons of military expediency to
evacuate Londonderry Prison, alternative accommodation had to be found for
the internees. At that time no accommodation on land was available, and at
the suggestion of the military authorities
it was decided to procure a ship. With
the co-operation of the Home Office, the
War Office, and the Ministry of
Shipping the S.S. "Al Rawdah" was
chartered and equipped. The internees
were removed to the ship at the beginning of September.
re-
Afterwards representations were
ceived from the Imperial authorities
requesting that the ship should be
evacuated as soon as possible. The Government of Northern Ireland has acceded
to this request, and the ship will be
evacuated as soon as alternative accommodation has been obtained. The hon.
Senator will see, therefore, that no
question of policy on the part of this
Government is involved.
In view of the fact that this request
for the evacuation of the ship has come
from the Imperial authorities, the British
Government is being asked to bear the
entire cost of the chartering of the
"Al Rawdah," except the cost of the
prison officials.
When the news of the intended
evacuation became known a petition was
received from the internees on board expressing their resentment at being moved.
back to the prisons. A copy of the
petition has already appeared in the local
Press, but it would be contrary to established practice and to the interests of
prisoners in general for the Government.
to give publicity to such documents.
Complaints have been received from
time to time from persons who have been
at some time or other interned in Belfast
or Londonderry Prison, but no complaint
has been received from those on board
the "Al Rawdah," except the petition to
which I have already referred.
Interned persons may be released unconditionally, or subject to such obligations and restrictions as the civil
authority may direct in accordance with
his powers under the regulations. These
regulations enable the civil authority to
prohibit internees on release from residing in or entering into any particular
or areas, but it has not recently
been necessary to exercise this power in
respect of released internees.
area
For some time past internees, whose
release has been granted otherwise than
unconditionally, have each been required
to enter into personal bail of £5 to keep
the peace and be of good behaviour for
the space of 24 calendar months, with
one approved surety in a similar sum.
There is no compulsion on interned
persons to reveal information about their
fellow internees, comrades or neighbours
although many of those
those who have
appealed against their internment have
volunteered such information.
As regards the incident which took
place in 1924, the facts are substantially
as stated by the hon. Senator.
Mr. MCALLISTER: It has come to
me as a strange. thing that this ship was
requisitioned by the military authorities
and, therefore, at the instance of the
Imperial Government. In other words,
the Imperial Government are prepared to
give German internees a proper camp in
the Isle of Man and treat them as civilised
human beings, but when it comes to Irish
Nationalist sympathisers, whether they
express themselves by constitutional
means or whether they do not, they
differentiate between them and the
Germans and consign them to a prison
ship in Northern Ireland, their natural
home and their motherland. That,
surely, will come as a surprise not only
here. but elsewhere. The British Government have not consigned their political
opponents and political internees into
prisons inhabited by ordinary criminals
and treated them as ordinary criminals.
It was said in the last war that
German internees were. treated as the
best in the land, but Irish internees are
refused the right of stating their case in
public so that the public may know the
charges alleged against them by a
political partisan Government. I do not
know who constitutes this committee that
hears the appeals of these men, but I
would like, to ask the right hon. Gentleman this question. Have any of the lay

Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: This House

best answer to those. Members who have

our country in time of need, getting practically no money for it. I strongly
repudiate that statement of the hon.
Member.
I have seen an extract from a letter of

and egg
for breakfast-that at a time
when the loyal people of this Province
are being rationed to an extraordinarily
low degree so far as bacon is concerned,
and when eggs are at a phenomenal price.
He also expressed the hope that the war
would last forever, because they were
having the happiest of times. I understand that unless these men appeal to the
Advisory Committee they are to be
detained during the period of hostilities,
and this man wishes to remain and to end
his life on board this ship.
Those hon. Members who are so much
concerned about these internees are barking up the wrong tree. The internees
themselves have repudiated them by sending in a petition to be allowed to stay on
the ship. They are men who are under
suspicion. All over England and Scotland
there are internment camps for men under
suspicion, and across the border in our
own island men are detained under worse
conditions than those that prevail in our
prison ship or in the Derry and Crumlin
Road prisons. I would like to know if
the Government of Northern Ireland is
in a position to introduce legislation to
deal with these men by means of military
tribunals so that they can be brought to
justice, and thus do away with this farce
of paying about £1,300 a week to feed
these men and maintain the ship, because
they are men who are alleged to be active
enemies of the British Government and
the Government of Northern Ireland.

Mr. ROBB: I have to report to the

Societies Bill-
Second Reading
22 OCTOBER 1940
Northern Ireland, in the name and on
behalf of His Majesty the King, has been
pleased to give his Assent to the
following Bill agreed upon by both
Houses: :-
Local Government Inquiries.
Societies (Miscellaneous Provisions)
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This Bill is intended to
assist the administration of the business
of societies under war-time conditions,
and I do not think that its provisions
will raise doubt or alarm in any quarter.
It follows the terms of a similar Measure
passed in the Imperial Parliament this
year, and bearing in mind the fact that
a large part of the friendly society business in Northern Ireland is carried on
by branches of cross-Channel societies it
is well that we should keep in line.
The definition Clause defines a society
as any trade union, friendly society or
building society, and such unincorporated
society as is mentioned in section seven
of the Building Societies Act, 1874, and
any society registered or deemed to be
registered in Northern Ireland under
the Industrial and Provident Societies
Acts (Northern Ireland), 1893 to 1929.
as
In broad outline the features of the
Bill are the modification of requirements
to meetings, the appointment of
officers, and the amendment of rules,
subject to the approval of the Registrar
of Friendly Societies; the suspension of
quinquennial valuations; the protection
of the rights of members of friendly
societies engaged in war service; a
simplified method of amalgamation for
building societies and trade unions; and
sanction for the establishment by the
societies of funds for the purchase on
behalf of members of National Savings
Certificates, Defence. Bonds, and other
Government securities.
I now turn to the Clauses. Clauses 1
to 3 deal with the modification of rules
and statutory requirements, and the
main provision in this behalf is in Clause
1. This Clause enables the rules of
societies and statutory requirements
relating to societies to be relaxed in some
of the
of a
respects during the period
emergency under the authority
direction to be given by the Registrar.
Thus any requirement that meetings are
to be held at specified times or periods
or at specified places may be dispensed
with, and trustees and officers may be
continued in office although their tenure
of office would otherwise have ceased.
Clause 2 of the Bill gives power for
the amendment of rules of the registered
branches of friendly societies.
Clause 3 suspends during the period of
the emergency the obligation upon
friendly societies to obtain a quinquennial
valuation unless such valuation
expressly required by the. Registrar.
is
Clause 4 will alter the law with respect
to the amalgamation and transfer of
engagements of building societies. It will
give the Registrar discretion to waive the
requirement for a two-thirds majority of
the shares of the society, and by subclause (3) it will give the Registrar some
control over the proceedings for the disbe a
solution of a society which may
method of cloaked amalgamation with
another society.
Clause. 5 will alter the law with respect
to the amalgamation and transfer of
engagements of trade unions. It requires
a special resolution and makes provision
for the vesting of property without any
conveyance, or assignment. The Registrar
is given power to waive the consent of a
two-thirds majority of members under
certain conditions.
Clauses 4 and 5 will not be limited in
their effect to the period of the national
emergency.
Clause 6 will enable members of
friendly societies in the service of the
naval, military or air forces of the
Crown to continue in good standing as
members although they fail to attend
meetings or to pay their subscriptions.
Clause 7 gives powers to establish
savings schemes.
The Bill is drafted in simple terms,
and I do not think it requires further
exposition on my part.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time".
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Expiring Laws Bill

(4) Nothing in this section shall be

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
6, sub-clause (4), after the word "favourable" to insert the words "to such
persons. I mentioned this small matter
on the Second Reading. I am afraid I
am still unable to satisfy myself that we
can do without the insertion of some few
words such as those I have on the Paper.
It seems to me that there is a possible
ambiguity in the sub-clause as it stands,
and we do not want to send a Bill from
this House with any ambiguity whatever
about it. We do not desire that anything
we enact should be the cause of any unnecessary controversy.
Let us look at the sub-clause for a
moment, taking the view that an
ambiguity is possible. First we have got
to remember that these friendly societies
have, as a rule, a common fund. They
are mutual societies. They have a common
fund which is used either for carrying to
reserve or, it may be, for increasing the
benefits payable to the various members.
There is, therefore, the possiblity of a
conflict of interests. The possibility of
friendliness to the one member we desire
to protect might conflict with friendliness
to the whole body of the rest of the
members.
That
That is a possibility.
seems to me to gather strength from the
proviso further up on the same page. It
seems to me to indicate that it is not intended that the member should have
things all his own way. We are invited,
as it were, to look on both sides of the
question.
way
I thought that perhaps there would be
some protection in the of a disinclination on the part of the registrar to
register any rule conflicting with the intention of this Bill, but I cannot find in
the principal Act, the Act of 1916, any
power to refuse to register a rule unless
it is in conflict with the Acts of Parliament relating to these matters. That
would seem to be begging the question,
and we cannot get any help there. The
only precedent I can think of is in the
Schedule to the Hire Purchase Bill which
we passed a short time ago, a Clause not
of very blessed memory. In that case
"favourable to him" was definitely inserted. I am afraid we must insert these
few words, and, accordingly, I beg to
move.
Question proposed, "That those words
be there inserted."
Societies Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
I
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The hon. and learned Member started his
argument with the premise, "Taking the
view that an ambiguity was possible."
do not think an ambiguity is possible,
and consequently it does not seem to me
that these words are necessary or advisable. What the Clause says is that any
provision in the rules of a friendly society
which purports to deprive persons of
membership by reason of their service in
any of the naval, military, or air forces
of the Crown, shall not apply as respects
any such service from 1st September,
1939, to the end of the war. Then it
goes on to say that where any person
serving as aforesaid was at the commencement of his service, or on 1st September, 1939, a member of a friendly
society and thereafter ceases to pay contributions to the society, he shall not
for that reason cease to be a member of
the society. The last sub-section shall
not affect any contract of assurance upon
human life in respect of which there are
separate premiums. Then the fourth
sub-clause says:
Nothing in this section shall be taken
to prevent a friendly society providing
by its rules for the continuance of the
membership of persons serving as
aforesaid upon terms more favourable
than those provided by this Section.
mean
I cannot see how any person can possibly say that those words can
anything except more favourable to the
member. There is no ambiguity I can
see or conceive in the words as they
stand. The Bill says that a member is
not to lose his rights in particular cases,
and then it goes on to say that a society
may alter its rules so as to allow that
person to continue his membership on
terms more favourable than those in the
Section. Can that mean anything except
more favourable to the member?
Mr. LESLIE: I am afraid it can.
Mr. ROBB: I do not think it can,
and I have not found any person with
whom I have discussed the point who
can conceive that it can mean more
favourable to anybody except the member. Therefore the insertion of these
words is quite unnecessary. If the proposed amendment was made it would
mean that the Bill would have to be reprinted and sent back to another place.
That would involve a certain amount of
delay and expense, but, apart from that,
I cannot conceive that any person could
possibly construe this sub-clause in any
other way than I have suggested. For
these reasons I cannot accept the Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: I am afraid I am not
a bit convinced. As regards the expense

any excuse for this House departing from
its primary duty, which is to revise intended legislation that comes before us
from another place. There is the
possibility that some society or otherbecause we are dealing with a large, number, the composition of each of which we
do not know-possibly from crankiness,
might take the view I have suggested. It
seems to me that this question of the continuance of membership affects both sides,
the man himself and the whole body of
the society of which he is a member.
Therefore, I think we ought to put it
beyond all doubt that the prohibition is
intended to operate only in the case of a
rule which might prejudice the member.
The whole, object of the Bill is to prevent
a member being prejudiced, and it does
not say so. What other Members think
I do not know because they have not told
us, but I cannot withdraw the Amendment. I am quite. willing to withdraw it
now and bring it up again on Third
Reading if that will suit the right hon.
Gentleman.
Mr. ROBB: No, it will not, because
I cannot see there is any doubt whatever
as to what is intended.

Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Questions to Minister.
GROWING OF WHEAT.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can state
if, in view of the fact that Canada has
sufficient wheat to keep Britain supplied
for three years, it is necessary for the
farmers of Northern Ireland to grow as
much wheat as they possibly can?
The farmers of Northern Ireland have
been appealed to to grow as much wheat
as they possibly can. Two or three days
ago we read in the public Press that
Canada has sufficient wheat to keep
Britain supplied for three years. I am
sure the Ministry of Agriculture will
welcome this opportunity of making an
announcement on this subject.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The hon. Member will realise that there
is a vast difference between there being
large supplies of wheat in Canada and an
abundant supply in this country. Moreover, wheat imported into this country
cannot be released as feeding stuffs for
livestock. It is, therefore, essential that
farmers in Northern Ireland should grow
as much wheat as possible to feed their
livestock, as supplies of wheat offal will
be far below the pre-war level.
Mr. LESLIE: The right hon. Gentleman did not tell us why the wheat
received in this country from Canada cannot be released for use, in Northern
Ireland.
Mr. ROBB: I said it could not be
released for the feeding of livestock.
Mr. LESLIE: Even so, why?
Mr. ROBB: I am not very familiar
with these things, but if you have wheat
in Canada you have to get it over here.
There is, of course, a difficulty about
transport, and consequently I presume
the Ministry thinks it necessary to conserve wheat supplies for human consumption and not to release them for livestock
in the meantime.
Major DOBBS: Then the farmers can
take it that it is of the greatest importance to grow wheat?
Mr. ROBB: Certainly.
Mr. TAYLOR: Considering the difficulties of transport, and more especially
sea transport, does the right hon. Gentleman not think that instead of the farmer
being restricted he should be encouraged
to grow more wheat, and other essential
foodstuffs?
Mr. ROBB: I always understood he
was encouraged.
Major BOYLE: Can the right hon.
Gentleman inform us if wheat is largely
used by farmers in Northern Ireland for
feeding livestock?
Mr. ROBB: I am informed that it is.
WINTER OATS.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can state
if the Ministry of Agriculture is in favour
of winter oats being grown, when possible,
to ensure early harvesting. Thirty years
ago the sowing of oats in autumn-that,
of course, is the black variety of oatswas very popular in some localities, but
it has now apparently become a thing of
the past. I am pretty certain that those
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
5 NOVEMBER 1940
winter or autumn oats ripen about three
weeks earlier than spring sown oats.
This is a matter of very great importance
to all the farmers of Northern Ireland
seeing that we are about five weeks later
than the South of England. It is a comparatively easy matter now, in the second
year of compulsory tillage, to get all the
necessary ploughing done, and even to
get the sowing done, but the question of
harvesting is a very difficult one.. Unless
the weather is extremely good and kind
farmers will next year find the greatest
difficulty in harvesting their crops.
Therefore, if they can sow some oats that
will ripen two or three weeks earlier it
will give them some two or three weeks
more time to harvest those oats.
Mr. ROBB: Yes, the Ministry of
Agriculture is in favour of the sowing of
winter oats, but the hon. Member will
appreciate that it is now too late to sow
winter oats this year.
INSURANCE OF CORN STOCKS.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the Leader
of the House whether he can state if the
Government propose to take steps to
enable, the farmers of Northern Ireland
to insure their stocks of corn against
enemy action.
It is very natural for the farmers to
try to thrash and sell their corn now.
This means there will be a tremendous
supply very shortly. A farmer cannot
insure against his stacks of corn, wheat,
oats, or barley being destroyed by any
enemy action. He can only insure against
the ordinary fire risks. If he could insure
at a reasonable rate against the stacks
being set on fire by enemy action, and if
the Government could see their way to increase the minimum rate on the sale of
wheat, oats and barley by a shilling or so
more per hundredweight in the spring
than in the early winter it would be an
inducement for farmers to put off thrashing, at any rate a certain amount of their
corn crop, until later, and it would do
away with the great congestion there will
be this month and next month at the
millers.
Mr. ROBB: There is nothing to prevent any farmer effecting such insurance
if he so desires, but such insurance is not
compulsory. The War Risks Insurance
Act, 1939, applies to Northern Ireland,
but only goods to the value of £1,000 or
over come under its compulsory provisions. When the Act was being brought
into operation the question of its extension to the insurance of agricultural products was considered at length by the
responsible authorities at Westminster, in
consultation with the Agricultural Departments. As a result of those consultations
the "War Risks Insurance (General
Exceptions) Order" of the 2nd March,
1940, provided that goods of the descriptions specified in the First Schedule to
that Order should be deemed not to be
insurable under Part 2 of the War Risks
Insurance Act. Among the goods mentioned in the Schedule are:
"Growing crops, plants and trees,
livestock; and in relation to a person
carrying on the business of producing
agricultural products the following
goods produced by that person on land
occupied by him in the course of that
business, that is to say, agricultural
products and articles of food and drink
wholly or partly manufactured or derived from any such products and
fleeces and the skins of animals.'
""
As I have already indicated, however,
it is open to any farmer to effect voluntary insurance on either his growing crops
or agricultural products stored on his
farm. Incidentally, I may mention for
the information of the hon. Member that
the National Farmers' Union and the
Uister Farmers' Union have in operation
at the moment a scheme whereby such insurances can be effected.
Major DOBBS: I am very glad indeed to have the names of those two
societies with which insurance can be
effected. A few days ago I personally
tried to effect insurance on stacks of oats,
wheat, and so on, and after the matter
had been considered for a few days I
was definitely informed that such insurance could not be effected in Belfast,
and, I presume, in Northern Ireland,
against enemy action.
PENSIONS AT 65 YEARS OF AGE.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask
the Leader of the House whether he
can state how many actual contributions are required by persons reaching
the age of 65 years, and whether franked
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
weeks count for the qualifying period;
whether it is a fact that persons who do
not qualify for the pension at the age of
65 years are thereupon deprived of all
unemployment benefits, the dole, public
assistance, or any benefits under the
Unemployment Insurance Acts or any
other Acts.
So far as my information goes, I understand that any insured person not
entitled to an old-age pension at 65
years of age is automatically deprived of
the dole, public assistance, or any benefits under the National Health Insurance Acts. If a person, through bad
times, such as the closing of mills or lack
of employment, is unfortunate enough
not to have the necessary number of
stamps to qualify for a pension at 65,
from 65 to 70 he is practically on the
scrap heap and can obtain neither the
dole nor public assistance. That is my
information, and it is certainly a very
serious state of affairs for a person who
has probably worked practically all his
life and then, through no fault of his.
own but because of bad times, he loses his
employment and is no longer able to
keep his card stamped to qualify him for
a pension when he reaches 65 years of
age. That unfortunate person is left
with practically no assistance until
he is 70.
If that is the existing state of affairs
I trust the Government will take steps
to see that these people, at the period of
their lives between 65 and 70, are
properly looked after, and are at least
given the privileges those who have
received a pension at 65 are entitled to.
If they are not entitled to the dole they
should at least receive public assistance.
Mr. ROBB: The facts are pretty much
as the hon. Member has stated. In
order to qualify for a contributory oldage pension at the age of 65-60 in the
case of a woman-104 contributions must
have been paid irrespective of "franked"
weeks. If, however, a claimant on reaching the age of 60 had not been continuously insured for a period of ten
years he must also have had an average
of not less than 39 contributions a year
for the three contribution years immediately preceding the date on which he
attained the age of 65, or 60 in the case
of a woman. For the purpose of this
average contributions are credited in
respect of weeks during which the
claimant was incapable of work by
reason of sickness and in respect of weeks
during which he was available for, but
unable to obtain, employment.
Any person reaching the age of 65, in
the case of a man or 60 in the case of a
woman, ceases to be entitled to sickness
and disablement benefits under the
Health Insurance Acts to benefits under
the Unemployment Acts, and to assistance under the Unemployment Assistance
Act whether or not a title to pension is
established. Where no pension is granted
any title to outdoor relief remains
unaltered.
A woman who was insured immediately
before her 60th birthday or on 1st July,
1940, when the age for pensions for
women was lowered, and who does not
satisfy the statutory conditions for a
pension to which I have referred can,
however, subject to certain conditions,
get 10s a week during incapacity.
Mr. LESLIE: Is it the case that any
person who employs a man of 65 or upwards, subject to that disability of being
incompetent to receive benefits, will have
to pay the unemployment stamp for that
person, or has there been any alteration?
Mr. ROBB: I believe he has to pay
the employer's contribution only.
Mr. LESLIE: The employer pays,
although the man cannot get any benefit
whatever?
Mr. ROBB: Yes.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Do "franked"
weeks count as a qualification for pension
at 65, and is there any limit to the
number of "franked" weeks as a qualification for pension?
Mr. ROBB: I stated in my reply that
in order to qualify for a contributory
old-age pension at 65, 104 contributions
must be made, irrespective of “franked”
weeks.
PENSIONS FOR BLIND PERSONS.
The following questions stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether blind persons are eligible. for
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
5 NOVEMBER 1940
the National Health Insurance Pension
at the age of 65 years, or the Old Age
Pension at the age of 70 years, and if
not, what steps do the Government
propose to take to ensure that all blind
persons, on reaching the age of 65 or 70
get the Old Age and Supplementary
Pensions, and that their infirmity does
not deprive them of any benefits under
the Old Age Pensions Act or other
Acts.
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what powers the
Government have to ensure that County
Councils adequately look after all blind
persons, and particularly to
ensure
that blind persons reaching the age of
65 or 70 years get from the County
Council pensions or weekly grants at
least equal to what they would be
entitled to under the Pensions Acts
and Supplementary Pensions Acts.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: To save time I
will deal with these two questions
together. Blind persons, owing to their
infirmity, have been deprived of supplementary pensions. The Armagh County
Council, owing to lack of funds, are not
paying full supplementary pensions, and
if they did pay such a pension to each
individual entitled to it it would mean a
rate of 9d in the £ on the ratepayers of
County Armagh. I understand that no
person at present applying for a supplementary pension to the Armagh County
Council gets more than 5s a week, for
the simple reason that the county council
cannot find the, money to pay any more.
I know of a number of cases where blind
persons have no other income beyond their
pension, and it is a very serious hardship
that because a person is blind he should
be deprived of what he is entitled to under
the Act, namely a supplementary pension.
I am stating a fact when I say that
every blind person in County Armagh
who has no other income beyond his old
age pension or supplementary pension is
deprived of at least 4s 6d, and in some
cases 6s 6d, a week owing to the Government not doing its duty and placing this
burden on the county councils. Surely
these poor blind people are entitled to
more consideration in view of the fact
that they will not receive a pension unless
it is proved by an eye specialist that they
cannot do any class of work. The Government have a responsibility towards these
blind
persons, and if they cannot see their
way to bring in a Bill to place
these people under the Supplementary
Pensions Act the same as other
people they should introduce an Act to
force the county councils to do their duty
by giving the maximum supplementary
pension to every blind person entitled
to it.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I would like to
put this point of view before the right
hon. Gentleman and the House.
In my
experience of the administration of the
Blind Persons Act the greatest impediment to people who are almost totally
blind getting the pension is the very
drastic and unfair wording of the Section
setting forth the qualification for a
pension. I have not the Act before me,
but my recollection is that in order to
qualify for a pension a person must be so
blind as to be unable to do any work for
which eyesight is essential. Owing to the
wording of that Section the Ministry and
the umpire have had to turn down applicants who owing to their blindness were
unable to earn their livelihood. I suggest
that the words in the Section should be
altered to read that a person must be so
blind as to be unable to earn his or her
livelihood.
Take the case of a woman who has
been engaged in sewing all her life. Her
eyes become so bad that she is unable to
sew. The inspector goes into the house
and finds her making a cup of tea. He
decides that as she is able to make that
cup of tea she is not unable to do work
for which eyesight is essential. The
Ministry and the umpire have been compelled to refuse pensions when they knew
as a fact that the applicants would, owing
to their blindness, be unable to get any
employment to eke out a living. When
social services are being provided on such
an expensive scale surely the most deserving class to receive the full benefit of this
legislation is the blind. The number of
blind persons to whom I refer must be
comparatively small, and the Government
should consider amending that particular
Section of the Act so as to make it cover
the cases of people who are so blind as to
be unable to earn their livelihood.
Mr. ROBB:
ROBB: The point to which
Senator McAllister has referred does not
arise on either of the two questions.

When we were discussing the Blind
Persons Act great difficulty was experienced by everybody in arriving at a
definition of blindness. At present we
are administering the Acts as they stand.
Possibly at some future time Parliament
may see fit to alter the definition regarding blind persons.
Regarding the general question on
which Senator McLaughlin has spoken,
he said the Government had failed in
their duty.
Parliament has cast upon
the county councils the duty of looking
after these people, and under the law
as it stands it is the duty of the county
councils to look after them. It is a fact
that in Northern Ireland, except perhaps in the case of the County Borough
of Belfast, the county councils have been
slow to take advantage of their powers
applying to these unfortunate people.
The Ministry of Home Affairs has within comparatively recent times circulated
the county councils no fewer than four
times to impress upon them the desirability of utilising the powers Parliament
has given them in regard to these people.

Mr. TAYLOR: Apart from the help

organisations which look after the
interests and welfare of the blind
population. These include voluntary
agencies receiving grants from local
authorities, clinics for the prevention and
treatment of blindness, and workshops

There are also schemes for the home
teaching of the blind, to fit them for
various crafts, for the marketing of goods
made by blind persons, and for the provision of books in Braille.
These various activities, in addition to
the direct assistance available under the
local authorities and the pensions under
the Old Age Pension Act, amount to a
complete and comprehensive scheme to
help the blind. Belfast Corporation
disburses over £6,000, plus any other
sums as may be available from Government grants or other sources, so that the
blind are pretty well looked after in the
city.

Questions to Minister.
SUPPLEMENTARY PENSIONS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if widows
under sixty years of age and the
orphans of deceased insured persons
whose mothers are under sixty years
of age are entitled to a supplementary
pension under the National Health
and Unemployment Insurance Acts;
and, if not, what steps the Government propose to take to ensure that
widows under sixty years of age, and
the orphans of deceased insured persons whose widows are under sixty, get
a supplementary pension, as is the
case of widows over sixty; and
whether the Supplementary Pensions
Acts, Schemes and Regulations in
Northern Ireland are identical with
those in Great Britain for insured
persons reaching the age of sixty and
sixty-five years, and for widows,
orphans, and blind pensioners.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I think the
question speaks for itself. We all know
that a widow under sixty, particularly
a young widow left with a large family,
finds it very hard to live and earn a
livelihood to keep her home and family
together. I hope I am mistaken in
thinking that a widow of that class is
not entitled to a supplementary pension.
If I am not I sincerely trust the Government will bring in an amending Bill
to ensure that widows of deceased insured persons under sixty will get
supplementary pensions not only for
themselves but in respect of their
children. I know instances where
widows were left with young families
and they find it very hard to manage
their homes and look after their
children; so much so that they cannot
get out to look for work. We all know
that a widow under sixty finds it much
harder to live than does a widow over
sixty, because the younger widow may
have greater responsibilities. She may
have a large family of young children,
and I sincerely hope the Government
will give this matter their sympathetic
attention. The latter part of the
question speaks for itself.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The answer to the first part of the
question is in the negative; as to the
second part there are no proposals either
here or in Britain to reduce the pensionable age further. The answer to the
third part of the question is in the
affirmative. In this, as in all other
cases, a line has to be drawn somewhere.
It is true there are hard cases below the
line, but when the age was fixed at sixty
it was a compromise age and there is no
proposal to reduce it further at present.
POSTAGE OF NEWSPAPERS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major Boyle:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether a daily newspaper published in
England, and received by an inhabitant of Northern Ireland, may be
transmitted by post to a relative or
friend in Eire.
Major BOYLE: Since the outbreak of
war the Government has impressed upon
all of us the necessity of saving money
to lend and also to save paper to make
more paper. Many have been doing this
and have been sharing daily and weekly
papers, magazines and periodicals with
their friends who were able to send them
from England to Northern Ireland and
from here to friends and relations in
Southern Ireland. Our enemies have no
doubt been making use of the paper post
for giving information by means of
pricking-which I understand is the
433 Questions to Minister
12 NOVEMBER 1940

Supplementary Pensions
technical term-and the censor is, no
doubt, trying to stop it.
The first intimation we received of our
not being able to send papers any more
was on receiving a paper back with the
intimation that it was irregular to send
paper by private individuals, and
permits were only issued to private individuals in exceptional or necessitous
cases. I would ask that the public, who
are willing to accept a further loss of
their liberties if it will help to win the
war, should be informed by the Government of these particular liberties being
withdrawn. I think it is only fair that
they should be so informed.
Major DOBBS: Before the right hon.
Gentleman answers that question I
should like to ask him whether this
censorship which has been established
between England and Northern Ireland
has been of the slightest use. If it has
been of the slightest use then I have
nothing more to say because we are prepared to put up with any annoyance if
it will be of value in winning the war.
But I am a little doubtful whether it is
of value in this case, and I should like to
have a definite answer on that point.
A short time ago I had a postcard from
my brother in England. He writes a
very clear hand. On one side was my
name and address, and on the other side
was, "Thanks for cheque. Love." and
my brother's initials. That was passed
by the censor. I have had a lot of correspondence from a bank in England on
trustee and other matters. It comes in
a perfectly plain envelope, well stuck
down. On the back of the envelope
is embossed, "Barclay's Bank, Ltd.,
Cambridge." Why has every one of those
envelopes been opened? I am a little bit
sceptical about the way this censorship is
being worked, and I should like to be
definitely told whether it is of any real
use or not.
Mr. ROBB: I am not in a position to
give an official reply to the question
asked by Senator Dobbs. The censorship is, of course, not under the control
of this Government, but I would assume
that the continuation of the censorship is
a fact from which one would infer that
it has been found of use.
As regards the question asked by
Senator Boyle, the answer is in the
negative. From inquiries which I have
made I understand that a person in Northern Ireland wishing to send a newspaper by post to Eire may only do so by
placing an order with the publisher or
with a newsagent possessing a permit
issued by or on behalf of the Director of
Postal and Telegraph Censorship. No
newsagent, however, may send a copy of
a newspaper which has been out of his
possession or one that has been handed
to him by the person wishing to have it
sent to Eire-the copy must be drawn
from the newsagent's own stock.
The provisions governing these matters
are contained in Statutory Rules and
Orders, 1940, No. 1190, made on
6th July, 1940, by the Minister of
Information.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Can the right
hon. Gentleman inform us whether these
Regulations were made by the Imperial
Government, which controls postal
matters, or by this Government.
Mr. ROBB: They were made, as I
have stated, by the Minister of Information, Mr. Duff Cooper.

Supplementary Pensions (Determination
of Need and Assessment of Needs)
Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1940.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That
the Regulations made by the Ministry of
Labour on the 24th day of July, 1940,
under sub-section (3) of Section 33 of
the Unemployment Assistance Act (Northern Ireland), 1934, as applied by
Part II of the Old Age and Widows'
Pensions Act (Northern Ireland), 1940,
prescribing the manner in which the
need of persons, in receipt of old age
pensions or widows' pensions, who apply
for supplementary pensions shall be
determined and their needs assessed, be
hereby approved."
Supplementary Pensions
Education Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
These Regulations have been made
under Section 33 (3) of the Unemployment Assistance Act (Northern Ireland),
1934, as applied by Part II of the Old
Age and Widows' Pensions Act (Northern
Ireland), 1940, and are identical with
those. in force in Great Britain. They
prescribe the manner in which the need
of an applicant for a supplementary
pension is to be determined and his
needs assessed. Their provisions are very
similar to the rules governing the grant
of unemployment assistance, but as they
are dealing with an older class of the
community-and old age often brings in
its train additional needs-they are somewhat more liberal in their terms.
In the. assessment of needs, for
instance, the scale allowance of a
pensioner living alone and without means
other than his pension is 19s 6d per week,
as compared with 17s 6d per week for
unemployment assistance. Again, the
computation of available. resources is
more favourable to the applicant. Where
there is a small amount earned by the
applicant, the first 5s is left out of
account as compared with 3s for unemployment assistance, and there are also
allowances in respect of superannuation
payments and sickness payments as provided in the Pensions Act. Power is
given to take into account special needs
due to winter conditions or other circumstances of an exceptional character, and
to increase the allowance accordingly.
A further concession has been promised
in regard to savings lent to the State,
but this requires fresh legislation and
cannot, therefore, be included in the
Regulations at present. These Regulations were made on 24th July, 1940, and
under Section 46 of the Unemployment
Assistance Act they must be submitted
for approval to each House of Parliament
within twelve months from that date.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Education (Emergency Provisions) Bill, to

Education (Emergency Provisions) Bill.
Resolved: "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: This Bill arises out of the
necessity for the provision of air-raid
protection at schools and training colleges.
In all elementary schools within an area
that will be prescribed by the Ministry in
consultation with the Ministry of Public
Security, it shall be the duty of the
education authority to provide air-raid
protection approved by the Ministry of
Education after consultation with the
Ministry of Public Security. Outside
that area the managers of schools or the
education authority may provide such
protection. In the case of secondary
schools and training colleges the governors
are charged with the duty of providing
air-raid protection. In the case
elementary schools the education authority
will receive from the Government a grant
of 65 per cent. of the cost of air-raid
protection, and in the case of the other
schools the Government will contribute
50 per cent. of the cost. The local
education authority may contribute such
amount as they may see fit in the case of
secondary schools, training colleges, and
technical schools. Towards that expenditure, whoever incurs it, the Government will contribute 50 per cent. That
is the general scheme.
of
In Clause 4 power is taken to order
schools to be closed. The Ministry has
been advised that there are certain
schools in the compulsory area which will
not bear protection against air raids,
because, the Ministry are advised by
their technical officers, if there was an
explosion these schools would collapse.
because they would not bear the protection contemplated. The only remedy
is that those schools shall be closed. Of
course, that power would only be used
upon the highest technical advice given
to the Ministry and the local education
authority.
Clauses 5 and 6 relate to small matters
dealing with evacuated children. Clause
5 says:-
-

12 NOVEMBER 1940
The Ministry may, in accordance
with regulations to be made by it,
make or undertake to make to an
education authority or to managers,
grants equal to the amount of any
approved expenditure incurred by or
on
behalf of the authority or the
managers, as the case may be, on
educational services on account of
evacuated children in a reception
area, in respect of—
(a) The hiring of premises in the
reception area to supplement the
ordinary school accommodation;
(b) additional expenditure on the
conveyance of evacuated children
to school;
(c) other educational expenditure of a
kind which would not have been
incurred in respect of the children
if they had not been evacuated.
Clause 7 deals with a different matter.
I may say that no case has yet arisen
to which this Clause is applicable, but
it might arise. Before the war sanction
was given to the expenditure of money
upon the construction of new schools.
That construction has been held up by
the war. It may be that the existing
school is very badly in need of repair,
and it is not right that the school should
be continued in that building without
money being expended upon it to put it
in a proper state of repair. It was not
contemplated before the war that that
building should be further used as
school; it was intended that it should
be scrapped, but the replacement of the
school has been held up. There are
many cases where it is necessary in the
interests of the children that money
should be spent to put in repair these
condemned buildings, and power is taken
in Clause 7 for the Ministry to make a
grant towards effecting repairs of that
kind.
a
Clause 10 gives a definition of
approved air raid protection. It says
that-
"Approved air-raid protection" means
air-raid protection approved by
the Ministry after consultation
with the Ministry of Public
Security, and includes the provision of such additional artificial
light as may become necessary by
reason of the works of air-raid
protection;
The inclusion of artificial light was
inserted in another place at the request
of some managers. Air-raid protection
includes the bricking up of windows, and
it has been suggested that that will increase the charge for artificial light in
some schools to a considerable extent.
Accordingly the grant is to cover that
expenditure. "School" means a school
in receipt of grants from the Ministry. I
beg to move, "That the Bill be now read
a Second time."
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I desire to call
attention to Clause 7, which deals with
grants to voluntary schools. The object
of the Bill is apparently to provide airraid protection for school-children, and
that being so there should be no differentiation between voluntary schools and
other schools.
Mr. ROBB: There is none.
Mr. MCLAUGHLIN : There is a
differentiation to this extent; that one
gets a grant of 65 per cent. and the
other only 50 per cent. The 50 per cent.
grant applies to secondary schools as well
as to voluntary schools. In the Schedule
there is a distinction between schools
managed by an education authority and
those not managed by an education
authority. It is the latter I am referring
to. I suggest that in a scheme for the
protection of children against air raids
there should be no difference made and
that all schools should get a grant of 65
per cent. It is not fair to victimise one
particular section of education and favour
the other section by giving a grant of 65
per cent. in one case and only 50 per
cent. in the other.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
have often expressed the sincere hope
that Hitler would forget about Northern
Ireland when he is bombing places in
Great Britain, but whether he does or
not I suppose it is a wise thing to provide against
against Hitler giving us any
trouble. I am sorry there is no provision
made in this Bill to properly clothe
evacuees. I had hoped the Minister of
Education would himself have seen the
necessity of including in the Measure a
Clause to provide clothing for children
who were being evacuated to safe. areas
in the country. When children are
required to evacuate from Belfast I find
that parents have great difficulty in

new

am certainly of the opinion that it would

The right hon.
Mr. MAGUIRE:
Gentleman has stated that the grant of

the whole expenditure on air-raid protection is paid for by the education
authority, and I would like to know if

Mr. ROBB: Yes.
Mr. MAGUIRE: That is not really
the point. Voluntary schools should be
treated in exactly the same way as transferred schools. This is not ordinary
educational expenditure. The managers
of voluntary schools are not responsible
for the conditions that exist to-day and
I contend that for the protection of all

met by the education authority.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I would like to
supplement the argument of Senator
Maguire to this extent. I think if there
is one class of school that requires the
full grant to cover its expenditure it is
the voluntary school.
Mr. ROBB: There seems to be some
confusion about this matter. In respect
of elementary schools, transferred and
voluntary, the education authority bears
the whole cost, and the Government
contribute 65 per cent. In respect of
secondary schools the Government pays
50 per cent. to whoever incurs the cost of
air-raid protection. There are a few,
but not many, secondary schools controlled by education authorities. Most
of them are controlled by voluntary
managers, and towards the provision of
air-raid protection in secondary schools,
no matter who manages them, the
Government will contribute 50 per cent.
of the cost. The education authority
may contribute the whole or any part of
the cost. I may say that the Belfast
education authority has passed a resolution that so far as secondary schools in
their area are concerned they will pay
65 per cent. of the cost of air-raid protection. That leaves 35 per cent. to be
paid by the governors, but they will get
a grant of 50 per cent. from the Government, which means that a Board of
Governors in Belfast will have to pay
only 17 per cent. Should it become
necessary in other areas we hope the
education authorities will follow the
example of Belfast. As regards all
elementary schools, the entire cost will
be paid by the education authorities.
Major BOYLE: I understand that
such schools as a Royal school will have
to pay only 17 per cent. of the cost of
air-raid protection?
Mr. ROBB: If the Royal school were
happy enough to be located in Belfast
they would have to pay only 17 per
cent., but otherwise they may have to
pay 50 per cent. if the local education
authority will not make any contribution.
In so far as the local education authority
makes a contribution towards the protection of a secondary school it will be in
relief of the governors.
Major BOYLE: Are not the lives of
children of a Royal school as valuable
as those of other children? I would like
to see the Government paying the whole
cost. I cannot see why there should be
any difference made between a secondary
or elementary school in Belfast and a
Royal school outside Belfast.
Mr. ROBB: I find I was wrong in
stating that the Belfast Education

THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 26th November, 1940.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
