Death of Lord Craigavon.
TRIBUTES TO LATE PRIME
MINISTER.
COLONEL SINCLAIR'S DEATH.
Mr. SPEAKER: I feel that every
Member of this House will join with me
when I express the very deep regret that
we all feel at the sudden death of our
Prime Minister, and how sensible we all
must be of the great loss which we have
sustained. I am sure our heartfelt
sympathy will go out to Lady Craigavon
and family in their bereavement. I would
ask hon. Senators to stand for a moment.
The House stood in silence.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That this
House do now adjourn."
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
am sure the House would wish me to say
something, however inadequate, of the
feeling of deep sorrow and of the, distressing sense of loss with which Senators
have heard of the death of Lord
Craigavon. The suddenness of the call
which came to him has shocked us all and
left us bewildered. But through the mists
of our sorrow there is an uprush of
memories of what the Prime Minister had
done in his day for that Ulster which he
loved so well and served so long and
faithfully, and of the great qualities
which made him not only a great and
beloved leader, but a very dear friend to
so very many in our Province.
The service of his country was the
prime motive of Lord Craigavon's life.
The call to that service came to him first
when the South African War broke out,
and, answering it, he saw service in that
war, service done. faithfully and well.
When he returned it was suggested to
him that Ulster needed men in Parliament to fight her cause. With diffidence
he again accepted the challenge and once
he had put his hand to the plough he
never turned back. At Westminster he
soon was recognised not only as a foremost advocate of the cause of Ulster, but
a man of staunch worth and great
ability.
as
When the war broke out in 1914 he
took a leading part in organising the
Ulster Division. It was not found
possible for him to go with it to France,
but scope for his energies soon opened up
for him at Westminster. Eventually he
became Parliamentary Secretary to the
Admiralty, and a career rising to Cabinet
rank seemed assured for him there. But
his heart was always here with us in
Ulster, and when the. Government was
formed for Northern Ireland, he was
chosen with the acclamation of everyone
to be our first Prime Minister and that
he remained for nearly twenty years. To
build up a Government, to create an
administration, to establish a Parliamentary tradition was a difficult task,
but for that task he was peculiarly gifted
by nature and, very luckily for us,
equipped by experience. That this
youngest Parliament in the Empire has
been able to carry on its business so well
and to conduct its controversies in so
good a tradition was due in the main to
the directing hand of Lord Craigavon.
His qualities were just those we needed
most.
Political feeling has often run high in
this North of ours, and we often are led
away by heated feelings to say and to do
things we regret. But Lord Craigavon
was always unruffled in spirit and calm in
speech. His courage was absolute. He never
hedged an inch, but he was always equable
in temperament, unhurried, and generous
to his opponents. This staunchness in
times of danger, and this calmness in
days of excitement, made him a tower of
strength and evoked in his followers a
passion of loyalty, and in his opponents
a respect and admiration. These qualities and these achievements won for him
a place in history which, I feel sure, will
be ranked very high in the annals not only
447 Tributes to late Prime Minister
Tributes to late Prime Minister 448
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
of Ulster but of the British Commonwealth.
Of his personal relations with his
colleagues I need only say this: that they
trusted his judgment and his word, and
even the youngest of them never hesitated
to bring to him difficulties, knowing they
would get an understanding hearing of
the facts and the soundest of advice.
It is scarcely for me to say anything
here of his private life, but this I can
mention. Knowing the help and companionship which sustained him in his
home, this House would like to assure
Lady Craigavon of their deep sympathy
with her and her family at this time.
as a
Might I also say a word about Colonel
Sinclair, whose death has come
matter of such great regret to all of us.
He had won for himself the very highest
place in the profession he had adopted.
He virtually created the great school of
medicine attached to our University, and
in this House, of which he was one of the
earliest Members, he will be remembered
as a man who spoke but little, but he
spoke with great knowledge upon those
subjects on which he undertook to comment. In moving in and out amongst us
all those years he gained for himself a
high place in our affection and esteem.
I
Mr. MCALLISTER: I rise to voice the
sympathy of myself and my colleagues in
the Nationalist party in this House. We
meet to-day under the shadow of death,
which softens and brings closer together
the hearts of all men. The late Prime
Minister of Northern Ireland held views
which were diametrically opposed to those
we hold, and were, in fact, the very
antithesis of our views; but over his
grave we do not intend to discuss those
questions. I prefer to confine my remarks
to-day to Lord Craigavon as a man.
sat with him for four years in another
place, where I learned to know something
of him. He was, I need not say, a very
able leader of his party. Tenacious in
purpose, he put forth his arguments with
forcefulness, but he also recognised that
other Members, as well as himself, were
entitled to express their views. He
listened with patience and respect to the
replies to his various arguments, so long
as they were within the rules of Parliamentary procedure.
Those who have been in public life for
some years could not help but notice that
throughout his activities in the public
welfare the late Lord Craigavon was
nearly always accompanied by Lady
Craigavon. She was present to give him
her support and sympathy, to join with
him in his successes and in his disappointments, because everyone has disappointments. She was ever there to assuage
and soften those disappointments and to
console him. I am sure I speak wholeheartedly the views of the party to which
I have the honour to belong when I say
that we express to Lady Craigavon and
the members of her family our most profound sympathy in this tragic event.
We must recognise the fact that the
late Prime Minister was a great personality in Northern Ireland. I have no
hesitation in saying that on account of his
undoubted ability he was a most sagacious
leader of his party. That is beyond
question. By reason of his ability and
sagacity within his Government and party
and throughout his entire following in
Northern Ireland he established himself
on a pinnacle from which I say in the
most genuine and respectful way he
dominated all those who followed him.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to associate myself with the
Leader of the House and the hon. Member who has just sat down in their
references to the great loss we have
suffered by the death of our Prime
Minister. It would be very inappropriate if my colleagues and myself did
not pay tribute to one whom I know was
always very kind and considerate towards
the working classes of Northern Ireland.
I remember on one occasion speaking to
the Prime Minister when he said, "Yes,
I am satisfied that the workers would be
much more content if it was not for the
fear of unemployment which is hanging
over them." That in itself shows that he
was thoroughly familiar with the conditions of the workers in this Province.
He was criticised on one occasion for
saying that if they could not cure unemployment on the other side he would do
so here. I believe he had the will and
the inclination to do that, but, unfortunately, it was too big an undertaking
for one man. However, I am sure he
tried honestly to do his very best for
those people for whom he had so much
449 Tributes to late Prime Minister
Tributes to late Prime Minister 450
26 NOVEMBER 1940
sympathy. We know that he was very
sincere in all his activities on behalf of
the people of Northern Ireland. He
seemed to understand thoroughly and to
interpret correctly the feelings of everyone in our Province. He was one of ourselves. He had mixed with all the
people, and he knew exactly what they
felt and thought. I say in all sincerity
that we have lost a very great leader,
and we regret his passing very much.
We join whole-heartedly in the expression
of sympathy to Lady Craigavon, because
while the loss of our Prime Minister is
great for us, it must be much greater
for his wife and family.
He
It has been brought home to those of
us who are original Members of this
House how small in numbers we are
becoming. One after another is passing
away, and we have now lost another
valuable Member of this House in the
person of the late Colonel Sinclair.
did not often address the House, but
when he did speak he was listened to
with the greatest possible attention and
respect. The words that fell from his
lips were always very instructive, and by
his death we have lost a very valuable
Member of the Senate.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I am sorry I forgot to refer to the death of our late
very esteemed Member, Colonel Sinclair.
I would like to give expression to our
feelings of regret at his passing in one
sentence. In my estimate and in the
estimate of my colleagues he was a man,
a modest and most lovable personality,
and that is the finest tribute I can pay
to him. I do so whole-heartedly.
Mr. LESLIE: At a moment when, as
indeed the hon. Member in front reminded us indirectly in a passage in the
very beautiful speech to which we have
listened with such satisfaction this afternoon, and under circumstances when we
are still listening for the echo of the
rustle of the wings of the Angel of Death
that is the classical phrase I
referring to it is difficult to bring one's
thoughts to the matter immediately
before us.
was
We think of the late Lord
Craigavon partly as a man and partly
as a politician and a Minister. Most of
us perhaps to-day would prefer to dwell
on the more human side. We shall think
perhaps rather of his warm-heartedness
and universal kindness than of his unflinching steadfastness of purpose.
People, even in the Northern Ireland
counties, have disagreed from time to
time with the utterances of Lord Craigavon and with some items of his policy,
but to-day the voice of controversy is
stilled. I think the whole world will
agree that, in the words spoken so long
ago of a Roman of distinction in his day,
magnus civis obiit et formidatus. He
was a great citizen, and, I think, he
inspired awe and respect in the hearts of
his opponents.
I do not think there is anything better
that could have been wished for Lord
Craigavon than that he, should have died,
as he did die, in harness. I am sure that
is what he would have wished, and we
can only express our satisfaction that it
is proposed that his mortal remains
should be laid to rest in the immediate
vicinity of the Parliament for the construction of which he was mainly
responsible. We can add not our hope,
but, I think I may say, our certainty
that his spirit has returned to its
Creator. We shall always think of him
with reverence and with affection.
I was glad to hear the references made
to our late colleague, Colonel Sinclair,
the father of this House. Colonel
Sinclair was, as was said by Senator
Joseph Cunningham, a Senator who did
not often address this House, but when
he did he always spoke on something
which he thoroughly understood, and of
which he had a most intimate knowledge.
The information he gave us was always
not only reliable but conveyed in language
which popularised the subject with which
he was dealing. The late Colonel Sinclair
was one who spent his life as a minister
of the divine attribute of mercy,
exemplified in the alleviation of human
suffering, and the world is poorer for his
loss.
I need scarcely say how cordially I join
in the expression of sympathy with Lady
Craigavon and the other members of her
family, and I suppose we shall in due
course pass to the normal business of the
House.

had the privilege of expressing my
sympathy with Lady Craigavon, and an
appreciation of our late Prime. Minister,

-(Mr. Robb.)
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill without any amendment.
Mr. ROBB: It is proposed that the
House should now adjourn during pleasure. As hon. Senators are aware, a Bill
is expected to come from another place
dealing with the burial of Lord Craigavon's body within the Stormont Estate.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Craigavon (Burial) Bill, to which they desire
the concurrence of this House.
Craigavon (Burial) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: This is quite a short Bill.
It says:
Notwithstanding anything contained
in any enactment or in any regulation,
order or instrument, it shall be lawful
to bury in the Stormont Estate the body
of James, first Viscount Craigavon of
Stormont in the County of Down and,
after her death, that of his widow, and
the Ministry of Finance may, for such
453 Craigavon Burial Bill

26 NOVEMBER 1940
Royal Assent
purpose, set apart such portion of the
said Estate as the said Ministry may
determine.
In this Act the expression "Stormont
Estate" has the same meaning as in the
Stormont Regulation and Government
Property Act (Northern Ireland),
1933.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committeed." (Mr. Robb).
On Question, Motion negatived.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time.”—(Mr. Robb).
Resolved,
"That this Bill do now
pass." (Mr. Robb).
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill without any amendment.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.
Royal Assent.
Mr. ROBB: I have to report to the
House, That His Grace the Governor of
Northern Ireland, in the name and on
behalf of His Majesty the King, has been
pleased to give his Assent to the
following Bill agreed upon by both
Houses:-
Craigavon (Burial).
Resolved, "That this House do now
adjourn until 11th December at Twelve
o'clock noon."-(Mr. Long).
Adjourned accordingly at
five minutes after Four
o'clock until 11th December at Twelve o'clock
noon.
Papers Presented

Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
have to report to the House That His

Adjournment.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That
this House do now adjourn until such
date as Mr. Speaker shall appoint."
Mr. LESLIE: I think one might be
permitted to ask whether there is any
reason to suppose this House will meet
again this year?
Mr. LONG: No, not this year.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

minutes after Twelve o'clock
until such date as Mr
